                                       INTRODUCTION
                                            AND

                                    EXECUTIVE SUMMARY

        This stud} summarizes the deliberations and conclusions of a
        number of consultants who were invited to consider proposals
        for the reform of secondary education in Jamaica. For a
        six-month period in 1989 and a one-month period in 1990. the
        study group addressed the terms of reference prepared by the
        Ministry of Education. i.e. the rationalization of existing
        institutional types. development of new curricula. and the
        feasibility of a unified lower secondary cycle. In 1983. a
        UNESCO report on education in Jamaica had suggested a common
        ~urriculum for the lower cycle of secondary schools.


        The proposals in the study are intended to establish a 

        lowe~-secondary curriculum which allows all students to have a 

        similar educational experience to the end of Grade 9. At the 

        same time. consideration is given to the institutional setting 

        in which this learning is to take place: to begin the process 

        of solving some of the chronic problems which are inherent in 

        the system. 

         The consultant team has found that most of the structural
         elements of a good school system exist in some form in
         Jamaica. Partly as a result of Jamaican ingenuity in the use
         of scarce resources and partly the result of projects from
         foreign agencies. there are institutions and organizations in
         place. They need. however. a unifying philosophy and a
        'systematic organizational approach in order to improve
         opportunity for more students.
        The unifying phi losophy required is that of ·equa I i ty of
        opportunity. While it is espoused now and was the original
        intention of many previous educational reforms. implementation                 J
        has been effectively sUbverted--:-Dy--a-seTectlOI'l"System~whTc'h                  /   '
        t.einforces self-ful~illing. a~d.usu~ltY:Ife~a_t:.~ye·-j.¥3!.(ymen..!:~ ~b~t
        students. In practlce. elltlsm controls toe system. Gooa
        teaching has been discouraged by examinations at every level of
        the primar~ and secondary schools.
        The study begins with an overview in Chapter 1 of the present
        system, the historical forces which still shape it. and the
        major problems which need to be addressed. These problems
        include the inequity in allocation of resources and facilities
        among different types of schools; the consequent unjust
        differ~nces in the quality of education provided; the lack of
        access to second-cycle secondary education for approximately
        SO% of the students. the injurious effects of selection
                                                                              1
                                                                       .,        ',"




examinations - particularly the Common Entrance Examination 
on the students and the curriculum at all levels of the
schools; the patchwork of school-leaving certificates; and the
idiosyncratic differences in the present-syllabi in the various
types of schools.
These features of thp pr(sent system provided a powerful
rationale for the cu_ricular and institutional proposals
developed in this study. The proposals for reform have been
placed in the context of the present economic realities of
Jamaica.
The major recommendation is the establishment of a common
junior-higb curriculum for all students in grades 7-9. This
curriculum will provide a general and broadly-based education
on which either job entry or further education at the
senior-secondary level can be based. It aims at correcting                  ".'-'
some of the problems identified such as chaotic diversity. and
narrowness. The recommended curriculum consists of seven
sub ject areas - AefU..betics,. _Language Arts (including a foreign
language), Mathemat.ics, personal/Family Studies. Science.
Social Studi"es -and Technology Studies. Suggested goals and                : -_.
emphases are suggested for each subject.
The details of the curriculum and teaching methods are best
designed indigenously, but two major suggestions are made. One
is for the adoption of mastery learning strategies which allow
students of different levels of readiness to learn at their
different rates and. as appropriate. with different
instructional methods. The second suggestion is to integrate
subjects wherever this is feasible.

on the student's understanding of the scientific principles
underlying technology and technological processes in everyday
l·if e.
Recommendations for focus and balance in the curriculum are
presented in Chapters ~ ana 4, and in the technical/vocational
supplement. Volume II. A curriculum. however. is not viable
until it is functioning within a school with teachers,
resources and facilities. including a social context which
enhances the motivation and the sense of self-worth of those

              who  work and study therein. The report therefore presents five
              alternative proposals for the structural reform of the school
              system.   These are:

                  o   Improvements in the present system.   This would leave
                      the present features intact but would target resources
                      t~ supervision of the common curriculum

                                             or

                  o   Expansion of high school enrollments through various
                      means such as the shift system and an increase in the
                      ·pupil-teacher ratio. This would address the problem of
                       access.

                                             or

                  o   Improvement of all-age schools.  This would target
                      resources to the least advantaged group. though the
                      features of the system would remain.

                                             or

                  o   Extension of the primary school to grade 8 or grade 9.
                      This would partly address the question of access.

                                             or

                  o   Creation of junior-hi·gh schools and programmes. or
                      departments. in all schools with grades 7-9 students.
                      This strategy directly addresses each of the problems
                      inherent in the system.                           ~

              The advantages and disadvantages of each reform strategy are
              discussed and tentative cost factors for each reform outlined.

              The study team recommends the last of the above alternatives
              because of its comprehensive solution to the system's
              problems.  Although apparently cost~y. a strong emphasis on an
              incremental implementation strategy ensures effective control
              by the Ministry of the rate of adoption of the policy and its
              associated costs.

              Adoption of ~he 'junior-high' strategy sugges~s that the
              following would be the ultima~e pattern of ~he types of
              secondary schools:

                  1.  High Schools, including Technical High Schools - with
                      junior-high and senior-high departments

                  2.  Primary/Junior-High Schools - former all-age schools

           4.  Senior-High Schools - grades 10-11 or 10-13

      More senior-high pupil places will be needed to provide
      increased access to senior cycle secondary education for those
      who graduate from junior-high schools and departments.
      The mix and location of schools will depend on the School
      ~~Eping Project now nearing completion in the Planning Unit of
      the Ministry of Education. Local school board initiatives for
      the implementation of curricular reform and the upgrading of
      schools must take place in the context of the School Mapping
      Project - see Chapter 7.
      The foregoing' strategy has implications for grades 10-11 and
      12-13 programmes. These are briefly discussed in Chapter 5 and
      are even more subject to student demographics than grades 7-9.
      At the same time. recommendations are made for reform of the
      Common Entrance Examination and other examinations which have
      such a deleterious effect on the schools.
      The reforms recommended require a comprehensive programme to
      prepa~e personnel for the new curriculum roles and the new
      structures of the schools. A plan for teacher education and
      staff development is presented in Chapte'r6. The plan
      discusses the pre-conditions for the success of the curriculum.
      e.g. equalization of resources. the resources needed to enable
      all students to benefit from instruction and to improve the
      quality of teaching and learning. The preparation, upgrading
      and updating of teachers and other personnel are considered
      under four approaches: pre-service teacher education.
      workshops. supervision by Territorial Education Officers. and
      school-based teacher development. A tentative costing is
      provided. I~n Chapter 7. an ,enhanced operational role for
      school boards within the context of strong central planning by
      the Minlstry of Education is described. An incremental
      strategy for implementation is presented as a guide to the work
      of school boards and the Ministers and officials of the
      Ministry in the task of introducing and implementing the
      reforms.

                 Chapter 1 - Part A
                 THE PRESENT SYSTEM


                 1.   ESTABLISHMENT OF THE EDUCATION SYf:TEM

                 The basic formal education system in Jamaica was established
                 under British colonial rule as a part of the post emancipation
                 package. No evidence exists of any structured initiative to
                 establish a formal system either during the time of spanish
                 rule (1494-1655) or during the period of slavery under Britain
                 (1655-1834).

                 The first serious attempt by the ~ate to make provision for
                 "popular educition" carne with the Act of the Abolition of
                 Slavery and the resultant "Negro Education Grant".

                      "providing upon liberal and comprehensive
                      principles for the moral and religious
                      education of the Negro population"
                                Gordon. 1968 p 20).

                 The initial attempt to provide elementary schooling for
                 children of the ex-slaves was fraught with problems of
                 inadequate space. unsuitable location of schools. irregular
                 attendance. poor ~uality of teachers. etc.   (See Latrobe
                 Report l ) Under Crown Colony Government. 1865-1944. the main
                 concern was with the improvement of the standard and efficiency
                 of elementary education. The 1889 Lumb Report 2 recommended
                 that greater efficiency of the system could be achieved by (1)
                 the amalgamation of the smaller and less efficient schools. (2)
                 the introduction of compulsory education. and (3) reorganizaton
                 of the curriculum.

                 Concurrent with the establishment of the elementary level of
                 education for children of the ex-slaves. a secondary level was
                 developed. through endowments and by churches. for the benefit
                 of the coloured and the whites who lacked the financial
                 resources for an education in England.


                 lLatrobe Report. see Gordon. Shirley. A Century of West
                 Indian Education. Longman. London. 1963.

                 2Lumb Report 1889. see Gordon. Shirley, A Century of West
                 Indian Education. Longman. London 1963.




                                                                             1A-1 

                                                                             '   ..

These "fee paying" schools offered a typical English grammar
school type curriculum and the intention was not only to                .'
                                                                       ,.
                                                                                 .'
provide a higher level of education for a specific segment
                                                                       ·,~. i
of the population but also to create, reinforce and perpetuate
a middle class stratum in the society.  Secondary education
remained the preserve of only those who could afford it until
1924 when "grant-in-aid" to those schools was irstit~ted.

Up until the 1940s there was no unified control of education
the elementary level and teacher educatiDn were supervised by a
Department of Education while the secondary level was
administered by the Jamaica Schools Commission.  There was no
attempt to create any articulation and mobility between the two
levels. This dual system therefore became clearly identified
with social status.

National awareness of th~ importance of education began to
emerge in 1944 with the granting of universal adult suffrage
and  representative government.

This 'water-shed' period marked the beginning of many
educational reforms which basically gave rise to the current
system of education.

The major changes that were instituted in an attempt to
integrat~ and rationalize elementary and secondary education
were:

       (1)  The establishment of a central Education Advisory
            Authority;

       (2)  Senior schools were established in 1944 to provide free
            education for 12+ children who were unable to find a
            place in the secondary high schools; .
                                                                       ,'~




       (3)  A Common Entrance Examination was instituted in 1958 to
            select students for places in "grant-in-aid" secondary
            high schools;

       (4)  Technical schools were established and technical
            subjects added to the curriculum of ·some grammar (high)
            schools.

These reforms, to a very limited extent, created opportunities
for students from the lower stratum to gain access to secondary
level schooling.

It is only in the last three decades, however, with the gaining
of independence and complete self-government, that any serious
effort has been made to expand the opportunities for secondary
level education for a greater number of individuals.

I   "   ·   t




                 2.   STRUCTURE OF THE SECONDARY LEVEL
                The various initiatives taken by successive governments since 

                1962 to create greater opportunity for secondary education. 

                have given rise to a complex system of schools and an equally 

                diverse system of examinations for selecting and certifying 

                students. 

                  The secondary level is divided into two cycles: a three year
                  cycle of Grades 7, 8, and 9, and a two year cycle of Grades 10
                  and 11. A general type curriculum is offered during the three
                  year cycle (see section 2.1). At the end of Grade 9, 70\ of
                  students continue to grade 10. These students are channelled
                  into specialized areas with an academic and/or technical
                  emphasis, depending on the type of school. or else they leave
                  school. A range of certifying examinations is offered at the
                  end of Grade 11 (see section 3.1). These determine the
                . students' prospects for further studies.or employment.
                There are an additional two years (Grades 12 and 13) for those
                who qualify. Enrollments by school type are shown in table 1.1.
                2.1     TYPES OF SECONDARY LEVEL SCHOOLS
                Six distinct types of schools can be identified at the 

                secondary level which have different entrance requirements and 

                therefore cater to a somewhat different clientele; have 

                different curricular emphases; and offer different routes for 

                certification. Linked to this are varying chances of 

                employability and trainability of Grade 11 "graduates". Unit 

                expenditure also varies dramatically from one type to another. 

                2.1.1    TRADITIONAL HIGH SCHOOLS
                Many of these 57 schools have their or1g1n in the schools 

                established by trusts and bequests during the nineteenth 

                century. They have traditionally offered a "grammar-school" 

                type academic curriculum. Latterly, there has been the 

                introduction of vocational subjects in many of these schools 

                but the emphasis continues to be on the arts and sciences. 12 

                of the 57 are newly created from former new secondary schools. 

                High schools are the most prestigious secondary level
                institutions. They are assigned the "better" students, attract
                the best qualified teachers, and carry the highest social
                currency in terms of future prospects for their graduates.
                Competition for places at the Grade 7 level is therefore very
                high and is determined mainly by performance on the Common
                Entrance Examination (C.E.E.). A few students at the Grade 9
                level.enter through the Grade Nine Achievement Test (GNAT).
                (see section 3.1.1). Students who complete five years of

               schooling sit the Caribbean Examination Council (CXC)
               examinations and the General Certificate of Education (GCE),
               ordinary level.  (see section 3.2.4). These examinations offer
               the best opportunity for (1) pursuing ~n additional two years
               in high school: (2) having access to higher paid jobs in the
               labour market; (3) gaining access to tertiary education.
               particularly university education in Jamaica and overseas.
               Most traditional high schools offer an additional two years
               which prepare students for the GCE Advanced Level Examinations.
               the main requirement for entry to the University of the West
               Indies (U.W.I.). Only about 6% of the Grade 11 students,
               however, continue through to Grade 12.
               2.1.2 COMPREHENSIVE HIGH SCHOOLS

               The six comprehensive high schools were established in the
               1960s with the intention of creating additional spaces for
               students who could benefit from the level of academic education
               offered in the traditional high schools and yet wished a
               programme of both academic and technical/vocational subjects.
               Entry to these schools is either through CEE and the GNAT or
               from designated feeder schools. At Grade 11, students sit the
               CXC, the GCE and a range of examinations offered by overseas
               examining boards for technical subjects.
               Only one comprehensive high school provides accommodation for
               students at Grades 12 and 13 level.
               Students graduating from these schools have good chances to
               move on to tertiary education and for direct employment in the
               lower level technical and vocational positions in the labour
               market.
               2.1.3   TECHNICAL HIGH SCHOOLS
               Eleven technical high schools offer programmes ranging from 4-5
               years. Entrance to these schools i$ determined usually by
               success in the 13+ Common Entrance Examination (CEE) for
               technical schools. A few enter via the GNAT and through tests
               set and administered by individual sChools.
               The curriculum in these schools has a strong
               technical/vocational emphasis and prepares students for a
               continuing education at UWI, the College of Arts, Science &
               Technology, for other technical tertiary institutions or for
               technical/vocational positions in the labour market.

2.1.5   ALL-AGE SCHOOLS

The 493 all-age schools in the present system are the
all-too-numerous survivors of the elementary schools which were
established after emancipation by the churches to provide a
formal system of education for the ex-slaves. These schools
offer a primary (Grades 1-6) as well as a first cycle secondary         . .i
                                                                            ·      t

(Gradss 7-9) programme. However both sections of the school
fall under the jurisdiction of the unit responsible for primary
education in the Ministry of Education (MOE), and in most
instances the programme offered at the Grades 7-9 level is an
extended primary education programme rather than secondary.
About 40\ of all grade 7-9 students are in all-age schools.

Students at the primary level who are unsuccessful in the 11+
CEE continue through to Grades 7-9 and make a second attempt to
gain access to the traditional high or technical high schools
through the GNAT and the 13+ Technical Entrance Examination.

Some students sit the Jamaica School Certificate (JSC)
Examination (see section 3.2.1) administered by the MOE but the
majority leave school at the end of Grade 9 without any form of
certification. Prospects for higher education are therefore
very slim and the majority unsuccessfully seek employment in
the lower paid unskilled openings in the work force.

                 2.1.6    VOCATIONAL/AGRICULTURAL SCHOOLS
                 There are three schools which offer specialized education at
                 the secondary level - one of which do~s so in conjunction with
                 a technical proqramme. Two of these schools offer a second
                 cycle aqricultural course with a stronq practical emphasis in
                 Grades 10. 11 and/or 12 for 15-17 year old students. Two more
                 are due to open durinq 1989-90.
                 Entrance to these schools is on the basis of a test which is
                 set and administered by the individual schools.
                 2.2     PROVISION OF PLACES

                 Provision of school places for the 12 to 18 aqe cohort is
                 mainly throuqh the six types of secondary level public
                 institutions (see section 2.1) and to a lesser extent throuqh
                 reqistered private independent sch~ols.
                 In 1988/89. there were 256.084 students enrolled in 703
                 schools. with 46.979 (96.4%) of these in 636 public
                 institutions and the remaininq 9.105 (3.6%} in 67 private
                 institutions. (See table 1.1)
                 In all cases enrollment exceeded estimated capacity (no fiqures
                 were available for Grades 7-9 only of all-aqe schools).
                 indicatinq over-crowdinq. In spite of this. however.
                 enrollment represented only 60% of the estimated 387.963 of the
                 12-17 aqe cohort. Many schools. particularly the new
                 secondaries. operate on a shift system.
                 The larqest proportion (37.1%) of secondary level students are
                 accommodated in the new secondary schools. However. if one
                 looks at the Grade 7-9 enrollment (160.498), 40.5% of these
                 students i.e .· 65.053, are accommodated in all-aqe schools.
                 This is of particular importance sinc& for the majority ot
                 students in the all-aqe institution. Grade 9 is the terminal
                 point of their formal education.

3.  THE EXAMINATION SYSTEM
                                                                      '1
3.1      SELECTION EXAMINATIONS                                           '. !
                                                                      d
              The UNESCO Report (1983) notes that 
                   _.
              "access to secondary education is universal             :\

              and it appears that there is a place in 

              Grade 7 for all who want oneil. (p.65). 


However, the concern of the study on secondary reform is with
the quality of education in all the Grade Sevens of the
different schools. There are three selection examinations that        ~    .....
are used to determine movement of students into the different
school types, viz.,

(1)  the 11+ Common Examination (CEE) taken at the Grade 5/6 

     level for placement in traditional and comprehensive high 

     schools. 


(2)  the 13+ Common Entrance taken at the Grade 7 level for 

     entrance to the technical high school&: and 


(3)  the Grade Nine Achievement Test (GNAT) taken at the Grade 9      ,      ,
                                                                            -~
     level for additional places in the traditional and
     comprehensive high schools.

3.1.1     THE 11+ COMMON ENTRANCE EXAMINATION

This examination is the first formal examination taken by
students in primary/preparatory schools between the ages of 10
years and a months, and 13 years. The majority of candidates
are therefore drawn from the Grade 6 level but because of the
eligible age range, candidates also include Grades 4 and 5
students.

It is the major device used       fo~   allocation of students into
either
                                                                      ·-1

(1)  traditional high schools or
                                                                      »      ·
                                                                      ~     .:


(2)  comprehensive high schools.

Access to these two types of schools increases a student's
chances of academic success and therefore movement into
tertiary level institutions or into the better paid jobs in the
labour force.  This examination is therefore considered to be
the major determinant of an individual's future life chances               .)
and prospects for social mobility.  See table 1.2.                    .      "

                 Source: Ministry of Education



                 The examination was introduced in 1958 and in the earlier years
                 two categories of awards were made - 'free place' scholarships
                 and 'grant' places. In the latter case the awardees paid a
                 part of the cost of tuition. However, in 1973 with the
                 introduction of the policy oi "free" education. the 'grant'
                 places were abolished and all awardees offered 'free places.'
                 Special government scholarships offering additional benefits
                 are awarded annually to the top performers.
                 Selection is not on the basis of merit but is determined by the
                 number of places available. Approximately 70% of those who are
                 eligiole take the CEE. The percentage of candidates selected
                 steadily increased between 1973 and 1978 from 14.5% to 24.7%.
                 Since then the percentage of students selected has ranged from·
                 a low of 19.2% in 1984 to a high of 21.5% in 1986. Between
                 1986 and 1988 the percentage has remained constant at
                 approximately 21%.  (see sangster 1 and table 1.2)
                 Data indicate that the selection process discriminates against
                 girls in the system. Twice as many girls take the examination
                 but the cut off point for selection is lower for boys than for
                 girls in order to maintain a balanced ratio of boys to virls in
                 the co-educational schools and in the system.



                 ISangster. Alfred, The Jamaica School Education System.
                 Torch. Vol. 29. 1986.


                                                                                        lA-9
                                                                  :-.
The CEE virtually dictates both what is actually taught and the
pedagogical practices in the primary school. in spite of the
fact thac curriculum guides are in place from the Ministry of
Education. Emphasis is on Mathematics and English Language in
preparation for the CEE and there is a strong tendency to
orient all teaching from the lower grades to the content and
the objective multiple choice nature of 'the examin:tion. This
is less so ~n most private preparatory schools where a more
broad based curriculum is pursued in the lower grades and
attention is diverted to the CEE in the final year.
In many primary schools. selection for the examination starts
as early as Grade 1 where the best students are grouped in a
top stream and progress through successive grades in a similar
stream. The students who make it to the highest stream are
those who are expected to have the greatest chances of
success. Those not deemed to be capable of passing the
examination are given less attention and not even be entered
although of eligible age.
This practice is less prevalent in the preparatory schools
because over the years the CEE has func~ioned in favour of the
children of the higher socio-economic status groups. i.e. those
attending these private fee-paying schools. In an attempt to
redress this situation a 70:30 ratio of places of primary to
preparatory students was instituted in the 1960s. This was
however abolished in 1972. Since 1970. the percentage periods
show that this ratio still holds approximately. Preparatory
school awards have declined from 33% to approximately 15%. See
table 1.3.
Another factor operating in the system is that the best
performers get placements in schools of their choice. so that
there is further stratification within the group of traditional
high schools. These select high schools therefore appear to
cater to students from the higher socio-economic groups because
many of the best performers are from the fee-paying preparatory
schools. The concentration of such students in these schools
in turn determines the general ambience of these schools since
parents are able to make a substantial contribution to their
children1s schools.
3.1.2   THE 13+ TECHNICAL COMMON ENTRANCE EXAMINATION
This examination was instituted in 1960 and is administered to
s~udents between the ages of 13 and 15 years.  Candidates are
drawn mainly from the all-age schools. A few candidates are
entered from private preparatory schools and there may be the
occasional case of students being entered from primary
schools. This would, however, be unusual since the age limit
of primary school students is 11+. Some candidates may also
enter from independent high schools.                              . ...

            The GNAT is regarded as the -final chance for gaining entrance
            to one of the prestigious secondary school types. This is
            particularly true of students in the all-age schools who 'fail'
            the 11+ and 13+ CEEs. On the other hand students who move into
            the new secondary schools are not eligible for this examination
            and therefore do not share this opportunity. Evans 1 notes
            the adverse effect that these three examinations have on the
            curriculum and on the teaching-learning process in the all-age
            schools.· The students are exposed to more or less the same
            curriculum from Grade 4 of the primary school since the three
            examinations are more or less based on the same material.

            3.2   SCHOOL-LEAVING CERTIFYING EXAMINATIONS

                    The UNESCO Report notes that
                    "one of the most characteristic features
                    of secondary education in Jamaica is the
                    wide array of examination and certification
                    possibilities it offers".  (p. 103)

            THe Report makes reference to the selection examinations (see
            Section 3.2) and then refers to the fact that

                    "the real array of examinations is to be
                    found at the end of secondary education
                    where a variety of certificate yielding
                    examinations exists to help the pupil
                    go up - into post secondary education 
                    or get out - into the world of work"
                    (p.  103).

            These certificate yielding examinations are:

            1.  The Jamaica School Certificate (J.S.C)

            2.  The Secondary School Certificate (S.S.C)

            3.  The General Certificate of Education (G.C.E.) of Cambridge
                and London (ordinary level).   .
            4.  The Caribbean Secondary Education Certificate (C.S.E.C.)
                sponsored by the Caribbean Examinations Council (C.X.C)

            S.  The Ordinary Level equivalent for technical subjects set by
                a variety of examining boards:
                (a)  the Associated Examinations Board (A.E.B.):
                (b)  the City and Guilds of London Institute (C.G.L.I.);
                (c)  the Royal Society of Arts (R.S.A.), and-the
                (d)  the Union of Lancashire and Cheshire Institutes
                (U.L.C. I).


            lEvans, Hyacinth, "Strengthening Secondary Education" A study
            of 5 parishes, GOJ/UNDP .. July 1988.

                                                                              ~_'




3.2.1    JAMAICA SCHOOL CERTIFICATE (J.S.C.)
This examination is administered by the MOE and provides an
intermediate type assessment designed te measure aChievement in
different subject areas. Certificates are awarded to
candidates gaining passes in five subjects including English
Language, Mathematics, Science, Social Studies and an optional
subject. The examination caters mainly to students in the
all-age and new secondary schools as well as to "private"
candidates outside of the formal system and to a lesser extent
to students from independent high schools and traditional high
schools.
At one time this was a very popular examination providing
individuals with a basic qualification for entry to the
"world-of work" and to further education. In recent years
however, the number of entries has declined dramatically (see
table 1.6). Sangster attributes this to the following:
(1)  Poor administrative performance in the Examination Section 

     of the MOE. 

(2)  Gradual replacement of this examination by the higher level
     SSC which now caters to the new secondary cohort.
(3)  Lack of employer acceptability of the examination and                   ..... ,
     therefore low "social currency" of the certificate.


ISangster. Alfred, The Jamaica School Examination System,                    ~.'
Torch, Vol. 29. 1988                                                               I.

Given the current structure of the secondary system. candidates
from the all-age schools will be the most 'disa~vantaged group
if this examination is discontinued because they have little or
no recourse to any other type of scheol-Ieaving examination.
Candidates from the other school types and private candidates
however. have access to alternatives such as the S.S.C., the
C.X.C., the R.S.A or the G.C.E.; although private candidates
cannot sit the S.S.C.

3.2.2   SECONDARY SCHOOL CERTIFICATE (S.S.C.)

This examination with elements of school based assessment, work
study and a final assessment was introduced in 1976 by the MOE
and was envisaged as a local examination for new secondary
students completing the second cycle of secondary education.

Twenty-six subjects are examined at one level.   In the case of
Mathematics and English Language, however, two levels are
offered - the "Functional" level and the higher "Continuing"
level.   These examinations are based on the Grades 10 & 11
curriculum of the new secondary school and since inception.
have only served candidates from this school type.   Candidates
are graded on a five point scale with 5 indicating the highest
level of performance.

Data related to these examinations are desegregated on the
basis of entry and passes by subject area and are too
cumbersome for presentation in this report.  The figures,
however, indicate that over the years the number of subject
entries has remained more or less ~onstant between 40 and 48
thousand with 10-11% attaining range 4-5.   Holders of 'Secondary
School Certificates in the 4-5 range have access to some post
secondary educational programmes.  The certificate is
recognized for employment in the civil service and has some
validity in the private sector.

3.2.3   GENERAL CERTIFICATE OF EDUCATION

Examinations leading to the award of a G.C.E. Certificate are
offered by Examining Boards of both Cambridge and London.   The
Cambridge examinations were introduced in Jamaica from 1882 and
were therefore most closely assoclated with the traditional
high schools, although in more recent years candidates have
also been supplied from the comprehensive and technical high
schools and from private day and evening institutions.




                                                            lA-IS
                                                                    ..... 

These examinations have always been the most prestigious school
leaving examination. offering holders of certificates at both
the "Ordinary" and "Advanced" levels greatest access to
tertiary education and to better paid jOb opportunities.  In
recent years. demand fQr this examination at the Ordinary level
has declined because of the introduction of the CXC. although
it still is perceived as the preferred route for a few schools
and for some private candidates. 2

3.2.4   CARIBBEAN SECONDARY EDUCATION CERTIFICATE (C.S.E.C.)

This certificate is awarded by the Caribbean Examination            .-....
Council which is a Caribbean based institution established in       - -I
                                                                           I

1972 to certify secondary school leavers throughout the             "-.)
region. The intention of the C.X.C .. in replacing the
British-based system. is that:

(1)  The examination should be suited to the needs of the 

     Caribbean societies and relevant to the goals of 

     participating governments. 

                                                                    .,.    }


(2)  The syllabuses should aim to provide a worthwhile course of
     study for students of a wider range of ability than pass       ,
                                                                        ')
                                                                        .. ~
     the G.C.E. '0' level;                                                 ;




(3)  The pass/fail concept should be abolished. l

'In accordance with (2) above. the examinations are set at two
levels, the General and Basic proficiency levels. The former
is the equivalent of the G.C.E. '0' level while the latter is
 intended to cater to two identifiable groups: 

(a)  candidates who will not pursue further studies in the 

     subject taken at this level. and 


(b)  students who would not 'pass' the subject at the '0' level 

     and therefore would normally be advised not to sit the 

     examination. 


The Basic Proficiency syllabus for each subject is similar to
that· for the General Proficiency level but with reduced scope;
the latter focuses on fundamental principles and concepts.




ISangster. Alfred. the Jamaica School Examination System.
Torch. Vol. 29, 1986.

2See the Caribbean Examination Council. Western Zone Office
Bulletin. September 17, 1981 (p.l).



lA-16
· ·   · *




            The C.X.C. examinations were first offered in 1979 in five
            subject areas but by 1987 syllabi and examination procedures
            were in place for twenty-eight subjects covering a range of
            academic and technical areas.   Candidates for the examination
            are therefore supplied from the traditional. comprehensive and
            technical high schools who favour the General Proficiency level
            and to a lesser extent from the new secondary schools who tend
            to take the Basic level. The C.X.C. ~ertificate gives overall
            grade for each subject on a five point scale from 1 to 5 in
            descending order.  In addition a profile of the student's
            performance in each subject based on certain criteria is
            included.

            A General Proficiency level certificate   at range I & II of the
            examination has gained recognition both   for access to tertiary
            education and to the job market but the   Basic Proficiency level
            certificate seems to have little social   value.

CURRICULUM CONCERNS

At the secondary level, each school type, and in fact, each
individual school, exercises a large degree of freedom in
determining the contents of the school's curriculum. There is
no enforcement of a common curriculum for a~l schools or for a
particular school type by the MOE. At one 'stage a curriculum
was designed for the new secondary schools but is not in
general use now and reprinting of the students' workbooks for
Grades 10 & 11 in Language and Mathematics has been only
recently carried out.
4.1     CURRICULUM OF DIFFERENT SCHOOL TYPES
This needs to be examined. in relation to the two cycles of
secondary education. During the first cycle (Grades 7, 8, and
9) some commonality exists in r~speet of the subjects offered.            ::,1,
During the second cycle (Grades 10 & 11) the curriculum                      .
organization is more differentiated among the various school              ~-   .
types and it is at this level that the ,different orientations
of the types becomes' evident. The external examination which
the school supports exerts a strong influence and becomes the
principal determinant of curriculum content.
4.1.1    CURRICULUM:   GRADES 7-9
A cursory examination of time-tables randomly selected from
each school type indicates that in terms of the subjects
offered and the time, i.e., number of periods allocated to the
various subject areas, two distinct groupings can be identified
- the traditionaL, comprehensive and technical high schools
have a similar curriculum pattern while the new secondary and
all-age schools are more similar to each other than to the
first three types. See table- 1. 7.
In the former case some twenty-three subject areas are
identifiably arranged in three groupings, viz., academic core
subjects, vocational subjects and non-core subject' areas.
During Grades 7-8 the academic core consists of subjects such
as English Languag~, Mathematics~ Social Studies, General
Science. Spanish/French and Religious Education.   The subject
areas of Language and Mathematics get the greatest allocation
of time. At the Grade 9 level there is further differentiation
of the curriculum areas and the separate components of Social
Studies i.e. History and Geography, and General Science, i.e.
Biology, Chemistry and Physics, are offered.

Similar vocational & technical subjects are offered throughout
the first cycle in the traditional and comprehensive high                q
schools with Home Economics being made ayailable for the girls           J
and Industrial Arts for the boys. All students pursue a
programme of Art & Craft.                                                .....
                                                                             (
                                                                         ..      ,
In the technicil h_gh school there is no Grade 7 programme
except in 6 scnools. Grade 8 students are exposed to similar
technical subjects' as those in the traditional and
comprehensive high schools. In Grade 9. however. partial                 :. i
specialization starts and students select one of five technical
options - Home Economics. Commerce. Building Engineering.
Electrical Engineering and Electronics.
The curriculum for Grades 7-9 in these three school types
includes non-core subjects such as Physical Education. and
Music and '~uidance Services.'
In the other two school types. i.e. the new secondary and the
all-age. similar groupings of subjects are offered. In the
case of the 'academic core', however. there is no                        .',1
                                                                               .

differentiation at the Grade 9 level, and Social Studies and
General Science are offered right up to the Grade 9 level. As
before the greatest allocation of time is to English Language            'oj

and Mathematics. There is little opportunity for tech/voc
subjects to be offered in the all-age schools, which have no
equipment, facilities or teachers with rare exception.
In the new secondary school a remedial stream is given
additional periods of English Language and Mathematics at both
the Grade 7 and 8 levels, at the expense of subjects like
Social Studies, General Science and Spanish which either get
reduced time or are omitted altogether.
In many of the all-age schools there is an even greater
emphasis on English Language and Mathematics with as many as
fifteen periods being offered to each of these areas in some
instances. What obtains in these schools is that there is an
extension of a primary type programme and teaching approach in"
an attempt to further prepare students for the two additional
selection examinations taken at the Grades 7-9 levels in these
schools. None or very little tech/voc is offered.
The above description does not take into account the actual
content of the curriculum offered in each school type.                   ':..... j



Collection of data related to this factor would have required
extensive field work. Indications are. however, especially in
the absence of any enforced curriculum guidelines from the
Ministry. that each school largely determines the content and
emphases of its Grade 7-9 curriculum. The textbook rental
scheme may standardize curriculum content, but it has not yet
                                                                         '~-    .:
been introduced into the All Age schools.

lA-2C
4.1.2   CURRICULUM:   GRADES 10 and 11
The organization of the curriculum is more differentiated at
this level and it is here that the orientations of the
different institutions are most evident.
(1) All-age schools - there is no second cycle offered in these
    schools. with 2 exceptions out of the 493 schools.
(2) New secondary schools: The intention during the second
    cycle (Grades 10 and 11) is that students select vocational
    options and pursue programmes that provide them with
    marketable skills. The curriculum therefore has a strong
    technical/vocational emphasis - 40\ of the curriculum time
    is reserved for the study of Agriculture. Art and crafts.
    Business Education. Home Economics or Industrial Education.
(3)  Technical high schools: The Grade 10 and 11 curriculum is
     oriented towards technical/vocational training of a more
     rigorous nature than that offered in the new secondary
     schools. The specialization started in Grade 9 continues
     through 10 and 11 and students are groomed for examinations
     offered by the C.X.C. and overseas examining boards whereas
     in the former case much less content is covered as students
     are prepared to satisfy the demands of a local Ministry
     based examination (S.S.C.). In addition the curriculum
     includes a technical programme of Home Economics. Business
    Education. Electrical Installation. Auto-Mechanics.
    Welding/Fabrication and in some cases Agriculture.
(4) Comprehensive high schools offer a mixed curriculum of
    academic and technical/vocational subjects but with less of
    an academic emphasis than the traditional high schools.
(5)  Traditional high schools offer mainly academic subjects at
     the Grade 10 and 11 level with a more limited array of
     vocational offerings such as Commerce. Home Economics.
     Technical Drawing etc. Although the curriculum appears to
     be organized along comprehensivs principles in these
     schools. in reality there is a greater demand for academic
     subjects since the majority of students choose these
     options. The practical areas are regarded as having lower
     status and are often associated with lower achievers.
(6) Many traditional high sc~ools also offer a programme at
    Grades 12 and 13 where students do a number of subjects in
    preparation for university matriculation at the advanced
    level. These programmes are characterized by very small
    classes.




                                                           1A-21 

                                                                   .. 




The foregoing indicate that each school type offers a programme
with a particular emphasis. As was noted. in the absence of               ......1
enforcement by the MOE. actual content of the curriculum.
particularly during the first cycle, is often determined by the           U
individual schools and even by individual teachers. The
factors which are the real determinants of what is offered by
~ay of curriculum content. in the different school types and in
any particular school. are:
(1)   teacher supply and quality
(2)   quality of students' previous achievements
(3)   availability of adequate facilities and equipment
(4)   the syllabus for the external examination at Grades 11 and          ,.
      13.

                                                                           .,
                                                                             .
                                                                           .~.

·   'Ii   ··




               TEACHER SUPPLY AND QUALITY 


               The concern about teachers at the secondary level is both
               quantitative and qualitative.

               S.l     TEACHER-STUDENT RATIO
               Data related to teacher supply in comparison to student
               enrollment in the different school types are presented in table
               1. 8.

               The figures indicate that it is the all-age schools which are
               at yet another disadvantage with a much higher teacher-student
               ratio (1:40) than any of the other school types which have an
               approximate ratio of 1:20.

               S.2     TEACHER QUALIFICATION

               Traditionally. the majority of teachers in the high schools
               were university graduates with a fewer number coming from the
               teacher colleges. with the reverse being the case in the other
               school types (see table 1.9). This pattern has been changing.
               however. and the percentage of university graduates in the
               traditional high schools has been declining. paralleled by an
               increase in the number of college trained teachers.   In fact
               between 1980/81 and 1987/88 a similar trend has been observed
               in all the school types with an exception of the all-age where
               the situation is reversed. This decline is most marked in the
               traditional high school where there has been a 10% decline in
               the number of graduate teachers.  This is not unexpected since
               increasingly university graduates in the system regard teaching
               as a 'stepping-stone' to the more lucrative openings in the
               private sector and therefore use teaching as a means of upward
               social mobility. This is reinforced by the fact that 13.~% of
               the university graduates teaching in the high schools do not
               have any professional training because they do not intend to
               stay in the system.  There is therefore a high annual attrition
               rate with the result that many of the teachers are young and
               inexperienced.

               Over this same period (1981-88) the percentage of teachers'
               college graduates in the system has increased in all the school
               types. The number in the traditional. comprehensive and,
               technical schools has increased considerably so that there are
               now far more teachers' college graduates than university
               graduates in these schools.   The number of teachers' college
               graduates had always been considerably higher in the new
               secondary and all-age schools but in both these instances there
               has been further increases between '80/81 and '87/88.

lA-24
·.


     In the all-age schools, however, the majority of teachers
     deployed to the secondary department are trained for the
     primary level so that they lack the content and methodology
     appropriate for the secondary level. _This is particularly true
     of subject areas like Mathematics and Science.
     The number of pre-trained teachers in the system has declined
     in all the s~hool types. The d~cliLe has been most marked in
     the all-age schools. This is due partly to the In-Service
     Teacher Education Thrust (ISTET) which was mounted to qualify
     all untrained teachers in the system and partly to government
     policy introduced in the early '80s, ~o stop hiring pre-trained
     teachers.
     These data further reinforce the distinction between the
     different school types. The traditional high schools which are
     regarded as the most prestigious i~ the system, still attract
     the highest pe~centage of university graduates and the lowest
     percentage of trained teachers. This also obtains, but to a
     lesser extent, for the comprehensive and technical high schools
     while the reverse is true of the new secondary and all-age
     schools at the other end of the continuum.
     5.3    CLASSROOM ORGANIZATION FOR TEACHING
     With respect to classroom organization the main difference is
     in the all-age schools where,' even at the secondary level, the
     majority of schools are organized for grade level teaching with
     a single teacher managing all the areas of the curriculum.
     As was noted earlier, the emphasis is· on rote learning by drill
     and practice with little attempt at individualization although
     there is a wide ability range of students in a given class.
     Teachrng is therefore characterized by 'chalk and talk' by the
     teacher, and seat-work activities -by the students.
     The other school types are organized for subject teaching and
     teachers are 'subject specialists.' The teaching style.
     however, is generally didactic with teachers dispensing 

     information and students taking notes. 

     6.    STUDENT CHARACTERISTICS
     6.1   AB~LITY   LEVELS
     The quality of the student entering the secondary cycle of
     education is to a large extent determined by the quality of the
     educational programme at the primary level. The primary
     programme has not succeeded sufficiently in providing graduates
     with the necessary minimal education attainment to allow them



                                                                lA-25
to benefit from secondary level education. As a result there
is an extensive need for remediation at the secondary level.
In the past this was most marked in the new secondary and
all-age schools but recently this phenomenon is evident in all
the school types.
Unsuccessful candidates in the 11+ CEE enter ne~ secondary        ,-  ,   "
                                                                               .

schools from designated feeder schools. Tt.e new secondary
school accepts all 12 year old students ~egardless of their       .-'
level of attainment in the primary school. As a consequence,      'i
                                                                  ·           ,
                                                                  :.J
30 to 50\ of the Grade 7 students require remediation to
effectively attain a Grade 7 level of literacy and numeracy.
A similar situation exists in the all-age schools where
students who are unsuccessful in the 11+ CEE move into the
secondary department displaying deficiencies in Language and
Mathematics skills. In t~ese schools the problem is further
exacerbated by the higher teacher-student ratio, the
unavailability of instructional materials and the lack of staff
capable of managing the situation. The problem, for the most
part. is therefore not addressed by the teachers.
In spite of the selection process which allocates the 'best'
students to the traditional. comprehensive and technical high     ·       .j
                                                                              ~.
schools. a similar phenomenon. albeit to a lesser extent. is
evident. ·Remediation. to differing degrees. is therefore a
necessity in all school types. Coping strategies are organized
on an 'ad hoc' basis from scbool to school. Secondary teachers
do not feel that they have either the facilities nor training
to effectively deal with the situation.
Streaming and grouping are the most common strategies applied.
They are organizep by each school according to its own criteria
and perceived needs. Many schools separate students into a        ·
remedial stream where additional periods are offered in           · -,    ,




Language (particularly Reading) with the intention of
integrating them into the main-st~eam by the end of Grade 7.
6.2   OTHER STUDENT QUALITIES
Students in the new secondary and all-age schools are
predominately from the lower socio-economic strata of the
society. Many of them have low self-esteem because of the
experience of 'faiiure' in the system. Disciplinary problems.     · -:

absenteeism and lateness are prevalent and there is a critical
need for guidance and counselling both in terms of personal
development as well as post-school opportunities for further
education and employment.




1A-26 

The attrition rate of students in the all-age schools is very
marked as students progress from Grade 7 through 9 (see table
1.10). Data for 1987/88 indicate that between Grade 7 and 8,
and 8 and 9, there is an 11% and 17% attrition level
respectively.   Some of those who leave transfer to the new
secondary school. For others it means 'dropping-out' after
failure at their second attempt to gain entry to the more
presti.gious technical high schools through the 13+ CEE. There
is no incentive for students to complete Grade 9 since there is
no certification and little is being done in terms of
vocational training in these schools.   Students most likely
seek admission to new secondary schools where opportunity for
vocational training exists. This hypothesis is confirmed by
the 10% increase in number between Grade 8 and 9 in these
schools.

 In the traditional high schools there is a moderate increase in
'number between Grade 7 and 9. At the Grade 9 level this is
 most likely due to the intake of students who were successful
 at the GNAT.

7.    FACILITY AND RESOURCES 

                                                                   ,....,
                                                                   · 1
7.1    PHYSICAL FACILITIES                                         .~-)

The new secondary schools were established in the 1960s. They
now need major renovation but are in relatively qood condition
compared to the ~ll-aqe schools, many of which date back to the
mid and lat~ 1800s. The buildinqs in which all-aqe schools are
housed are therefore usually old and dilapidated with open
                                                                       · "
classrooms separated only by chalkboards. Rooms for aqe qroups
or specialized subjects are therefore lackinq.  In many
instances there is no special room to accommodate staff and the
principal's office tends to be a qeneral area where a variety
of tasks and functions are carried out.

All the other school types have specialized rooms for different
curriculum areas, staff rooms and an area to house the
administrative staff.

Althouqh all schools suffer from shortaqe of furniture for
students and teachers, this is the most marked in the all-aqe
schools where in many instances, students are accommodated on
seatinq suitable for the primary level, i~e. low benches and
desks for seatinq qroups of three.  In other cases each student
occupies a sinqle desk and chair which are usually in short
supply.

Of all the school types, the traditional hiqh schools have the
qreatest capacity to maintain and replace furniture and
equipment with funds solicited throuqh the P.T.A. or throuqh
special fund-raisinq events, as well as throuqh fees charqed.

7.2   INSTRUCTIONAL RESOURCES

The UNESCO Report of 1983 notes that the 'lack of instructional
materials and aids is aRother serious problem havinq neqative
effects on the quality of what is belnq learnt' (p. 9).   In the   ·    ,
past the traditional, comprehensive and technical hiqh schools     i.. ;
were the least affected because of the capability of many
parents to pay for textbooks or to obtain books throuqh rental     (    "
schemes operated by many schools.

Over the past three ye~rs the G.O.J./U.K. Secondary School
Textbook Project has provided ~asic textbooks in core subjects
on a rental basis to Grade 7-11 students in 143 public
secondary level schools. Unfortunately this scheme did not
                                                                   ,   -,
include the all-aqe schools which have the qreatest need for             ,
such assistance.




1A-28 

Additionaliy. there are no laboratories or equipment in the
all-age schools to facilitate the teaching of science. When
the new secondary schools were established laboratory
facilities and equipment were provide~ but as the UNESCO Report
observes 'some of the equipment was too elaborate and unsuited
for the curriculum and too complex for the average science
teacher to handle' (p. 9).

Due to the lack of funds for maintenance many of these
laboratories and pieces of equipment are not non-functional.
In most high schools, science laboratories are inadequate in
size and the installations (gas. electricity, water. etc.) are
below standard and at times unsafe (UNESCO Report p. 90).

A similar situation is observed in the case of facilities and
equipment for practical vocational subjects. The high and
comprehensive schools could benefitted from improvement.   The
technical high schools have benefited considerably from the
USAID Basic Skills Training Project. New secondary schools are
generally in a better position and. as is always the case,
facilities are non-existent in most all-age schools.

In all cases consumable materials for both the teaching of
science and practical subjects are insufficient.

8.    FINANCING OF SECONDARY EDUCATION

8.1    BUDGETARY ALLOCATION FOR EDUCATION

The figures presented in table 1.11 represent both recurrent
and capital expenditure and indicate that although there has
been a steady increase each year (1983-89) in the actual amount
spent on education, these increases do not represent a bigger
portion of the national budget because since 1985 the amounts
represent a smaller percentage (%) of total government
expenditure than previously, and they represent approximately
the same percentage of the Gross Domestic Product.

8.2   DISTRIBUTION OF RECURRENT EXPENDITURES ON EDUCATION

The figures in table 1.12 indicate that two-thirds of the
budget for education was allocated for both the primary and
secondary levels in 1987/88.  In 1988/89, however, there has
been a 1% decline in spending at the secondary level with-a
corresponding 3% increase at the primary level. This is in
keeping with government policy to improve conditions at the
primary level.

lA-30
8.3  DISTRIBUTION OF RECURRENT EXPENDITURES AT THE SECONDARY
     LEVEL
The way funds are distributed at the secondary level among the
different school types for recurrent expenses is totally in
keeping wieh the perception held of these schools.  In fact it
is this very practice which has given rise to and fostered and
reinforced the inequalities in the system.

The figures in table 1.13 show that the all-age school receives
the lowest per capita grant and that 89% of the amount goes to
teachers' salary.

No expenditure is recorded under 'other personnel' because
these schools do not, as a rule employ either support ancillary
or secretarial staff.  In most instances these duties have to
be carried out by both staff and students. This practice
erodes the amount of time devoted to teaching and further
reduces the quality of learning.

The estimated amount spent on books and materials is J$8.00 per
student. The effect of this on the quality of the learning
environment is self-evident.

       Chapter 1- Part B
       THE CONTEXT OF REFORM

       A.
     Educational reform does not take place in a vacuum, nor is it
     affected only by conditions within the educational system.
     Prevailing economic and demographic conditions and trends are
     important determinants of the success or failure of innovations
     in educational policy and practice. Changes in the
     government's financial circumstances are also important
     influences on the array of constraints and opportunities that
     face prospective reformers. In this section, some of the most
   . salient features of the context of secondary school reform in
     Jamaica are examined, including rates of growth in the
     population and in the national economy, and trends in the
     structure of employment. Some data is also presented on recent
     changes in aggregate budgetary allocations for education and on
     the distribution of educational revenues within the system, as
     well as some estimates of the revenues that may become
     available for education in the coming decade.
       I.   RATES OF GROWTH IN POPULATION AND GOP
       Table 1-15 provides data on the growth of Jamaica's population
       and economy between 1980 and 1988. Rates of population growth
       have remained relatively low, and have tended to decline in
       recent years, mainly because of large flows of international
       out-migration. The total population of the island nevertheless
       increased by approximately 10 percent in this period.
       In the same period Gross Domestic Product (GOP) increased by an
       aggregate 8.2 percent, which implies a real decline in per
       capita incomes through the decade. Since the downturn in 1984,
       however, the economy has recovered, and per capita incomes have
       increased significantly. Despite the damage caused by
       Hurricane Gilbert, growth is projected to continue in 1989.
       The rate of inflation has fluctuated markedly in the past
       decade, de~lining sharply between 1980 and 1981 and rising
       equally sharply between 1982 and 1984. Since 1984 the
       inflation rate has declined 'once again, to slightly more than 8
       percent in 1987 and 1988, but it is projected to rise to
       approximately 12 percent in 1989.

           declining fertility and --for the schools--declining enrollments.
        2. The unemployment rate is·tar higher among young people under the age of 25 than among older
           people, and higher among women than among men.




213-2
·. 


       II.    TRENDS IN THE STRUCTURE OF EMPLOYMENT AND OUTPUT
       In Table 1-16 data is provided on the changing structure of
       employment in Jamaica between 1980 and 1988. The number of
       people employed has increased steadily since 1982, rising by an
       aggregate 22.7 percent. The rate of unemployment has declined
       correspondingly, though it still remains high at almost 19
       percent of the labor force.                       .
       Within sectors. the share of the labor force employed in
       agriculture has declined steadily in the 1980's, falling below
       30 percent in 1988. while the share employed in services has
       remained fairly steady at between 47 and 48 percent. The
       percentages employed in manufacturing and construction have
       meanwhile increased significantly, rising from 10.6 to 15.0
       percent and from 3.5 to 5.6 percent respectively. The
       percentage of the labor force that is self-employed has also
       declined since 1980. and more particularly since the economic
       recovery began in 1985.
       The share of GDP produced in agriculture has tended to decline
       slowly through the decade. to less than 8 percent in 1988. The
       share produced in services has meanwhile tended to rise. to
       approximately 70 percent at the end of the period.
       III.    IMPLICATIONS FOR EDUCATIONAL POLICY
   It makes no sense to prepare people for jobs that do not exist.
   or for jobs in declining industries. and we are therefore
   skeptical about the wisdom of attempts to make the curriculum
   in Grades 1-9 more "relevant" by incorporating specifically
   vocational content. This is particularly so with respect to
   training in agriculture. and with efforts to meet the training
   needs of the tourism industry.
   To refer to Jamaica as an "agricultural country" is
   increasingly anachronistic. either in terms 'of employment or of
   aggregate output, as the data in Table 1:16 show. To propose
   that the curriculum in Grades 1-9 adopt an increased
   agricultural orientation is to defi both the course of the
   economy and the preferences of households, who send their
   children to school not to make them better farmers but to equip
   them to find work outside of agriculture. Improvements in the
   quality of instruction in basic subjects are likely to be-far
   more useful. and far less costly. in this regard than
   specialized training in agriculture. which is in any case more
   effectively provided on the farm than in the classroom.

·. 


   Tourism is an increasingly important industry in Jamaica. both
   in terms of its contribution to GDP and ift terms of the numbers
   of people that it employs. The most valuable preparation for
   workers in this sector will likely prove to be solid mastery of
   basic literacy and numeracy skills. al~ng with facility in the
   use of standard English. More specialized skills are best
   acquired out of school. in specialized training institutions.
   or on the job. This is equally true of other ser~'ice
   industries. including finance. commerce. and }overnment. In
   our view it would be a mistake for the Ministry of Education to
   orient the formal curriculum to the provision of specialized
   training for tourism or other service industries. despite their
   importance in the Jamaican economy. On the one hand. the
   provision of specialized training of this kind requires the
   diversion of resources from the basic curriculum. where they
   are desperately needed. On the other hand. it is almost
   certain that whatever needs for specialized training exist in
   the tourism industry or in other service industries~ they will
   be met by non-formal training programs. by specialized private
   educational institutions. or by employers themselves. There is
   no good reason for the formal education system to get directly
   involved in these activities.
   IV.   THE FISCAL CONTEXT OF EDUCATIONAL REFORM
   There are significant obstacles to any comprehensive reform of
   secondary education in Jamaica. Some of these are political:
   constituencies who benefit from the present system. or who have
   accommodated their behavior to it. will not welcome changes.
   For example. a common curriculum for Grades 7-9 goes against
   the historically elitist and differentiated grain of secondary
   schooling in Jamaica. and the postponement of selection from
   Grade 6 to Grade 9. would undermine what is at present the
   central organizing principle of the entire educational system.
   Other obstacles are financial. The most significant of these
   is the obligation imposed by Jamaica's public debt. The
   percentage of public revenue committed to debt service has
   remained steady at approximately 40 percent of total public
   expenditure. and 40 percent of total export earnings. for the
   last several years. More recently. following Hurricane Gilbert
   and the last election campaign. the debt service burden has
   increased dramatically. from 39 to 42 percent of government
   expenditure between 1987 and 1988. and further in 1989. As a
   result. barely more than half of the revenue collected by the
   government is available for public investment and spending on
   current services.




                                                             1B-5 

As the data in Table 1:17 show. the share of total p~blic
expenditure allocated to education has declined steadily 6ver       ;',
the past decade. to little more than 11 percent in the most         ·
                                                                    ·
                                                                    ~}
                                                                         J
                                                                         )

recent period. The gravity of this decline is increased by the
fact that the percentage of the education budget, commi tted to
teacher's salaries has steadily increased over this period.         (='1
                                                                    , .j
though the real purchasing power of their salaries has ~lso         ··. .1

fallen significantly. This has meant that the funds aVdilable
to pay for other educational resources -- books, materials, ,
maintenance of facilities -- have practically vanished in many
schools. Despite these declines in educational revenues,
however, the schools continue to receive nearly 17 percent of
the government1s recurrent budget, because of the large share
of total expenditure committed to debt service. Under these
circumstances, major increases in recurrent funding for
education seem unlikely. Such increases would only become
possible with a significant reduction in Jamaica's debt burden.
with a large fall in world interest rates, with a large
increase in taxes, or with large cuts in other public services,
most of which choices can be rated as at best improbable.           ·   ,
                                                                    ··-j,




 Large increases in the revenues available for the exparision and
 improvement of secondary education are made even less likely by
 the Government's stated commitment to give priority to primary
 education in the allocation of revenues available for public
 education. As Table 1:18 shows, the government has
 significantly increased the share of the education budget
'granted to primary schools in the current budget year. with
 corresponding decreases in the shares provided for secondary
 and tertiary education. The share allocated to central
 administration has also increased, and should be watched
 carefully as further increases in administrative costs can be
 one result of administrative reform1
The commitment of the government to the improvement of primary
education is to be welcomed, given the current state of
Jamaica's primary schools, but it makes a large infusion of new
re.ources into the secondary school system even less likely
than it would otherwise be. As a result, the reforms that are
proposed for secondary education will probably have to be
achievable at low or no cost, or else they wi'll have to be
funded by savings achieved elsewhere within the government.
Alternative Projections
Two projections of the public revenue available to the Ministry
of Education during the coming decade based on contrasting sets
of assumptions were prepared for this study. The first set of
projections was based on a series of uniformly'optimistic
assumptions about the Jamaican economy in the coming decade.
