   It was assumed, for example. that Gross Domestic Product will
   increase by 3 percent per year, in contrast to net growth of
   less than 1 percent per year in the past decade. It was
   further assumed that declining rates of interest and the
   rescheduling of a part of Jamaica's d~bt will allow an increase
   to 70 per.cent in the share of total public expenditure
   allocated to the recurrent budget, and that a renewed public
   commitment to the schools will produce an increase in
   educational funding from approximately 16 percent to 20 percent
   of total recurrent expenditure. If these and the other
   assumptions on which the projection is based prove valid, the
   revenues annually available for secondary education will nearly
   double between now and the turn of the century. which would
   provide sufficient revenues for an ambitious program of
   reform. To the extent that any or all of the assumptions are
   too optimistic. however (e.g. if the economy grows by less than
   3 percent per year), the revenues available will be reduced.
   The projections presented in Table 1:19-are based on a more
   realistic set of assumptions. It is assumed that annual
   economic growth will average 1 percent in the coming decade,
   which is slightly higher than the rate achieved in the past ten
   years. Additional assumptions are that 65 percent of total
   public spending will continue to be assigned to recurrent
   expenditure (i.e. no increase in the debt service burden), that
   the Ministry of Education will continue to receive 16.5 percent
   of the total recurrent budge~. and that the share of revenues·
   available for secondary education will fall to 30 percent of
   all Ministry of Education revenues as the government's
   commitment to primary school improvement takes effect. Under
   these assumptions the revenues annually available for secondary
   education will increase by approximately 15 percent over the
   course of the next decade. to 291 million Jamaican dollars per
   year.
   It should be noted that even these assumptions may prove unduly
   optimistic. If annual economic growth falls below 1 percent.
   for example, or if the share of government revenues required
   for debt service continues to increase, which now appears more
   likely than not, with the recent decline in the exchange rate
   against the U.S. dollar, then the modest rise in revenues
   projected in Table 1:19 could quickly be wiped out. It should
   also be noted. however, that the government is not the only
   source of revenues for financing education. Even if public
   revenues remain stagnant or decline during the coming decade it
   may be possible to generate the resources needed for reform by
   other means. These might include community and/or household
   contributions to the construction or maintenance of schools;
   increased fees in some parts of the system, including senior
   high schools and tertiary institutions; and savings produced by
   better use of current revenues.

       Some possible sources of savings are identified in other
       sections of this report. They include abolishing or bringing
       about major reductions in publicly subsidized boarding
       facilities in secondary schools; reducing the present
       generosity of teachers' leave policies; introducing policies
       that would allow the redeployment of teachers to the schools
       where they are most needed; reducing the cost of schooling in
       Grades 7-9 by de-emphasizing vocational or pre-vocational
       subjects and introducing Technology Studies; and expanding
       enrollments in high and new secondary schools by increasing
       class sizes and introducing the shift system to those schools
       that do not now use it. The revenues that could be saved by
       these means are in some cases sUbstantial. Eliminating public
       subsidies for secondary school boarding would save nearly 10
       million dollars per year. for example. while reducing teachers'
       leave entitlements could save as much as 20 million dollars per
       year.

  Chapter 2
  A RATIONALE FOR CHANGE

  The preceding chapter described the major featu~es of the
  secondary level of education and the proble.ns which need to be
  addressed in any reform as well as the financial constraints
  which will shape efforts at reform. The problems and the
  related issues include the curriculum, access to secondary
  education, inequity, the effects of the Commpn Entrance
  Examination, and the quality of primary education.
  THE CURRICULUM
  The curriculum of Grades 7-9 is characterized by diversity,
  inequality and deficiencies in some areas. The diversity of
  subjects offered in the high and new secondary schools is
  paralleled in the all-age schools by the variation in the
  emphasis accorded the core and the practical subjects. The
  lack of curricular supervision and the high"degree of local
  discretion perpetuate this diversity. The absence of an agreed
  upon curriculum content for each type of school further
  diversifies student learning.
  The knowledge and skills which students now learn in the three
  types of schools - high, new secondary and all-age - also
  differ in status and value. Thus, students who leave these
  schools with their various certificates, are prepared to pursue
  different paths and to take different and separate places in
  society. An educational system in a democratic society cannot
  continue this official policy of sorting individuals.
  The deficiencies of the curriculum are manifested in students'
  achievement levels in critical subjects such as maths, science
  and language. Furthermore, the special needs of the adolescent
  are poorly addressed in the majority of schools but especially
  in the all-age schools. A balanced curriculum needs to include
  guidance and counselling as well as other aspects of personal
  development for all students.
  There is a need, therefore, for a common curriculum which
  avoids the shortcomings of local discretion, variation in
  content and neglect of students' developmental needs. Such a
  common curriculum, incorporating the knowledge, skills and
  attitudes deemed necessary for all young Jamaicans, is best at
  the grades 1-9 level. It should, in addition, consider the
  special developmental and personal needs of Jamaican
  adolescents in grades 7-9, addressing the problems of
  underachievement, lack of identity and self-knowledge, and low

                                                             2-1 

self esteem. A common grade 7-9 curriculum would be A
continuation of the grade 1-6 curriculum and would provide- a
foundation for a more diversified curriculum at Grades 10
and 11.
                                                                   ','" '1

ACCESS
There are not enough secondary school places for the ave
cohorts of grades 7-9. Access to the valued types of secondary
level education - aspired to by all in society - is extremely
limited. Only 20% of the age cohort at Grade 6 are able to
obtain entry to the high schools. Furthermore. middle class
children continue to enjoy greater chances of gaining entry
than children from poorer classes. Today. only 15% of the
students in the high schools are children of the unskilled.
semi-skilled and unemployed (Miller. forthcoming) despite the
fact that these groups make up about 50% of the population.
Physical expansion, therefore. does not always result in
sUbstantial changes in the relative pattern of opportunities.
Any effort to expand and equalize opportunities must be
accompanied by measures to equalize the possibility of ,
profiting from these opportunities; i.e .· curriculum reform and
improved teachings and more equitable r~source allocation.
OTHER INEQUALITIES
In addition to inequities in the curriculum and in access,
there are gross inequities in resource allocation and staffing
which result in major variation in the quality of instruction
and student learning. These inequalities in resource
allocation widen the differences that exist in the curriculum
and in the composition of the student body in each type of
school. Such discrimination must be reduced or ~liminated.
THE EFFECTS OF THE COMMON ENTRANCE EXAMINATION
In a highly competitive educational system where the number of
pl~ces in the prestige schools is small. some selection device
is necessary. The Common Entrance Examination serves this
purpose but has several harmful consequences. Its effect on
the primary school curriculum is well known, as teachers
convert the curriculum into a drill as a preparation for the
exam. Thus. students get an inferior. unbalanced and limited
education by virtue of this practice. Secondly. those who go
on to a secondary level education - whether in the high schools
or new secondary or grades 7-9 of the all-age schools - are
poorly prepared. lacking basic learning and problem-solving
skills. Finally. the overwhelming majority of the age cohort 
i.e. those who are not selected to sit the exam' and those who
are not selected for the few high school places - experience a
devastating sense of failure which damages their feelings of
self-worth and competence.

2-2
·. 


  Because of the injurious effects of the CEE, and until it is
  discontinued, efforts must be made in the short term. to
  improve curriculum and instruction in the primary school.  The
  replacement of the CEE by an aChievement examination. either
  gradually through expansion of the present CEE test pattern. or
  by immediate substitution of the National Assessment Programme.
  could achieve this goal.

  In summary. a reform effcrt must include the following:

       o   expansion of the number of places available at the
           Grades 7. 8 and 9 level. of high school level.

       o   elimination of the selection devices that serve to
           exclude certain social groups from the most valued
           educational programmes.

       o   measures that will equalize the possibility of all
           children to profit from a secondary education.

  At the same time. the problem of the multiplicity of school
  leaving examinations and their effect on the actual curriculum
  must be addressed through ri~orous supervision. enlightened
  school policies and a reduction in the number of examinations
  that students take.

  The problems and issues discussed in this chapter. all have
  harmful consequences for the Jamaican society and for
  democracy. The three main types of secondary schools - with
  their unequal status and unequal access to the knowledge that
  is most valuable for upward mobility - are aligned with social
  class. Middle class children go to the high schools. poorer
  children to the all-age and new secondary.   This lack of equity
  and th~ favoured status of some groups do not go undetected.
  The resentment and hostility further widen the divisions in the
  society. militating against one of- the very goals of
  education-achieving a good and worthwhile life. Furthermore.        a
  democracy cannot continue to have one group or one class of its
  citizens very poorly served by the education system.

  Nor can Jamaica continue to have the scarce resources of the
  educational system inefficiently ~sed.  The teachers in the
  schools represent a significant hUman resource for the country
  as a whole.  A major portion of the educational budget goes
  towards teachers' salaries.  And yet. the inadequacy or' absence
  of necessary instructional materials and other resources,
  including in-service programmes. limits the teachers'
  effectiveness and compromises the value of their contribution
  to students' learning.  The value of their training. expertise
  and dedication is thus partly negated.




                                                                2-3
The extraordinarily large amounts spent on examinations in the
sys~em, when compared with inputs such as curriculum              "1
development, supervision and textbooks, is another example of     .-1

inefficient expenditure.
Finally, the high drop-out rate in Grades 7-9 reduces the
productivity of the school leavers and treir contribution to
their individual and national welfarJ, and increases the
likelihood of their dependence on the state. There is an
urgent need to increase the quality of instruction and hence
the attractiveness and value of schooling, to increase
students' ability to remain in school, and to increase the
number of places at the secondary level.
The inequalities described in the foregoing section have been
features of education systems in both developed and developing
nations. In the latter case, the colonial heritage of many of
these nations, dictated pa~terns of schooling and curricula
which therefore reinforced those of the metropolitan powers.
Maddock (1983) therefore observes that traditionally most
systems of education have displayed some form of 'bifurcation'
or structural differentiation. The result has been a
separatist system of different types of schools catering to
different segments of the society.
Concommitant with such a system is a process of selection and
allocation and as Maddock (op. cit) notes, systems only differ
in the stage at which the differentiation is carried out. More
importantly, Maddock posits that since the allocation is to a
number of schools, the selection process must be carried out by
a bureaucratic structure which is outside of and transcends the
school.
Many writers (Maddock 1983; Lewin 1985; Hudsori 1985; Whittaker
1983) point to the fact that over the past two to three
decades. many countries have introduced reforms intended to
shift from a system of structural differentiation to one based
on the 'comprehensive principle.I  In the former case,
differentiation of pupils takes place on the transition from
primary to secondary education; i.e., approx~mately after six
years of schooling; in the latter case, dif~erentiation tends
to be delayed to the end of a 'junior high school; i.e., after
                                                  I

nine years of schooling.
Maddock (op. cit.) observes that this shift towards the
'comprehensive principle' removes the corresponding process of
differentiation and selection from an external bureaucratic
structure leaving the structuring and allocation of pupils in
the 'local' administrative regime of the school itself.



2-4
The most important aspect ~f such a shift is a change to an
organization where the crucial structures are the classes
within the school rather than different groups of pupils in
different schools.

These reforms. Lewin (1985) posits. have placed increased
emphasis on the non-economic benefits of equity. national unity
and social development associated with greater access to
education. Similar reforms in the education system in Jamaica
are of paramount importance if large numbe~s of the 12-16 age
cohort are to be beneficiaries of an education which will equip
them to contribute meaningfully to both their personal as well
as national development.

        Education. V. 19(3) 1983

        curriculum reform in developing countries. Comparative

      .   ,
Chapte~   3
A COMMON CURRICULUM


The fi~st and most fundamental p~oblem that must be add~essed
in any reform in education 'is the cu~riculum i.e. what is
taught to students. and how it is taught. The curriculum is
the formal plan for learning that the schools must try to
implement. and it is also the processes of teaching and
learning that students and teachers undertake.

The education of the individual is a result of both the
explicit goals of the schools - the official curriculum that is
required by the government: and the implicit or attitudinal
results of the policies and practices of the school that affect
students: both of which result in the behavioral effects on the
learner of her or his experience in school. This chapter
brings together these two aspects of curriculum in the Jamaican
context.

3.0   A COMMON CURRICULUM

3.l DEFINITION - A common cu~riculum is a plan of learning for
all children in terms of content goals and learning
experiences: but it must allow for students of different levels
of readiness to learn differently and at different rates.    It
is a plan that includes suggestions for adjusting a common
content and common learning experiences to the varying
abilities of students.   Its organization allows for moving from
relatively simple learning experiences to more complex ones.
and for learners to do so at different paces. The organization
of the curriculum guide establishes a sequence of cumulative
learning. or continuity. as learning units move from the simple
to the more complex.   This ensures a progressively more
demanding performance as more complex materials are dealt with.
and there is more exacting analysis, a greater depth and
breadth of ideas to unde~stand. to'relate to one another, and
to apply.  There is also a greater sophistication and subtlety
of attitudes and sensitivities. While all children are exposed
to essentially the same content, the cumulative progress of
learning the content by some will be more rapid and
sophisticated than by others.   In effect. a common curriculum
provides all children with the same basic subject matter, but
it allows for children with different levels of ~eadiness and
ability to proceed at rates of learning.




                                                           3-l
Inherent in this approach is the notion of 'mastery learning'
where a level of performance representing mastery of a given            .' -.
                                                                    c            ,
skill or concept is established (Bloom 1976). Where this
approach to teaching and learning is app~ied, mastery learning
theory holds that instructional time and resources should be
used to bring all students up to an acceptable level of
achievement, Slavin (1987). This contrasts with the view that
differences in student aptitude will necessarily lead to
differences in stud~nt achievement. Thus rather than holding
instructional time constant and allowing achievement to vary as
in traditional instruction, achievement level is held constant
and time allowed to vary.
If this principle is accepted then the major problem that
arises is that some students take longer than others to achieve
a particular objective. Slavin (op. cit) contends that to
overcome this obstacle two options are possible. Firstly
corrective instruction can be given outside of class time to
those who need additional assistance. corrective instruction
given during class time would mean that those who achieve               ..   ,
mastery early would spend considerable time waiting for the
slower learners to 'catch up'. This option is, however,
expensive and difficult to arrange. On the other hand.
enrichment or lateral extension activities can be given to
early masters while corrective instruction is given to those
who need it.
Mastery learning should not be interpreted as justification for
holding students back from progressing through the common
school grades with their peers. Failure to master the content
ass igned to a grade level, i. e. "Grade I" reading or "Grade 4"
mastery. should not be used as a criterion for inhibiting a
student's maturity.
A common curriculum should also provide for integration among
units of different subjects. Thus, for example. mathematics -
where appropriate -- will be included in and reinforce science     ~.        i
                                                                        , :
or social studies. so that the learning experiences and
concepts amplify one another and do not occur 1n isolation.
The reinforcement and subject interrelationships may be more
sophisticated and complex for the faster learners than for the
slower learners. Nevertheless, there is an attempt to provide
all studen~s with quality learning experiences. The curriculum
guide then. provides both for sequencing, pacing and cumulative
learning within subject areas, and for horizontal reinforcement
among the units of work of different subjects. This
combination of vertical and horizontal interrelationships -- or
integration -- is a basic characteristic of good curricular
organization. This is essential if the school is to provide
common content and learning experiences for students with
differences in readiness and learning capacity. It is also


3-2
more reflective of the reality in which the child lives and
will live as an adult-- the world is not Qrganized into
discrete and unrelated areas of knowledge!
3.2 RATIONALE - Why should there be q common curriculum for
grades 7 to 9 in Jamaica? The basic reasons were outlined in
Chapter 2. Three specific objectives are: to achieve greater
equity in the secondary school system to grade 9 at level 5; to
improve the quality of learning: and to enhance individual
productivity.
3.2.1 EQUITY - The most serious flaw in Jamaican secondary
education is lack of equity. Children have unequal access to
opportunities for learning. Some schools are more effective
than others. partly because of the different resources given to
them and in the use of those resources to provide a quality
education. Chapter 1 outlined some of the differences among
the three major types of secondary schools - new secondary.
traditional, high and all-age. Student achievement rises
markedly.
Table 3.1 compares the three major types of high schools. new
secondary schools and all-age schools -- which provide
education for the vast majority of lower secondary school
students -- 98.9% of the total.
The provision of a common curriculum -- along with supporting
implementation measures and adequate reform of the school
structure -- is recommended in this study, as the most likely
method of breaking into the existing cycl. that sees the
schools perpetuating poverty and inequity in Jamaican society.
3.2.2 QUALITY - Providing equal access to education is one
thing; providing equal access to quality education is another.
There is a serious need for a general improvement in teaching.
By the end of Grade 9. adolescents need to have the basics of a
quality education: literacy, numeracy and enough related
knowledge. skills and attitudes to cope with their
environment. Nearly one/half of all Jamaican students do not
achieve these standards in sChool. (See Chapter 6: Human
Resource Issues).
3.2.3 PRODUCTIVITY - The third objective of a common
curriculum is to enable J.amaican students to be productive
citizens. whether they continue their academic education. enter
technical or vocational training. or enter the world of work.
Every child needs a minimum set of cognitive competencies to be
productive; and when the majority of students acquire them they
will be trainable in the more complex job skills required in
today's workforce. A basic education of nine years is
essential if the majority of Jamaicans are to assume
professional. skilled or semi-skilled positions, and if they
are to have the capacity to acquire more education or training
rapidly and effectively.

3-4
3.2.4 In countries like Sweden 1 and Finland 2 , these
objectives of greater equity" and better quality were attained
by introducing a common or comprehensive school structure from
Grades 1 - 9 with free compulsory education for children
between the ages of 7 and 16. In both countries the intention
was to replace the variegated system with a system which would
unify. lengthen and broaden education for this age cohort.
In Sweden equity was ellsured by statutory provisions concerning:
      (1)  goals by which teaching was to be governed;
      (2)  subjects and courses which were to ,be provided by all
         schools throughout the country.
Recommendations were laid down concerning:
      (1) working methods;
      (2)  parental and pupil participation in decision making.

The reform of the curriculum in both these countries aimed at
promoting the balanced growth of integrated individuals who
learned through creative activity. In Sweden at the Grade 7 
9 level in the main the curriculum consisted of language.
mathematics, social subjects and science subjects and to a
lesser extent of home economics and handicraft.
3.3 GOALS - Goals for a general education for the first cycle
of secondary edu~ation should include the following:
         To think logically and clearly;
         To be aware, creative and flexible in outlook;
         To learn how to learn so as to acquire useful
         knowledge, skills and attitudes for the rest of one's
         life;
         To communicate effectively through written and oral
         expression;
         To be numerate and to manipulate efficiently minimum
         math functions;
         To appreciate and manage basic scientific and technical
         concepts and skills;

      in Finland's school reform. In: Comparative Education,
      Vol. 19 No.1 1983.

        To express oneself aesthetically and to appreciate
        aesthetic forms and behavior;

        To understand and participate meaningfully in social

certainlY, other goals or broad-based ~,earning objectives are
desirable, but these emerge as a Lore set of goals for each
child to attain.
3.4 SUBJECT AREAS - From these goals the study has identified
seven content areas: language arts, including foreign
language, mathematics, science. aes'thetics. technology studies,
social studies and personal family studies. These areas
                                                                   ~.
                                                                   ,
                                                                            ..  1
                                                                                "




organize conceptually the planned learning experiences which
should follow from the above goals as well as from the                          "
                                                                                    i
practical daily life of the child in schools:- then provide a      ".'

                                                                            ','
                                                                                    I




base for further studies in senior grades or for on-the-job
skill development in the world of work.
A survey turned up the list of 22 subjects currently taught in
Grades 7, 8 and 9 classes. (Chapter 1, Table 7). There is of
course, no necessary correlation between the syllabus of a
course taught in one school with a course taught under the same
title in another.
The seven subjects recommended below are an amalgamation of the
present areas of knowledge taught in the schools, combined with
the needs that people have now in the modern world.*
                          LANGUAGE ARTS
The overall aim is to foster students' acquisi~ion and use of
language so that they can communicate effectively, confidently
                                                                       "
and appropriately to a variety of audiences and for a variety
of purposes. The Language Arts Programme should consist of an
integration of listening. speaking. reading and writing and
thinking skills. It should provide for (i) identification of
the speaking, reading and language arts needs of ~ach student;
(ii) special attention for students who have not reached a
satisfactory level of speech and reading achievement in primary
school: and (iii) development of comprehension to the level
where students can, think reflec~ively about problem-solving and
achieve satisfaction in doing so. The programme should include
the basic categories of clear speech; of reading, both silent
and' oral; composition, in which children write in a variety of
styles for various purposes; literature. in which appreciation
of good prose and poetry is fostered: and enough grammar to
analyze the structure of language in the context of clear
expression. Particular attention should be paid to the
Creole-English dichotomy, with the use of tape recorders for
self-analysis and comparison with standards whenever possible.

3-(
.. 


       Children should be taught to have pride in the fact that they
       will know two languages if they master creole and standard
       English.  In some schools, with Ministry permission, a foreign
       language such as Spanish could be offered.

                                 MATHEMATICS

    The overall aim is to build on the numeracy knowledge and
    skills developed during Grades 1-6, in order to impart new
    knowledge and to encourage a deeper appreciation of
    mathematics.  In addition to the basic four operations of
    arithmetic. students should develop some knowledge and skills
    in algebra and geometry. A range of skills in these areas
    needs to be developed in order that students learn to describe
    a variety of situations in a precise manner and to approach the
    solving of problems in the real world with confidence. They
    should develop appreciation for the intrinsic power and beauty
  . of pattern within mathematics. by using applications and
    examples rooted in the physical world to develop examples of
    concepts in the early stages.

                                   SCIENCE

   The overall aim is to consolidate and extend the work done in
   Grades 1-6, focusing on process and inquiry skills, knowledge,
   and attitudes. The students' environment should be the prime
   source of materials for scientific study and analysis.   Content
   and process skills. as well as attitudes. can best be taught
   through an integrated science curriculum. as a necessary
   foundation for the separate sciences and technical/vocational
   subjects, after Grade 9; attention to the biographies of great
   scientists ~nd their working techniques is also useful. This
   approach will develop in Grade 7-9 students a degree of
   scientific literacy and technological awareness. of special
   importance for thos~ whose formal education may be concluded at
   Grade 9.

                               SOCIAL STUDIES

   Social studies is primarily concerned with the study of people
   -- their activities and relationships as they interact with
   each other and react to their physical and social environments
   in an effort to meet their needs. As a multi-disciplinary
   subject. it draws subject matter from history. geography,
   political science. economics. sociology and anthropology.   The
   overall aim is to explore how individuals and groups cope with
   living, change. conflict and interdependence. More
   specifically. it should develop in students an understanding
   for:  how people make use of their physical environment, and
   how the physical environment contributes to and influences the
   way people live; how people organize themselves in communities:



                                                                 3-7
                                                                   ,   .


selected national. regional and global issues and problems: and
continuity and change in human societies as well as the nature
of social change and reactions to change. The factual content
is best taught in themes. with modules of work integrated
around concepts of human society.
                       TECHNOLOGY STUDIES
Technology studies as proposed here is an innovation in the
curriculum. (see chapter 4 and the supplement on Tech/Voc.
Education) It combines an understanding of principles and
concepts taught in science with the real and practical world of
technology. Students should learn how simple and available                 ,"
machines work (ego telephones. toasters. small engines) by
working with them through assembly. repair. use and design
analysis. The overall aim is to develop an understanding of
how some basic and relatively simple technologies work; to
acquire skills to use them effectively; and to unde~stand them
as a process. Social technologies should also be discussed and                  ,
                                                                           .    :
developed: principles of mass production. management. and
banking. Technology studies should provide students with a
common base on which to take more advanced science or upon
which to specialize after Grade 9 in technical and vocational
studies. There will be some overlap with science. but the                  i.,'
emphasis should be on systems and manual skills.
                           AESTHETICS
Aesthetics combines music. drama. art. crafts and dance to
develop in students an understanding and appreciation for art
and art forms. The overall aim is to enhance the emotional
maturity of the adolescents. to encourage their creative self
expression. to sensitize them to be responsive to art forms.
and to enrich the quality of their lives. Jamaica is
especially rich in music. dance and art forms. and students
need to understand their history and function in contemporary
society. Aesthetics should help students to plan and engage in
leisure activities through their personal experience in the
arts: they should develop effective. cognitive and psycho-motor
skills in a context of expressive awareness and action.
              PERSONAL DEVELOPMENT/FAMILY STUDIES
Personal Development draws on physical education. health
education. and guidance. The overall aim is to deepen students
understanding about themselves, their emotional and physical
well-being. and how they can live healthfully and happily as
individuals and in families. While many of the activities
should contain knowledge and skills useful to attain this aim.
there should also be opportunities for learning career
development skills and the sociology of family life. There
should also be opportunities for exercise. sportsmanship,
sporting skills. team-building and general physical development.
3-8
·. 

   It is recommended that the F~mily Studies elements of the guide
   be developed distinctly. The curriculum guide should.combine
   Food & Nutrition. Clothing & Textiles. and Money Management
   under the general rubric of Family Studies.

   The schematic representation on Figure 1 depicts the seven
   subject areas and describes how the varying abilities of
   students can be accommodated through a mastery learning
   approach. At the lower end of the diagram. corrective
   instruction catering to the slower students is more teacher
   directed. At the upper end. where students are assumed to be
   capable of early mastery. there is increasing dlversification.
   independent activities and what Slavin (1997) refers to as
   lateral extension activities. The integrated nature of the
   curriculum is represented by .the dotted lines and arrows
   between subjects.

   3.5   TEACHING METHODS

   For the above goals and objectives to be achieved. the
   curriculum must be inquiry/discovery oriented. with students
   being given opportunities to raise questions and seek answers
   to these questions under the guidance of a teacher.   Teachers
   must believe that every child is capable of learning. although
   in different manners and at different rates.  Classrooms should
   be managed in an open. flexible and democratic manner. Reading
   and information-gathering skills should be emphasized.
   Problem-solving approaches. involving skills of research and
   analysis are recommended. as are group process skills -
   working in groups. respecting the opinions of others. assuming
   different roles (leader. rapporteur. participant. etc.).
   Communication in the classroom should be from student to
   student; teach~r to student; student to teacher and not only
   from teacher to class.

   Instruction should be organized in interdisciplinary units. and
   use should be made of classroom and community resources. both
   human and physical. as a means of enriching the experiences
   provided. Field trips. demonstrations. projects. role play,
   team teaching, radio and audio-visual aids each have a part to
   play in this process.

   Language development should be the concern of every teacher and
   every teacher should be aware of the Creole-Standard English
   continuum. and able to use an ESL approach in teaching
   English.  The teaching of good communication skills should be
   integrated into every activity in which students. are involved.
   Emphasis should be on oral work. which should always precede
   any writing task.   Approaches through drama should be
   frequently adopted.

     3.6                                         .
           REQUIREMENTS FOR IMPLEMENTING THE CURRICULUM
     This section identifies essential measures for" implementing a
     common curriculum for secondary schools: and it identifies
     likely additional measures whichwoul~ enhance the overall
     quality and effectiveness of such a curriculum. There are six
     essential measures, and to achieve minimum effectiveness, a'll
     seven measures -- in greater or less degree . - need to be
     adopted. The additional measures are more discrete in that
     each stands more by itself than in combination with the
     others. These additional measures have been ordered by 

     importance. 

                            ESSENTIAL MEASURES
     It must be emphasized that good planning and implementation at
     the Grades 7, 8 and 9 level of curriculum will be only
     marginally useful if the primary schools remain at their 

     present levels of effectiveness. 

     Most of the measures discussed below apply equally to the first
     and second cycles of primary education.
     3.7 CURRICULUM GUIDES - Curriculum guides are concise outlines
     of the fundamental ideas in a subject or area of study in the
     curriculum, adapted to a particular age group of students. The
     guides are for use by principals and teachers in the selection
     of topics for teaching and learning: and for education
     officers as standards for implementation and evaluation. They
     should be a high priority for printing and distribution by the
     Ministry.
     3.8 TEXTBOOK PROVISION, DISTRIBUTION AND ADAPTATION 
     Increased production and distribution of textbooks and
     materials is necessary for the implementation of the
     curriculum: and to ensure that all children have access to
     learning activities. However, their contents must be relevant
     to the topics of study in the curriculum guides so that
     teachers can use them effectively. It is not enough to provide
     textbooks; efforts must be made to ensure that they are used in
     pedagogically sound ways by teachers and students. Curricular
     adaptation of the texts and materials is an important first
     step in this regard; supervision by principals and education
     officers must include in-service strategies for this purpose.




                                                               3-11 

3.9 PRE-SERVICE TRAINING ADAPTATION - It is not env~sioned
that there will be fundamental changes in the teacher training      "I
curriculum: however. if teacher training is to influence the        ,
                                                                     !. 1
                                                                         t

instructional practices of teachers, at-least three steps           t...>

should be taken:
                                                                    "'"'
                                                                     ·    1

       (i)    teaching practices should be adapted to ~he various
              curriculum subject areas, curricular glides and
              textbooks
       (ii)  remediation should receive priority with respect to
             the basic skills of numeracy and literacy.
3.10 IN-SERVICE TEACHER EDUCATION - A compatible in-service
training program needs to be designed and implemented to
provide teachers with supportive knowledge, skills and
confidence so that they can teach the curriculum. This element      ,
                                                                         ,;
is discussed in more detail in Chapter 6.
 3.11 STRENGTHENING OF SUPERVISION - The improvement of
 instruction is dependent on adequate supervision in the
 schools. The current cadre of territorial education officers
 who supervise primary and secondary school operations must
 increase in number, be upgraded through training, and be           . i
 supported organizationally and legislatively. Implementation
 of the common curriculum will require at least 100 such
 officers. organizationally, one or two senior officers at each
.regional office would supervise the work of education officers
 in the field.
It is proposed that both primary and secondary officers be          ..   ,
managed by a single unit at the headquarters of the MOE and by
a single management unit in each regional office*. Status and
pay should be equal for both primary and secondary officers,
dependent on equivalent qualifications. Officers should be
seen as concerned with the entire public system, and the
responsibility for consistency and coherence in the school
system. Upgrading would take place through management training
courses which could be provided jointly by the Ministries of
                                                                    .... 

                                                                    . ,

Education and Public Service. Most importantly, education
officers need organizational authority from the Ministry
through revision of the Education Act and the Code of
Regulations to support them in their dealings with principals
and teachers. While the education officers need to be helpful
and advisory to principals and administrators. they will also
have to supervise teachers and principals with authority to
ensure that curricular guidelines are followed, and that
students are served and protected as the law requires.


*See Appendix C - the role of the Education Officer

3-12
3.12 EXAMINATION REFORM - Currently steps are being taken to
implement the National Assessment Program (NAP) the Common
Entrance Examination. The NAP includes achievement and
diagnostic testing throughout primary schools with the purpose
of reorienting teachers from focusing ~ainly on the quickest to
adapting instruction to individual differences. particularly
those of slow learners. Ultimately. at the completion of the
common curriculum at G~ade 9. a School Orientation Exam - in
~ombination with contiauous testing - should provide valid
criteria for selecting students for further academic or
technical schooling, or for the world of work. The NAP testing
would:.

         discriminate effectively between those able to succeed
         in the second cycle of secondary schools and the less
         capable pupils;

         not discriminate unfairly against underprivileged
         students:

         test knowledge and skills relevant to the curriculum
         and to society at large;

         provide incentives for teachers to raise the overall
         quality of basic education:

                       ADDITIONAL MEASURES

3.13   TEACHER SUPPLY AND INCENTIVES

The educational level. the training and the motivation of
teachers are crucial in raising the quality of teaching.
Therefore the Ministry needs to recruit, select. train and
promote the best possible candidates to ensure quality of
instruction in the curriculum. This is especially important
now as the profession of teaching is not as attractive as it
used to be in Jamaica. While teachers' salaries are
competitive with those of comparable civil servants,
opportunities in the private sector and abroad are perceived to
be greater. A combination of changes, however. involving
salaries. promotion increments and allowances could attract and
retain capable and motivated tea~hers. This issue is discussed
in more depth in Chapter 6.

3.14   SCIENCE AND TECHNOLOGY MATERIALS. EQUIPMENT AND CABS

Given the importance of science and technology in any
modernizing society, it is important that teaching allows for
much practical activity and that materials and equipment are
available. Hands-on work is important not only for students
pursuing further academic education, but as a base for those
going into technical and agricultural schools later on.


                                                              3-13
                                                                  .,
                                                                       -   .

The use of cheap, readily available materials has already been
described in a handbook, "Improvisations in Science: A
Handbook for Teachers" by J.Holung and J. Mitchelmore of the
MOE. Jamaica.
3.15   POLICY RESEARCH AND FORMATIVE EVALUATION
The Planning Unit and other rela_~d units in the MOE will need
strengthening of their data gathering and processing
resources. This is particularly the case regarding statistics
related to enrollments, intakes. repetition. drop-outs. student
graduates; and teaching and administrat~ve personnel. In
addition. formative evaluation exercises of the kind planned in
the NAP are needed to identify. analyze and provide
alternatives to trouble spots in the teaching of the common
curriculum. The same should be done for the large-scale
in-service training that ~ill have to be conceived and
launched. The information thus gleaned should be constantly
given to Education Officers and senior officers across the
Ministry. As a prerequisite for accurate data. Education
Officers should continually audit the accuracy of school
information at the classroom level.
3.16   SCHOOL RENOVATION
Without assuming capital costs for construction or measures for
extensive renovation. there are opportunities for communities
to assist in school renovation. All-age schools, particularly
in rural areas, need community support to repair or renovate
classrooms. teachers quarters. or assembly halls. In addition.
a policy of matching grants from the MOE along with community
efforts can assist in these efforts. Equally important is the
mObilization of community support whereby the parents have an          . !
identity with the school and its new common curriculum. When
parents are active in the educational process. it is more
likely that their children will attend school with motivation.
Recent moves by the Ministry of Education in this direction
hold great promise.
3.17 As a final comment on a common curriculum, it is
important for the reader to understand that.curriculum cannot
be dealt with in isolation from its context. The experiences
that students have. in school "getting an education" - are the         ,   ;
result of:
        the formal plans that are made
        the behavioral effects of processes and plans
        the organizational and policy framework
This chapter has dealt with all three of these elements.
Chapters 5, 6 and 7 discuss with various aspects and
consequences for implementation of these three domains, if
genuine reform is to be accomplished.

·.

     A Common Curriculum - Process and Content
     A school system that has a well-developed, up-to-date
     curriculum, tailored both to the needs of the students and of·
     the nation, has a backbone from which~trong. flexible,
     responsive school activities can develop. And the vertebrae in
     the backbone of a school system are curriculum guides that
     outline the studies required of students.



     This study cannot overemphasize the importance of the central
     role that curriculum guides, developed by the Ministry of
     Education. ought to play in the organization and management of
     the schools.   If MOE curriculum guides are considered to be the
     central basis for the organization of the school system. then
     they become the basic source of supervision and direction for
     teachers. Each subject or each grade must have an outline of
     what is expected of students. and therefore, of their teachers.

     What are Curriculum Guides

     A curriculum guide should be a concise outline of the
     fundamental ideas in a subject or an area of study in the
     curriculum. adapted to age groups of students.

             Sometimes. guides contain sub-topics that are described
             as outcomes expected of students.

             sometimes also. there are suggestions about teaching
             methods that are suited to a topic.

             There are sometimes suggestions or even prescriptions
             about timetable allocations for a subject.

             The best guides for students in primary and first-cycle
             secondary schools are interdisciplinary, or integrated
             in the patterns of knowledge that they require students
             to study.  Children to age 14 are still trying to form
             and test values. Research has shown that value
             formation stops when the specialized teaching of
             separate subjects begins in secondary schools.

            Se.nior cycle guides can be more subject-specif i·c as
            students begin to specialize and narrow their tertiary
            or vocational choices.

            Evaluation processes compatible with the goals and
            structures of the subject of a curriculum guide can
            also be included in a curriculum guide, but the
            outcomes that are expected of students should not be
            stated as absolutes. Each student is unique, therefore
            teachers must have latitude to adapt outcomes to each
            child's pattern of growth and development.
                                                                 :; -15
Developing Curriculum Guides

The development of national curriculum quides requires a            :,
                                                                   ' ..-I
                                                                             t


process of consultation under the guiding hand of Ministry of
Education officers.  The Ministry of Education already has a
statement of goals for the curriculum of the schools.  These
'loah shouid be the guiding principles that govern the work of
~urriculum committee members, after the statement has been
examined and approved by the senior executive group of the
Ministry, including the Minister and the Minister of State.

The members of guide development committees are usually
teachers, professors, subject experts and sometimes future
employers. The documents that are developed must be compatible
with the goals of the nation, must be based on the best
available research about learning and about the subject matter,
and must be capable of implementation by teachers, using
available resources.

It follows, of course, that guides must be periodically
reviewed and revised, in order to maintain an appropriate blend
of:
                                                                       . .i

        philosophical goals: 

        research findings, and 

        realistic objectives . . 


When completed and approved, curriculum guides are used to
direct and evaluate what students learn, and what teachers
teach. A description of the role of the Integrator in
developing the curriculum is provided in Appendix c.

Grades One to Six                                                            .

                                                                            ':1


There is already a complete set of Jamaican-developed
curriculum guidelines for Grades One to Six. Although these
are nearly ten years old, they deal with the basic skills of
 learning and are not in urgent need of change. There are
higher priorities for curriculum development right now, so
"revision of these documents should be delayed until the matters
that follow have been undertaken.                                  .'-.'

The existing Grades l-to 6 documents should be redistributed,
and reprinted if necessary, in numbers sufficient to re-equip
education officers, principals, teachers, school offices and
teacher education institutions.  Some thought and effort might
be given to summarizing and consolidating the six documents
into one, if economies in printing and distribution can be         "   .....

suggestion) .                                                      ....

     Grades 7, 8 and 9
     A process of development of curriculum guides for Grades Seven,
     Eight and Nine is beginning now. Teams will have been named to
     begin writing documents. These teams ~hould include not only
     one or two academics but good practicing teachers and education
     officers with broad experience in classroom observation.
     Drafts of the documents produced by the writing teams should be
     circulated for comment and criticism by interested and
     qualified persons. before final recommendation for approval and
     printing is made.
     Grades 10 and 11
     The next few years should be ·a period of transition for Grades
     10 and 11. as the effects of the intrOduction of a common
     curriculum in Grades 7, 8. and 9 are realized.
     There are some guides available for the subjects taught in
     Grades 10 and 11 of secondary schools. high schools and
     technical schools.
     For the academic subjects, existing guides should be used by
     education officers on an ad hoc basis, until the effects of the
     new curriculum in Grades 7, 8, and 9 can be ascertained. CXC
     syllabi will be used for many subjects.
     For the tech/voc subjects, guides exist in all subjects and
     should continue to be used until the effects of any new
     emphases in Grades 7. 8 and 9 can be ascertained. along with
     the results of the current analysis of the appropriateness of
     the CXC syllabi in technical subjects.
     In general, the goal should be to work closely with the CXC to
     establish syllabi that are suitable for Jamaican students, with
     regard to both content and difficulty.
     For students who do not sit the CXC the alternate route to the
     Secondary School Certificate should remain in place for the
     next few years. Providing temporary courses of study for these
     students should be a major priority of the core and tech/voc
     curriculum units, using the field experience of the education
     officers. until there is sUfficient information and experience
     to mount a major revision of the curriculum for these grades.
     Curriculum Guides and Textbooks
     The Primary Textbook programme has been closely allied with the
     Grades 1-6 curriculum guides from the start. The manuscripts
     of the readers were written through the initiative of.the Core
     Curriculum Unit and were ready for publication when the free
     book/printing programme was conceived. Local authors are a
     quality element in the Jamaican school system.

High interest. low~vocabulary books should be produced by the

Curriculum Guides and Testing
The National Assessment Programme tests are already coordinated
with the existing curriculum guides at the primary school level.
The pattern of aChievement testing in the Grades 1 to 6
assessment programme should be extended to the Grades 7 to 9
common curriculum.
As soon as the Grade Six Achievement Test (NAP) is validated,                r-",
it should be used as the Common Entrance Exam. Such a move
will_increase the use of the curriculum guides by primary
teachers, particularly in Social Studies and Science and
Aesthetics. and thus enable teachers to help students develop
better awareness of themselves and the world.
A Grade Nine Achievement Test. or orientation exam. should be
developed. on the same model as the Grade Six Test. as soon as
the curriculum guides for Grade 7. 8 and 9 are completed. (See
appendix (a) - The National Assessment Program).                             ,        I




Cu:riculum Guides and Teacher Education
If MOE curriculum guides are considered to be the basic
organizing element of the school system, then they also become
the major component of the academic studies at teacher training
institutions. Teachers-in-training have to learn about process
- about how to teach - but their other major objective is to
learn about what to teach - about content.

3-1B
·.

     The MOE curriculum guides must be familiar to all teachers; and
     beginning teachers must be particularly knowledgeable about
     those curriculum guides for which they will b~ responsible in
     their first years on the job. An adequate supply of curriculum
     guides must always be available at th~Teachers' Colleges and
     the Faculty of Education of the university.
     Curriculum Guides and Teacher In-Ser.vice
     The in-service programs that are a constant necessity for
     teachers everywhere normally should be based on the curriculum
     guides. Education officers should be constantly assessing the
     needs for improving teaching in their groups of schools. The
     officers should then help to plan in-service programs to meet
     those needs. The curriculum guide should serve as the
     coordinating tool for cooperative action by teachers' subjects
     associations. by teachers' college staff members. by visiting
     consultants. or by anyone else from the public or private
     sectors who can be useful in the improvement of instruction.
     It is often argued that curriculum guides are "not used" by
     teachers and therefore should not be produced. or should be
     distributed only to educati~n officers and principals. In
     either case. the role of the curriculum guide as the backbone
     of the school system is not clearly understood.
     Good teachers will use the guides as signposts through the
     complex and confusing variety of sometimes conflicting advice
     from experts. of the variety of textbooks and other prepared
     materials advocated by publishers. and of the problems
     associated with planning and adapting work to the needs of
     different students.
     Education officers will use the guides as the evaluation
     criterion for observing the work of teachers and students.
     Teachers who do not use the guides will still find themse~ves
     directed by their contents. because both approved textbooks and
     Ministry achievement tests should be designed from the topics
     of the guides. Ministry education officers. working with
     school principals. will evaluate a teacher'S performance
     against her or his skill and dedication in helping students
     understand the learning described" in the guides.
     We have given little attention to the topic of computers in
     education. for two reasons. Firstly. Jamaica has scarce
     resources. and despite the continual lowering of costs. the
     possibility of acquiring significant numbers of computers is
     remote. The danger of further skewing the inequities in the
     system by providing a few machines that would. in the present
     system. go to the already privileged high schools. is contrary
     to the thrust of this study.


                                                               3-19
                                                                    ..              .. 




Secondly. and as important. is the fact that computers in
themselves are not a subject of study in the lower secondary
school. but rather. a different kind of tool that can enhance
some kinds of thinkinq. Computers are oeinq used in three
distinct ways in those countries that have been able to afford
them in quantity in their schools.

           help the user compose prose (word processors). or
           manipulate fiqures (spread sheets). or retrieve               ....~ !.

           qeoqraphy. indeed. most subjects.
 Of the three uses. computer science has typically developed
 first. as the understandinq of computers has developed from

'that are used in business and industry.
Only recently has the use of curriculum-content proqrammes for

       Chapter 4
       THE SPECIAL CASE OF TECHNICAL/VOCATIONAL EDUCATION IN THE
       CURRICULUM
       NOTE: See Volume II - "The Reform of Technical/Vocational
       Education in Jamaica - Analysis and Recommendations" for the
       in-depth study on which this Chapter is based.
       In common with many countries around the world, Jamaica has
       been providing pre-vocational education to 12 to IS year old
       students in the curriculum of the secondary schools. There is
       now a realization, worldwide, that the effort of the past 20
       years has not produced the hoped-for results. A new pattern
       must be developed.
       Currently in Jamaica, the pre-vocational components of
       Grades 7,8 and 9 are grouped under five headings: Agriculture,
       Art and Crafts, Business Education, Home Economics and
       Industrial Arts. Substantial investment has been made in
       teacher training, workshop~ and equipment.
       This study is proposing a most significant change in the Grade
       7-9 component of the technical vocational curriculum; that
       change being the consolidation of the technical vocational
       curriculum into two subject areas for timetabling - Technology
       Studies and Personal/Family Studies. The rationale for this
       recommendation is based primarily on the fact that technical
       vocational education at the secondary level has far outgrown
       its capacity to provide effective education and training. As a
       result, secondary vocational education represents an
       unful£illed promise - in terms of its pivotal role in the
       national human resource developme,nt strategy, and in terms of
       its ability to provide a proper educational foundation for
       daily living.
       This situation has occurred for a variety of reasons. and they
       can all be subsumed under two key factors: the overexpansion
       of the curriculum; and the significant" shortage of resources:
       financial. human and material. ~otwithstanding, the issue at
       this junction is how should the- s i tua tion be amended. Even
       with a,massive inflow of resources. the situation is not likely
       to make any significant change for the better, if programming
       is continued at the present level. There are just too many
       structural defects. To attend to one without attending to the
       other will lead to failure; and to attend to all at the same
       time may cause such a level of confusion and disorientation. it




                                                                   4-1 

                                                                   .
                                                                        ;
                                                                                .,

                                                                       -~

                                                                        -.
too will be doomed to fail. It seems that the prudent response
is to cut back on the level of proqramminq (number of courses 
        ,'""""!

offered) and to build a redu~ed but vibrant delivery system                     )
which could be effectively supported and maintained in order to        "

It is recommended that the srecialized skill traininq that is           ..

                                                                                 :

have a better idea of their career interes~s, are more capable

and theory.

                                                                            "


It is this last point that is crucial to technical education in
the last decade of the twentieth century_ T~e skills of the
work force must beat higher technical levels than formerly, if
countries are to compete in international markets. Workers
must know somethinq of the "why" as well as the "what" and
"how" of the work they are performinq, whether it is a
secretary usinq a word processor instead of a typewriter, a
farmer aware of the chemical hazards in his pesticides, a hotel
employee makinq entries into the computer work station in the
kitchen or an employer who must be able to use spreadsheets to
handle the complex variables of planninq and manaqinq a company_
Paradoxically, as specialization becomes more necessary to deal
with new knowledqe, inteqrated knowledqe also becomes more
necessary_ Both the worker and the manaqer who know their
speciality in detail, and who also know somethinq of the wider
relationships of their speciality. are the people who are
already most in demand and most useful in the modern workinq
world.                                                                          .,
                                                                                 i

                                                                        .... .J
For technical education, this means that hiqher levels of
academic education must accompany technical traininq, in order
that theory and practice may be understood as complementary and
not as opposite ends of a spectrum.
1.0   THE PROBLEMS WITH PRE-VOCATIONAL EDUCATION                                ",




Diversifyinq the secondary school curriculum to include
prevocational subjects has been an educational policy that has
been promoted viqorously, by developinq countries and by
                                                                       :...,·.".
international aqencies, for the past twenty years. It was felt
that the advantaqes would be:
        wider access to secondary schools by less privileqed            _,f

        socio-economic qroups (better equity)
        better aChievement in both pre-vocational and academic
        subjects by all students (internal efficiency)

4-2
        better employment opportunities for students when
        equipped with a combination of academic and
        pre-vocational skills (external efficiency)
        greater awareness and more def~nite ideas concerning
        employment and the world of work by students

A considerable body of evidence has been accumulated in the
past few years concerning pre-vocational (diversified) and
vocational programmes. The evidence does not support the
potential advantages listed above. The following has been
learned:

        With very few exceptions, the general quality of
        education has not improved; and where improvements
        occurred, they were qualified and uneven, mainly
        because of a shortage of qualified teachers, of books,
        materials and equipment, particularly consumable
        materials.

        Employment opportunities had unsatisfactory outcomes.
        Graduates of pre-vocational and vocational programs do
        no better in the labour market than graduates of other
        kinds of programmes. Employers commonly express
        dissatisfaction with the first.

        There was no empirical evidence of student attitude
        change towards labour, and in the majority of cases,
        students still preferred academic to vocational
        training.

        Ser~ous questions are being asked about the comparative
        cost-effectiveness of a diversified versus an academic
        secondary school curriculum because of the large
        capital and recurrent expenditures for pre-vocational
        facilities compared to the relatively low earnings of
        graduates.

The foregoing statements are based on the followin9 reports:
   Wadi Haddad, "Diversified Secondary Curriculum Projects:         A
   Review of World Bank Experience 1963-l979", Report EDT
   57. World Bank:   1987

   George Psacharopoulos, "Curriculum Diversification,
   Cognitive Achievement and Economic Performance: Evidence
   from Columbia and Tanzania", Report EDT 80, World Bank;
   1987




                                                            4-3 

In addition. there are other publications appearing now 

discussing the issue and raising similar questions.   For 

example: 


         "Vocationalizing Education:   An International 

         Perspective" edited by Lauglo and Lillis. Un. of Lond .·        -    ,

         Instit. of Ed .· Pergamon Press. England. 1988. 


        "Towards a New Industrial America". by Berger et al.         .....'":t
                                                                         -  I
                                                                             ,
        Mass. Instit. of Tech .· Scientific American. June. 1989.            ,

1.2   A COMMON CURRICULUM AND TECHNOLOGY STUDIES                     ".~




A common curriculum for Grades 7-9 as proposed in this report 

is an attempt to consolidate rather than diversify subjects. 

The technical/vocational subject areas must be subjected to 

the same process in order to manage the continuing expansion of 

kn?wle~ge that is affecting education everywhere. 


Technology Studies is a key component of a common curriculum
                                                                     "       .
                                                                             '.

because it pulls together much of the knowledge and some of the
skill development in the original 5 subject areas of tech/voc        -..      ~ 



education in Jamaica. and gives them a conceptual focus.   That
focus is the application and practice of scientific. social as 

well as natural. principles and processes in everyday life. 

The focus is on learning. more than training. 


In Chapter 3 it was asserted that a common curriculum should 

provide students with a basic education and the means for 

lifelong learning.  A basic education then is not skill 

training for immediate entry into the job market. Various 

analyses in recent years have confirmed that employers and 

parents recognize this.  The approach recommended for 

Technology Studies relates well to other curricular areas such 
     .       ,
                                                                    .. J
as science. mathematics. social studies. and should enhance
integrated studies as a teaching method.                            .. ,
                                                                       ,


Most importantly. Jamaica must participate and compete in a
 scientific and technological world.   The people need to have a
-solid foundation on which to build more sophisticated knowledge     "   ...
 and skills related to modern computers. machinery and              .... i
 equipment ·. and to complex systems. techniques and procedures.
 The learning of vocat~onal skills should take place in
 vocational training institutions. in no case before a solid
 base of basic skills has been acquired by the individual.          .
                                                                    " 

 including an awareness of the technical aspects of society.

        2.0   OPTIONS
        The foregoing concerns - the need to consolidate the diverse
        skill areas, to widen and deepen their content. and to
        emphasize science and technology in the common curriculum 
        lead to the follo~ing kinds of choices.

        Three options are suggested:

        Option 1 - Maintain the same skill areas currently used, but
        broaden their content, upgrade their knowledge base, and
        organize them more coherently.
                    Industrial Arts. including woodwork. metalwork and
                   technical drawing;
                   Art and crafts;
                   Agriculture;
                   Home Economics;
                   Business Education.

        Option 2 - Reduce the number of subjects according to current
        priorities and values. broaden and upgrade their content, and
        organize them more coherently.

                 Industrial Arts          Industrial Arts
                 Home Economics    OR     Business Education
                 Agriculture              Home Economics

        Option 3 - Reorganize and upgrade the entire range of skill
        areas, upgrade the content thus derived and relate it to other
        subject areas in the curriculum through a theme approach such
        as Technology Studies.

        Because of the priority need for science and technology in the
        educational opportunities offered to young people. the need to
        educate and not to train children aged 12 to 15, and the
        absolute need to consolidate the excessive amounts of subjects
        matter. the third option is recommended.

        3.0   TECHNOLOGY STUDIES

    Definition - Technology Studies is the application and practice
    of scientific principles and processes in everyday life.

    The focus should be on how people develop and use
    organizational procedures, psycho-motor skills. tools,
    machines. rou~ines and communica~ion patterns to adapt and
    control their physical. biological and social environment.   It
    should be an inclusive perspective which describes, explains
    and illustrates how people solve everyday problems.




                                                                   4-5 

                                                                  .. 


                                                                         ,-~      .
Technology Studies must be interdisciplinary in that it should
draw principles and examples from physics, chemistry, bioloqy,
agriculture. electricity. mechanics. drawing, metalwork.
woodwork. and related areas. It should~lso draw on the social
sciences in a major way, to explain and illustrate how people
organize themselves for solving technical problems.
As a curriculum innovation, Technology Studies should abstract
ideas from the tech/voc subjects now taught in Grades 7, 8 and
9 and organize the concepts into themes. Themes are relatively
easy to identify since the subjects chosen should be the
development, understanding and appreciation of scientific
applications in everyday life.  (see Appendix A - Suggestions
for Curriculum Guides in the Seven Subjects of a Core
Curriculum)

This study does not have the mandate to prescribe conte~t fot
curriculum guides, nor should it. The section of Chapter 3
that deals with curriculum guide development describes one
possible administrative pattern for developing guides, but the
writing of guides should be done by educators in the system.
The following 4 tables explain the thinking behind the idea of
Technology Studies.

broader framework of technology and technical/vocational

technical/vocational subjects in the second cycle of secondary
school; however. some prerequisites cannot be avoided (e.g .·

Special attention should be given to the design of curriculum
modules that give general support to the technical studies that
will be carried on in Grades 10 and 11.
4.2 Equipment, Facilities and Materials - There will be little
need for new equipment and facilities for Technology Studies.
Much of the existing specialized equipment at the Grades 7, B
and 9 level should be relocated to the senior secondary level.     ."",
Considerable planning will be necessary to effect this change.            -   ,
There will be a need however, for materials such as a simple
wiring diagram to illustrate basic principles of electricity,
and the wiring of the school ieself can be used to illustrate
the application of the principles. Tools and machines such as
irons, toasters or other appliances can be used for
disassembly, assembly, and repair to illustrate basic
principles of conduction and resistance of electricity; and
also of the processes of design, materials manufacture,
assembly, and marketing that constitute the technological
systems surrounding relatively simple appliances.
 4.3 Teachers - Because Technology Studies is an innovation.
 the training and supervision of teachers will have to be
 carefully managed. Territorial Education Officers will need to
 have in-service sessions about the intent and content of the
 subject, in order that they may help teachers and teacher                    .
-college instructors to implement the curriculum guide             .~ ,




 effectively.

various sections of the Supplement.
