4-12
Chapter 5
ALTERNATIVE STRUCTURES AND THEIR   COST~


5.1    Overview
The main objective of the current reform of the Jamaican
secondary education system is the definition of a common
curriculum for all the students of Grades 7-9. A common
curriculum is not an end in itself. however. The aim of the
reform is an educational system in which all students complete
nine years of schooling, and finish Grade 9 with basic
literacy, numeracy, and analytical skills: in which all
students have equal access to opportunities for further
education or training; and in which all students develop the
capacity to contribute as productive citizens to the further
development of Jamaica.
The accomplishment of these objectives, therefore, implies at
least three major policy changes in the Jamaican secondary
school system:
      1)  The postponement of selection within the system until
          after the completion of Grade 9, so that all children
          are provided equal opportunities to complete the new
          curriculum;
      2)  The expansion of enrollments in Grades 7-9, until all
          children remain in school through the end of Grade 9;
          and
      3)  Improvements in the quality of education provided to
          all students in Grades 7-9, but especially those who
          now attend all-age schools.
         A related condition for the reform of secondary
         education is improvement in the quality of primary
         schools.
These objectives will not be achieved immediately: policies to
accomplish them will have to be phased in over a number of
years. Reorganizing the educational system to achieve these
goals will also exact significant costs. not only in money but
also in administrative disruption and political opposition.
There are a number of different ways in which tQey might be
approached in the short and medium term, each of which will
require different commitments of time, money, and political
resources. In this chapter, five main policy alternatives are
identified, and their costs and some of the barriers to


                                                              5-1
                                                                          -J
                                                                   ~I




                                                                   ;."   .~



implementation outlined.  These five alternatives to not
exhaust the possibilities for reform in secondary education.
nor are they necessarily mutually exclusive. Any policy that
is finally adopted by the Ministry of Education will almost
certainly represent some combination of approaches.  The
alternatives presented here do. however. provide an
introduction to the kinds of choices that educational policy       ;;      .
makers will face as they seek to rationalize Jamaica's
secondary school system.                                                      j
                                                                       ·"'i


II.   PROJECTING THE COSTS OF REFORM

It should be noted at the outset that the value of cost            ... 1
analysis in education as in any other field depends crucially
on two factors:    the quality of the data on which the analysis
is based and the realism of the assumptions that guide the             -"
                                                                              j
analysis.   The quality of the available data in this case
should not be exaggerated.    The ongoing Unit Costs Study has
produced some useful estimates of recurrent costs in different     .' ,
                                                                         . ,
parts of the educational system.    These are the best estimates
that can be produced with the data now available. but they are
fftr from precise. and significant gaps in the data persist.
Fugitive data on capital costs have been collected from various
sources. including" documentation from past education projects.
Capital investments by the Ministry of Education have been
sporadic in the past decade. however. and many of the data are
out of date or affected by idiosyncratic circumstances. or
both.   Data on enrollments in primary and secondary schools
remain suspect. and estimates of drop-out and repetition rates
vary widely depending on the assumptions chosen by the
analyst.   Data from the school mappingexe~cise that should
provide information on the present state of educational
facilities in Jamaica have been PQblished for only a single
parish. though the exercise has been completed in three other
parishes and is nearly done in the others.    As a result of
these and other similar circumstances. the projections of costs
that are presented below are subject to a potentially large              -,
                                                                   .....,
                                                                   .

degree of error. and should not be accorded an undue degree of
precision or reliability.

An example will illustrate the scope of th~ problem.  One major
component of the cost of comprehensive secondary education
reform will be the cost of new school places for students who
now drop out of school before completing Grade 9.  Estimates of
this cost depend not only on the cost of school places but also
on the number of dropouts who might in the future remain in
school.  It is widely assumed that about 85 percent of each age
cohort now complete Grade 9. but estimates range as low as 70
percent. depending on the data source and the assumptions
chosen by the analyst.  Projecting the cost of enrollment




5-2
expansion on the basis of conventional assumptions about
drop-out rates thus means that the estimates produced-may be as
much as 12 percent too high or 20 percent too low. depending on
the real (but unknown) drop-out rate. The Planning and
Statistics Units within the Ministry are making rapid progress
in their efforts to increase the quality and timeliness of the
data they coll~ct and analyze. but for now the problem remains.
The uncertainty that necessarily accompanie~ these (and other)
cost projections advises a great deal of caution in their
interpretation. Relatively small shifts in assumptions or
errors in the underlying data can produce large changes in
projected costs. Iri the analyses that follow. there is an
attempt to be explicit about the assumptions that guide the
analysis. and the realism of these should be very carefully
considered in any use of the cost projections that are
presented in this report.
III.   POLICY OPTIONS
Option A - Incremental Improvement in the Present System
This first alternative proposes some relatively small but
critically important changes in the character and quality of
education provided in Jamaican secondary schools. within the
context of the prevailing structure of the secondary education
system. The irreducible core~f the reforms now under
discussion is the definition and adoption of a common
curriculum for students in Grades 7-9. This strategy calls fOT
the development of such a curriculum. and for the provision of
the minimal resources necessary to ensure its implementation in
the schools. These would include a major expansion of the
in-service training capabilities ~nd efforts of the Ministry of
Education. to acquaint secondary school teachers with the new
curriculum: the implementation of the National Assessment
Program. to renew the pedagogical focus on the formal
curriculum in primary schools; the extension of the TextbooK
Rental Scheme to students in all-age schools; the establishment
of Regional Education Offices and the appointment of
Territorial Education Officers. to monitor the adoption of the
new curriculum in .he schools and to provide additional
in-service assistance to teachers and administra~ors: and
distance education facilities for. children in remote areas who
lack access to secondary schools~ Direct consideration of the
underlying issues of selection and equalization is otherwise
postponed under this alternative.




                                                           5-3 

                                                                   ....   ,


Projects addressing many of these issues are already underway.
The NAP for Grade 6 is nearly ready for implementation. and the
Overseas Development Agency is committed to a project that will
extend the textbook rental scheme to all remaining secondary
schools. including Grades 7-9 in the all-age schools. The
Ministry of Education is committed to the reestablishment of
Re1ional Education Offices. and recognizes the importance of.
Territorial Education Officers. The need for a new common
curriculum for Grades 7-9 is also acknowledged, and preliminary
planning is underway under the auspices of two IBRD teams.
With the implementation of these ongoing projects. the
development of projects to address Ministry commitments in
other areas (e.g. curriculum. TEO's). and the expansion of the      . '~!
Ministry's capabilities in distance education, the minimum        ,i




conditions for improvement in secondary education will have
been satisfied.
The advantage of this strategy is that it can probably be
achieved in a relatively short period of time. with a
relatively small commitment of new resources. The obvious
disadvantage is that it addresses the policy changes needed to
accomplish the larger objectives of secondary education reform
indirectly if at all. The highly stratified distribution of        ·      >
educational resources and opportunities in Jamaican secondary          ..,
schools ~s left intact. and the Common Entrance Examination (or
its functional equivalent) remains in place at the end of Grade
6. Current rates of drop-out can. therefore, be expected to
persist indefinitely. and universal enrollment in Grades 7-9
will not soon be achieved. This strategy can be expected to
produce some incremental improvements in the performance of the
present system. but no major changes in the system with respect
either to equity or to efficiency.
Estimates of the costs oi reform under this alternative are
                                                                   ,--,
presented in Table S.l. They include the costs of employing.
training. and maintaining 100 Territorial Education Officers.
the cost of developing the Ministry'S capacity to produce and
disseminate distance education materials. the cost of a massive
in-service training effort for all teachers in secondary
schools to familiarize them with the new curriculum, and the
cost of increasing the capitarization of the Textbook Rental      ..... !
Scheme. with sufficient allowance made for administrative
expense and contingencies. The cost of developing and
implementing and Grade 9 NAP is also included ..
In the view of the study team these costs must be viewed as
minimal. Resources to cover many of them have already been
appropriated, either by the Ministry or by donors, and
resources to cover the balance must be found .if real
improvements are t~ take place in Jamaican secondary schools.

The incremental recurrent cost of implementing changes in each
of these areas is on the order of J$S million per year. which       'l
                                                                    ': I
represents a budgetary burden that it should be possible to         ~J
bear under all but the bleakest economic circumstances. The
other four reform options discussed below assume at least this
level of expenditure. plus additional costs entailed by more
wide-ranging changes in the secondary school system.   .
It should be noted that even this minimal reform program will
not be immune to political opposition. For example, the
definition of a common curriculum for all students in Grades
7-9 may be perceived as a "watering down" of the traditional
high school curriculum. The social. political. and emotional
significance of the Common Entrance Examination means that any
eff ort to alter its conte.nt or function. much less abol ish it.
is likely to arouse great controversy. The political obstacles
facing more comprehensive approaches to secondary school reform
will be even greater.
Option B - Expansion of High School Enrollments
The focus of this strategy is on expanding enrollments in
secondary and vocational high schools. which have traditionally
been the goal of most students within the Jamaican educational
system. Two principal means would be adopted to achieve this
end: raising enrollments in traditional high schools through        ·
                                                                    ·
                                                                        -,,
increasing pupil/teacher ratios and introducing the shift
system: and continuing the conversion of new secondary schools
to high schools. (With the consolidation of workshops and
vocational training facilities some might also be converted to
technical high schools.) Enrollments in the remaining new
secondary schools might be raised simultaneously, by similar
means, to draw students in Grades 7-9 out of all-age schools.
Over time. therefore. this strategy would aim to bring about a
steady increase in enrollments in the secondary schools with
the best facilities and teachers. and a corresponding decline
in enrollments in those with fewer resources.
The main advantage of this strategy as against the others is
that it acknowledges the traditional aspirations of Jamaican
households. by providing more students with the opportunity to
attend high schools. Investment and new recurrent expenditure
are focused on those schools that students want to attend. _
rather than those that they are obliged to attend. Subsidiary            ;
advantages include the fact that selectivity within the             ·-'
educational system will be de-emphasized as larger numbers of
students obtain admission to high schools. While the Common
Entrance Examination may continue to determine access to the
best schools. the numbers of examination "failures" will
steadily decline. In addition, a rising percentage of students
in secondary schools will receive the full -benef i ts of the new
common curriculum.
There are two main disadvantages to this strategy. First,
expansion of enrollments in high schools will almost inevitably
be accompanied by some deterioration in the quality of
education provided. The available evidence_on the effects of
increased pupil/teacher ratios or shiCt systems on student
achievement is equivocal, but no one argues that the effect is
positive. In any case, the average quality of "high schools"
will be reduced as more new secon1ary schools are converted.
The lion's share of the J$lO million thus far allocated for the
ongoing conversion of eleven new secondary schools has been
used for the physical rehabilitation of the schools, while a
smaller amount has been used to provide four weeks of
in-service training to teachers and administrators. No funds
have yet been allocated for the expansion and improvement of
the science labs, libraries, and other facilities that would
make these schools "high schools" in more than name.
Second, for the foreseeable future some presumably large
residual group of students will be left in all-age schools,
still without access to "real" secondary education. Under the
assumptions that underlie the cost projections presented in
Table 5.2, more than 37,000 students will be left in all-age
schools. Because of the geographical distribution of secondary
and high schools and the costs associated with "high school"
attendance, these students are likely to be drawn .
disproportionately from poor and rural households. Reforms
that provide additional advantages to the best schools at the
effective expense of the worst may well increase the already
enormous inequalities in the secondary school system. They may
also increase the disaffection of the students who remain in
all-age schools, and of their teachers. An offsetting effect
might be a decline in pupil/teacher ratios resulting from the
enrollment of students in high rather than all-age schools.
Nevertheless, the drop-out rate could rise, and net enrollment
rates fall, as a result of "reform" along these lines.
Estimates of the costs of reform under this alternative are
presented in Table 5.2. The capital costs entailed by this
strategy are quite low in the short run, because this approach
to reform relies on making more intensive use of existing
facilities rather than expanding or constructing additional
ones. These costs will rise in the future, however, as target
levels of school quality in secondary and high schools are
raised. Incremental recurrent costs are high, because of the
need for rela ti vely large nu.mbers of new teachers as
enrollments in high schools are increased. The cost of
potential deterioration in the quality of instruction provided
in traditional or newly converted high schools should also be
noted. Some indicators of declining quality might include
rising rates of drop-out. repetition, or examination failure.

·. 



   The benefits of this strategy in terms of student learning are
   uncertain, and depend u~on the relative weights of changes in
   the aChievement of students traditionally enrolled in high
   schools~ students newly enrolled in high schools, and students
   remaining in all-age schools. Achievement among the first and
   third groups might be expected to fall to some degree with
   these reforms, while achievement in the middle group might be
   expected t~ rise, but the relative magnitudes of these changes
   are impossible to predict.
   The budgetary savings to be achieved under this policy could be
   large: the marginal per student cost of raising pupil teacher
   ratios and adding second shifts in traditional high schools is
   likely to be significantly lower than the present average per
   pupil cost, as evidence from the recent study of unit costs
   suggests. Additional savings could be realized through the
   abolition of publicly supported boarding facilities for
   stud~nts in Grades 7-9, and especially so if these facilities
   can be converted to classroom or other uses. There does not
   appear to be much scope for generating new revenues from local
   communities under this policy, but income from fees could
   probably be increased in some schools.
   The formal conversion of new secondary to high schools could
   probably be accomplished fairly quickly, with sufficient
   external assistance to support physical rehabilitation and the
   provision of laboratories. libraries, and other facilities.
   Indeed, as has recently been demonstrated, it can be
   accomplished. Significant improvements in the quality of
   education provided in the new high schools (or protection of
   present quality standards in traditional high schools) will
   take considerably longer and will require more resources,
   however.
   Option'C - Improvement of All-Age Schools
   The priority under this policy is improvement in the quality of
   facilities and instruction provided in the all-age schools. in
   which more than 40 percent of students in Grades 7-9 are now
   enrolled. The aim is to inc~ease the resources available to
   the worst schools and the poorest students (who are often
   enrolled in the worst schools), with the ultimate objective of
   providing them with ed'ucational opportunities more nearly equal
   to those available to students in more favored schools. In the
   first instance this effort might develop along the lines now
   being pursued in St. Thomas Parish, where all-age schools are
   to be upgraded and consolidated, with some designated as junior
   high schools offering Grades 7-9 and others reverting to a
   status as primary schools offering Grades 1-6.




                                                              5-9 

Estimates of the costs of adopting this strategy are presented

in Table 5.3. The capital cost of reform is very high. because 

of the large quantities of resources that will be needed to 

refurbish. expand. and equip the all-age schools. The 

incremental recurrent cost is relatively low, however; the main 

component is the salaries of the new teachers needed to reduce 

the pupil/teacher ratio in these schools. 

  In addition to the minimal reform costs identified under the
  first strategy described above, the costs associated with this
  strategy include the cost of constructing or expanding
  "central" all-age schools and refurbishing "feeder" primary
  schools. As in the project now underway in St. Thomas. this
  will, in most cases, require identifying centrally located
  all-age schools and expanding and equipping them to serve as
  junior high-schools. with secondary departments offering Grades   .,
  7-9. In some areas. new schools may have to be constructed.
  Other all-age schools in the vicinity of the junior high
  schools will serve as primary "feeder" schools, offering Grades   .,
  1-6. It is unlikely that many new primary schools will have to
  be constructed, but many are in need of extensive
  refurbishing. If the St. Thomas project can serve as a guide
  to the capital cost of these improvements, iSland-wide reforms
  of this kind will require a lot of new resources. although it
  must be noted that those costs included hurricane damage.
- Insofar as schools in other parts of the island require less in
  the way of rehabilitation or equipment. however. the capital
  cost of reform will be reduced. The estimates of capital costs
  in Table 5.3 and Table 5.4 shOUld. therefore. be regarded as
  estimates of the maximum quantity of resources needed to carry
 out the proposed reforms.
Teachers who are now teaching Grade 7-9 in all-age schools are 

commonly trained as primary rather than secondary teachers. and 

so will require extensive in-service training to prepare them 

for effective teaching in junior high programmes. Most of 

these teachers have been trained as class rather than subject 

teachers. and many are not prepared to assume roles as subject 

specialists. These will require more extensive upgrading of 

their qualifications than can be accomplished in brief 

in-service courses. and so will have to be provided with 

longer-term training opportunities-. More generally. the first 

requirement for improving the quality of all-age schools and 

making them more nearly equal to high schools is a significant 

decrease in the pupil/teacher ratio. which means that reform 

will require large numbers of new teachers. Recruiting, 

training. and retaining these teachers will make reform along 

these lines very costly, especially in light of the present 

shortage of trained teachers and the prospect of rising rates 

of enrollment in Grades 7-9 as the quality of the all-age 

schools is improved.

5-12
·. 


   There are some all-age schools in remote areas that lie beyond
   the reach of any rational program of school consolidation,
   because their students would have to travel too far to reach
   central schools. These schools will Lemain all-age schools
   offering Grades 1-9 for the foreseeable future, but if their
   students are to share in the benefits of reform some provision
   will have to be made for improving the quality of instruction
   in Grades 7-9 through distance education. For studfnts at this
   level radio is likely to prove more effective than alternative
   approaches (e.g. correspondence), and the costs of this
   approach to reform therefore include the costs of producing and
   disseminating radio programs, and of training and equipping
   teachers to make effective use of them.
  The goals of secondary school reform include equalization of
  educational opportunities as well as improvement in the
  education provided to the worst off, and increasing levels of
  funding for all-age schools will consequently be accompanied by
  some decr-ease (or at least a relative decline) in the real 
  level of funding provided for high schools, because of the
  higher rate of growth in the resources provided to the all-age
  schools. Decreasing levels of public support for high schools,
  possibly through a freeze in public subsidies for one or more
  years, need not be accompanied by a decline in the quality of
  facilities or instruction that they offer, however: the loss 'of
  revenue might for example be compensated to some degree by
  increasing fees for those students who are able to pay.
  It is likely that additional resources for the upgrading of
  all-age schools can be mobilized in the communities where the
  schools are located, though government assistance cannot be
  made contingent on the provision of local revenues. One
  mechanism through which this might be accomplished would be the
  creation of a Facilities Equalization Fund, which would
  dispense supplementary matching grants for school improvement
  to communities, through local school boards, that produced
  corresponding contributions in cash or in kind. In contrast to
  other secondary schools, all-age schools draw their students
  from clearly bounded communities. but the current perception of
  these schools as dumping grounds for CEE "failures" undermines
  community identification with them~ It is therefore possible
  that a clear commitment by the government to the upgrading of
  the all-age schools would begin to foster a sense of community
  responsibility and support for them, which would produce some
  local resources for the upgrading and improvement of specific
  schools.




                                                             5-13 

                                                                  '. 


The main advantage of this approach to reform is that it
directs attention and resources to raising the quality of
education received by those who are worst served by the present
system of secondary education, and who are most in need of
additional resources. Over time improvements in the quality of
all-age schools should increase both efficiency and equity in
secondary schools, by reducing drop-out rates. raising literacy
rates, and increasing the employability of system graduates;
and by bringing about a more equitable distribution of public
subsidies within the system.
This strategy also has a number of disadvantages. Most
significantly, it leaves selectivity intact as the organizing
principle of the secondary school system, though it ameliorates
some of the worst effects of selection by increasing the
resources and improving the education provided to the system's
"failures." Beyond this, the shift of resources to all-age
schools, even if only on a relative basis. is likely to lead to
a perceived "watering down" of quality in high 'schools, which
will not be greeted with equanimity by those who attend. work
in, or otherwise identify with traditionally favored
institutions. The political problem here is a more general
one: reform efforts focused specifically on all-age schools
are likely to encounter resistance from those associated with
high-prestige schools, which may undermine not only policies
aimed at equalizing resources and opportunities within the
system. but the very idea of a common curriculum for Grades 7-9
as well. This represents a very strong argument in favor of a
comprehensive approach to secondary school reform. rather than
an approach targeted to specific schools or sectors within the
system.
Option D - Extension of Primary School                                   . '" ~
This alternative would entail the extension of the primary
school to eight grades. or possibly nine, to ~e followed by
three years of secondary schooling. The secondary school might.
be extended to five years for specialized programs of study.
including preparation for 'A' levels: Under this alternative.
selection would take place at the end of Grade 8 or 9 for
admission to diverse programs in academic. technical. and
vocational high schools.
                                                                           -,
                                                                            ,
This strategy addresses the c~ntral issues in the reform of
secondary education directly and comprehensively. Advantages
include the fact that it postpones selection. allows sUfficient
time in the primary school curriculum for remediation for
students who need it, and also makes possible additional time
for completion of CXC curricula in secondary schools.




5-1~
·   ,




        The policy builds on existing structures and facilities, rather
        than requiring the creation of new institutions. It therefore
        promises improvement in the quality and coherence of primary
        schooling, and perhaps in the quality of secDndary schooling as
        well.
        The main disadvantage of this approach to reform is the nearly
        unanimous professional opinion tha~ adolescent children should
        not be retained in primary school, for both pedagogical and
        developmental reasons. Children of these ages have unique
        needs that are best met in their peer groups. A second
        disadvantage is the administrative and organizational scope of
        the reform, which would require changes of some kind -- either
        expansion or contraction -- not only in secondary schools but
        in nearly every school in Jamaica. A further disadvantage is
        that reform along these lines is likely to create strong,
        inexorable and probably undesirable pressures for the expansion
        of enrollments in secondary schools, and/or for the extension
        of the standard secondary curriculum past Grade 11. If the
        primary school is extended to eight years with selection at the
        end of Grade S, for example. there will be pressure to keep
        students in school at least through age 14. Alternatively, if
        the primary school curriculum is extended to Grade 9, pressures
        will emerge for a standard three-year secondary curriculum to
        allow adequate time for CXC and vocational preparation. Either
        way. enrollments and costs would rise significantly, not only
        in primary schools but to an ev~n greater extent in secondary
        schools. Additional pressures for expansion at the secondary
        level will be created by the fact that keeping students in
        primary school for two additional years will reduce present
        enrollment levels in many secondary schools, which school
        administrators and parents will naturally wish to fill.
        Because of these objections, no estimates of the cost of this
        alternative have been prepared. They would include the cost of
        expanding and upgrading facilities in primary schools which now
        offer only Grades 1-6. and expanding and upgrading facilities
        in those secondary schools that do not have facilities for
        students in Grades 7-9. in order to accommodate the new
        distribution of students. Recruiting and training new teachers
        would also be expensive. and especially so insofar as the
        reform leads to enrollment expansion at the secondary level.
        As with the creation of junior high schools, however. it is
        possible Chat some of these costs might be borne by local
        communities. as primary schools are community based. A further
        obstacle to the implementation of this policy is the loss of
        grades 7-S in high and new secondary schools. which will
        reduce their enrollments and probably increase their unit
        costs. thus placing pressure on budgets elsewhere in the
        educational system.



                                                                  5-15 

                                                                  .. 



E.     Creation of Junior High Schools and Departments
The alternative approaches to reform described above propose
improvements within constraints defined-by the present 
organization of the secondary school system. They leave the
main features of the system unchanged: profound inequalities
between high schools and other secondary schools ~nd pe'rvasive
emphasis on selection are likely to remain ir.definitely as
dominant characteristics of the system under either of these
policies. Insofar as the present reform aims to reduce                   ...
inequalities or limit the salience of selection within the
system. a more comprehensive approach to reform is
recomm·ended. This option is a strategy for the comprehensive
rationalization of secondary education in Jamaica.
Differentiated programmes in Grades 7-9 must be abolished. and
a new class of school. to be called the junior-highschool.               ·     1
offering the junior-high school curriculum must be created. At
the same time. the junior-high school curriculum. (the common
curriculum). must be offered in traditional and technical high
schools. which would then have junior and senior-high
departments. Reference has already been made (see Chapter Z)
to similar structural and curricular reforms based on the
comprehensive principle in countries like England. Sweden and
Finland. In Sweden, for example, the system for Grades 1-9 is
divided into three levels viz. Junior (1-3), Intermediate (4-6)
and a Senior level (7-9). Pupils attend th~ same school
through the junior and intermediate levels. but the transition
to the Grades 7-9 level often means a change of school. With a
Junior-High School system. a similar situation would obtain in
Jamaica where students would attend a primary level school for
grades 1-6 and a junior-high programme in the nearest available
school. Junior-high schools or departments would admit
students on the basis of geographic residence. with all
students attending designated schools on the basis of a "free
~low" from Grade 6 to Grade 7.  Curriculum, facilities. and
expenditures would be as nearly equal as possible across
schools. with selection for differentiated academic and
vocational institutions postponed to ttie end ~f Grade 9. Some
curricular differentiation would have to take place within
schools. however. in order to address the variable requirements
of students in need of remediation. for example. or of those
preparing for the CXC exams.                                                 .',

Under this alternative all high schools (including vocational            ....
                                                                         :

and technical high schools) would include senior secondary
programmes for students in Grades 10-11. with some offering
academic and others both academic and technical or vocational
programs. Many new secondary schools would continue
diversified senior programmes and in time become complete high
schools, or junior-highs only though a few more could be


5-16
converted to senior-technical-hiqh schools. Workshops and
other vocational traininq facilities could be consolidated in
centrally located technical-hiqh senior schools. which would
offer specialized proqrams in particular trades (e.q. auto
mechanics. or carpentry) rather than a-full array of vocational
courses. This would allow both a rationalization of
expenditure and the upqradinq of traininq in specific skill
areas.
The advantaqe of this junior-hiqh alternative is that it
directly and comprehensively addresses each of the central
objectives of the proposed secondary school reform. Selection
of students for differentiated opportunities is postponed until
the end of Grade 9. This will ensure that all students enjoy
equal access to the new curriculum. by reducinq the drop-out
rate and keepinq more children in school throuqh the completion
of junior-hiqh school. The creation of a new class of schools.
ultimately with a common curriculum and similar instructional
and other facilitie5. will ensure a qreater deqree of equality
in the distribution of educational resources and in the quality
of education provided across schools than is possible in the
present, hiqhly stratified system.
The disadvantaqe of this approach to reform is the maqnitude
and administrative complexity of the chanqes that would be
required to implement the policy and the effort it will require
in teacher upqradinq and supervision/in-service. While some
capital expenditure will be required for renovatinq senior
technical facilities, and providinq secondary type facilities
in the all-aqe schools, the major expenditure will have to be
made on ensurinq that the quality of teachinq is uniform across
the junior-hiqh proqramme in all schools. Chanqes this
sweepinq would be likely to encounter political resistance from
many quarters. both inside and outside the educational system.
and may be beyond the p~esent administrative capacity of the
Ministry of Education, unless reqional offices and headquarters
reorqanization are accomplished in the near future.
Estimates of the cost of this reform strateqy are presented in
Table 5.4. In qeneral, they ~re similar to the estimated costs
of the precedinq alternative, but the ultimate capital costs of
creatinq a common junior-hiqh school proqramme are
siqnificantly hiqher than the costs of improvinq all-aqe
schools, mainly because of the assumption that postponinq
selection to the end of Grade 9 and improvinq the quality of
instruction in Grades 7-9 will increase the percentaqe of
students completinq junior-hiqh school. The opportunities that
comprehensive reform provides for redeployinq teachers and
students amonq schools within the system. however. makes the
recurrent cost of comprehensive reform lower than that of the
strateqy focused solely on the all-aqe schools.


                                                          5-17
                                                                   ·,




 The implementation of reforms on this scale would encounter a
 variety of obstacles The pressure to convert all the new
 secondary schools into high schools at the senior level would     '"';
                                                                   ;
                                                                   :     I
                                                                   ,--, ..J
 be strong while costs would be high. Tiaditional high schools
 will pot welcome their loss of control over entry of students.
                                                                   ,
                                                                         ,
                                                                         ,
One consequence )f this resistance could be some loss of
clientele f:om public to private schools, and possibly some
reversion of the most prestigious high schools to the private
sector as well. While such developments are not entirely
desirable, they would nevertheless have the positive effect of
removing some schools and students from public payroll, thus       t'


freeing public resources for use in schools where they are more
urgently needed. In addition, the fact that junior-high
schools would serve students in the local community would
increase the possibility of calling upon community resources
for their support, thus freeing an additional quantity of
government funds for other uses.
  Despite these disadvantages, the study team strongly supports
  and recommends this option because of the potential it offers
  for addressing each of the chronic problems of the educational
  system. The implications which this options has for Grades
  10-13 are outlined below. The last two remaining chapters on
  teacher education and staff development and on Implementation
. Strategies assume that this option will be adopted.
Implications of the Junior High option for Grades 10-13
The following kinds of changes would be made to existing 

secondary level schools: 

1.     Traditional High Schools
                                                                    .'·l
                                                                   ":.:'.;
a. Grades 7-9 in all high schools would be designated 

junior-high departments. 

b. Grades 10-11 of high schools would be designated 

senior-high departments. This department would have a 

comprehensive programme offering subjects to the CXC level. 

c. Grades 12-13 would be clustered in central locations for 

teaching. purposes and would persist as an extension of the 

senior department in some schools. The grades 12-13 wouid 

offer an advanced level academic and business programme. 





5-18
2.  Comprehensive High Schools
These schools could be converted to high schools with the same
structure and programmes as outlined above and with the
possibility of some technical/agricultural studies dependent on
geography, local needs, funding and the national technical
system requirements (see Supplement).
3.  Technical/Agricultural High Schools
The  existing technical and agricultural high s~hools would be
transformed into senior-high schools, Grades 10-11. offering
specializations in these subjects. There might ultimately be
specialized academic senior-high schools also in the
specialized Technical or Agricultural High Schools might have
concern junior-high departments. depending on accommodations
and geography.
4.  New Secondary Schools
Some new secondary schools will become high schools. offering
junior-high and senior-high programmes. Others will become
junior-high or senior-high schools. In the short term. some
will remain new secondary schools with junior high departments
until they are converted at the senior level.
S.  All-Age Schools
The grades 7-9 of some all-age schools will become junior-high
departments. It is envisaged that many all-age schools
(grades 1-9) in isolated areas will remain. Even these schools
should offer the common junior-high curriculum. with improved
facilities and resources. and perhaps some distance education
for individual students.
The mix and location of schools as described above will depend
on the data from the school mapping project now nearing
completion in the ministry of education. In the long term, the
goal for the mix of schools at the secondary level should be:
a.  High schools   (~rades   7-11 or 13)
b.  Junior-high schools (Grades     7~9)

c.  Senior-high schools (including
    senior-~echnical/agricultural-high     schools) Grades 10-11 or
   Grades 10-13.
d.  Primary Junior-high schools in areas where numbers do not
    justify separate junior-high schools.



                                                              5-19 

                                                                  .. 



V.     FINANCING EDUCATIONAL REFORM
Both the capital and recurrent costs of adopting any of the
four preceding strategies for educational reform will be high.
but the full burden of these costs need not be borne out of the
Ministry of Education's budget. It is likely. for example.
that much of the funding needed to pay the capital costs
entailed by reform can be obtained from foreign donors. and
donor resources may help to defray some recurrent costs as
well. at least in the short run.
It should also be recognized. that not all of the local
resources needed for educational reform can or will necessarily
come from the Ministry of Education's annual budget
allocation. To produce the requisite quantity of revenue it
may be necessary to call on funds from other sources. These                 ;
                                                                            i
                                                                         . .i
would in the first analysis include the savings to be realized
by cutting back on current levels of subsidy or service in some
areas: and the generation of new resources through the
mobilization of increased levels of community and household
support.
Savings from current expenditures that could be used toward the
accomplishment of secondary education reform might come from
the gradual phase-out of vocational and pre-vocational programs
in Grades 7-9. with a concomitant decline" in the resources
needed for materials and the maintenance of workshop
facilities. Additional savings might come from the phase-out
of boarding facilities.

5-22
Chapter 6
TEACHER EDUCATION AND STAFF   DEVELOPME~T



The success of the reforms recomme~ded in this study will
depend on in-service staff development for teachers. education
officers and other people in the system. and on appropriate
changes in pre-service· teacher education. In addition.
physical and material pre-conditions for the successful
implementation of the common Grades 7-9 curriculum in all
schools need to be established. In this chapter, we first
determine these pre-conditions by examining the features of the
existing situation. Requirements for teacher education and
staff development are then discussed.
A.   Pre-conditions for the success of the curriculum
Chapter 1 described the poor physical facilities in all-age
schools and some new secondary schools, the virtual absence of
instructional materials ·and resources in most schools, and the
grade-level organization which exists in the upper grades of
all-age schools. Achievement levels of students in the all-age
and the new secondary schools indicated a need for extensive
remediation as well as guidance and counselling. Three major
thrusts are needed if progress toward a common curriculum and
equality of educational opportunity can be made. These are
equalization of resources, improving the ability of all
students to benefit from instruction. and improving the quality
of teaching and learning.
Equalization of resources
In order for a common curriculum to be implemented to the same
degree in all types of schools. efforts have to be made to
equalize the physical facilities and resources and to improve
the quantity and quality of. instructional resources and
materials.
This requires:
     o   extensive physical upgrading of all-age schools.
     o   individual classrooms in all-age schools.
     o   more classrooms to reduce the teacher/pupil ratio.
     o   staff rooms where needed in all-age schools.



                                                            6-1 

                        .   11




                                                                   .,

Maximizing the ability of all students to benefit from
Instruction                                                               ,   ,

The notion of equity should include not just the existence of a
common curriculum. but an equal or equivalent instructional
environment as far as this is possible. In order for the
schools to plan for and work toward equalizing instruction. the
following are necessary:
      o   Remediation of all students who need it in
          Grades 7-9. This will include remedial reading and            ."    '.

          the implementation of remedial strategies in the
          all-age school and in the new secondary and high
          schools as well. One approach is to devote all or
          nearly all Language Arts periods to remedial reading
          for those students who need this remediation.
          Additional teachers with a special orientation will be
          required to address this need.
      o   Development and use of diagnostic tests in the schools
          but especially in the all-age school.
      o   The establishment of special classes in the primary
          schools for remediation. This would be a temporary
          measure until the reforms of the primary (Grades 1-6)         r'

          level are put in place.
      o   Removal of the local discretion allowed schools in the
          time allocated to subjects on the curriculum.
      o   Provision for guidance and counselling of all-age
          school students: the only type of school where
          guidance




6-2
·. 


            and counselling is non~existent. There is evidence
            that this group of students have limited parental
            guidance. experience severe conflicts in parent-child
            interaction. are lacking in knowledge of human
            sexuality and boy-girl relationships (one reason for
            high-drop-out rate among girls). and are ignorant of
            possible careers and their requirement.
       o    Preparation of curricular materials that will allow
            students to progress at their own rate. This may be
            in the form of workbooks and other instructional
            materials. audio-visual materials. academic games and
            puzzles or alternative textbooks.
   (Instructional organizations such as these will allow the slow
   learner to learn at his/her own pace without becoming
   demotivated. and the mor~ able to progress and not be kept
   behtnd. This will also. in part. address the range of ability
   which now exists in the schools).
       o    Provide basic necessities for schooling which poorer
            parents are unable to provide (e.g. costs of
            transportation. uniforms. etc.). This could reduce
            the high drop-out rate among low income students.
   Improving the guality of teaching and learning
   Equalizing resources and maximizing students' ability to
   benefi~ from instruction are necessary but not sufficient for
   reform. One has to improve the teaching/learning process as
   well. The following are needed:
       o    Adoption of alternative teaching strategies. These
            strategies are needed not only to be consistent with
            the new curriculum materials, but also to provide
            substitutes for those teaching methods now in use
            which fail to enhance student learning. This problem
            is particularly acute in the all-age schools. Use of
            individualization. e.g. grouping, and mixed ability
            teaching. These are necessary even when classes are
            streamed.
       o    Improvement in the social/emotional climate of all-age.
            schools and possibly new secondary classrooms.
       o   Use of teacher-made classroom tests. Such tests will
           become an essential part of the curriculum if
           materials are designed so that students work at their
           own pace.




                                                             6-3 

                                                                      .. 




      o    Upgrading of the content knowledge of all-age and 

           possibly new secondary school teachers. This is 

           necessary in all subjects as a~preparation for subject 

           teaching, but is especially critical in Maths and 

           Science. 

      o    ~stallishment of some resource centres for teachers in
           6trategic locations throughout the island. These
           centres would provide professional reading for                        ,       .
                                                                             ,'
           teachers, resource materials for the teaching of                  ."
           school subjects, and advice in the preparation of
           visual aids and other instructional materials. Twenty
           of these regional resource centres have been planned
           for in a previous project. This reform can provide
           the secondary-level materials for these resource
           centres.
B.    Teacher Supply, Teacher Education and Staff Development
 Effort in these three areas must take into account the
 characteristics of teachers now in the system, the existing
 teacher education programme. and the demands of the new
 curriculum. The significant teacher characteristics described
 in Chapter 1 are as follows: in the all-age school. and                     . 1

 possibly in the new secondary schools, the majority of
'Grades 7-9 teachers are trained for primary level teaching and
 more than one-half are lacking in sufficient knowledge to teach
 Maths and Science effectively. At the High School level. about
 40\ are university graduates. but 16\ have no professional
 training. There is a high attrition rate among all groups.
1.    Supply of teachers and other personnel
                                                                             .' ,
With a decrease in the pupil-teach~ ratio and the introduction
of some subject teaching, more teachers will be needed for the .
all-age schools. The candidate pool, however. is not large.
Many teachers' colleges. in fact. have difficulty filling the                ,       l

first year places. Furthermore. the all-age school is the last
choice of the teachers' college graduate. The following can be
done in the short term:
      o    Invite retired teachers to return to the classroom for
          ·2-3 years. Such teachers would get regular pay. and
           pension. They would also be required to undergo any
           required in-service courses.
      o   Review teachers' salaries to make them more                        :-'

          competitive (this will be discussed further under
          teachers' careers).




6-4
·. 



        o    Provide more resources for and expand the preliminary
             programme in the teach~rs' colleges. This will
             increase the number of candidates for teaching.
        o    Investigate the effects of teacher vacation leave
             policies on staffing.
   There will also be a need for more guidance and counselling in
   all-age schools. A guidance counsellor would have a reduced
   teaching load. Or in urban centres, a guidance counsellor can
   be assigned to more than one school. The guidance counsellor
   can also be responsible for teaching aspects of the Personal
   Development course.
   With the planned expansion of the corps of territorial
   education officers (T.E.Os), more persons will be needed for
   these positions. These persons can also be recruited in the
   short term from retired teachers and principals.
   Training of personnel
   An increase in the demand for guidance and counselling requires
   preparation of these personnel. This preparation can be done
   at the UWI's Faculty of Education, if resources are provided to
   support such a programme. TEO's can benefit from the Faculty
   of Education's B.Ed programme which has units in supervision
   and Leadership. Administration and Curriculum. The curriculum
   of these units should be reviewed periodically with the members
   of the supervision unit of the Ministry of Education.
   2.   Teacher Education - Pre-Service
   since the vast majority of the teachers at the Grades 7-9 level
   ere prepared in the teachers' colleges, attention should be
   paid to the pre-service programmes of the colleges. The
   existing teacher education curriculum should be modified to
   include the following:
        o   Knowledge of the new Grades     7-9   curriculum.
        o   Remedial   ~eaching   strategies.
        o    Instruction in remedial reading for all teachers.
        o   The teaching of Aesthetics at the secondary level.
        o   The teaching of Technology Studies at the secondary
            level.

C.     School-Based    teache~       development

This app~oach to staff development is t~eated unde~ th~ee main
a~eas:  supervision by Te~~ito~ial Education Office~s. (TEOs).
school-based teache~ improvement. and induction.

       i)   Supe~vision       by   Te~~ito~ial     Education   Office~s    (TEOs)

            TEOs will play a c~itical ~ole in the actual
            implementation 6f the new cu~~iculum. ~nd the
            maintenance of teachinq and cu~~icula~ standa~ds over
            time. Existinq and new TEOs will need to be t~ained.
            Wo~kshops can be planned coope~atively between the




                                                                              6-7 

            Ministry and U.W.1. Familiarity with the .IDB
            workshops should be a-requirement. Workshops should
            include the following topics:'
                The nature. and models. of teacher development
                The role of the supervisor - curriculum and               I
                administration                                       :..~ I

                The philosophy of the curriculum
                Coaching and supervision

                Continuous assessment

                School management                                    ./

                School-board relationships
TEOs should have experience in teaching. preferably at both the
primary and secondary levels and be familiar with the
curriculum. Since TEOs will likely be subject specialists by
background. the deployment of secondary level TEOs may be
different from that of primary TEOs. TEOs may also benefit
from some of the teachers' workshops.
                                                                     .   ,~



      ii)   School-based teacher improvement                         . l



            In addition to workshops and supervision by TEOs. it
            is important to build a capacity within the school
            itself for continuing teacher improvement and
            experimentation. In this regard. the principal plays
            a key role. He or she will be the one to set norms
            and expectations regarding quality of teaching. use of
            time. testing. and interaction with students and
            parents. The principal will provide opportunities for
            teachers to plan. observe. share with each other. and
            will recognize. acknowledge and capitalize on
            teachers' strengths. Such a role is possible if the
            principal is relieved of fund raising and/or if the
            vice-principal has a reduced teaching load (not often
            the case in the all-age school). The librarian can
            also plan an important role in this process; he/she
            will have to be oriented to this new responsibility.
            School-based teacher development will require some
            training for principals and librarians. Workshops on
            school-based in-service can be organized for those
            principals and vice-principals who have not
            participated in the IDB workshops. Principals will
            also require some help and guidance in organizing for
            subject teaching. This may be provided by the TEOs
            and may not require formal workshops.




6-8
·. 


            In light of the global recession facing all developing
            countries. staff development through supervision by
            TEOs must be shared.' Consequently. a school-based
            approach should be adopted. and. as the literature on
            staff development suggests. may be most effective in
            bringing about teacher change.

       iii) Induction

            The importance of the first year or two of teaching is
            increasingly being recognized. The teacher picks up
            habits and dispositions. good or bad. which persist
            over time and influence teaching.  It is critical that
            the school intervenes to set expectations but also to
            help the new teacher to adjust to the curriculum. to
            the school and the students. This adjustment will be
            of special importance in cases where the new teacher
            is not trained for the secondary level. Planning for
            induction also requires careful thought to teaching
            load and deployment. Building the capacity for
            on-going induction is an essential part of
            school-based teacher improvement. but merits separate
            attention.  Induction can be the joint responsibility
            of the principal and the TEO.

   Teachers' Careers

   Teaching has traditionally been a respectable occupation in
   Jamaican society. There are distinctions in the teaching
   force. historical in origin, which are related to
   socio-economic background and type of school. Teachers in the
   primary/all-age and new secondary schools have traditionally
   been the children of the agricultural and rural classes and
   have received their education in the teachers' colleges. High
   school teachers have. up till the 1970s. been recruited mainly
   from the middle classes. and have had 'A' level or university
   qualifications.  This is now changing. Today. more than half
   of high school teachers have teachers' college training.

  There is a fairly high attrition rate among all teachers. Many
  use teaching as a means of upward social mobility. Graduate
  teachers in the high schools are easily recruited into the
  private sector in a society where there is a shortage of
  personnel with such qualifications.  Salary, status and working
  conditions are powerful factors pushing teachers from the
  profession. for though teaching is respected. it has relatively
  little status in a society which has become more materialistic
  than in the past. Furthermore, the escalating cost in recent




                                                             6-9 

years of basic necessities such as housing and transportation.

have forced some dedicated teachers to switch careers. or to          ,.. ; 

                                                                      · .:
moonlight. in order to survive economi~ally. Today. reports
from high schools are that very few students wish to ctioose          · .1
teaching as a career.
Proposals for the future
There are tqree issues which need to be addressed in qny
reform - teachers' qualifications. teacher supply and teachers'       ·      ,
salaries.
Teachers' Qualifications
If a common curriculum is to be implemented in all types of
schools. there must be teachers who are more or less equally
qualified. The present inequities and inadequacies can be
addressed in the following ways in the short term:
       1.    Ensure that teachers in the all-age and new secondary
             schools are trained for the secondary level. Those       .,.. :
             80\ in the all-age schools and the unknown number in
             the new secondary schools who have primary level
             training can be included in the present                  · .j
             post-certificate programme now administered by the
             JBTE. This programme was originally intended to
             upgrade teachers with a certificate to a diploma level
             within their original specialization - primary or
             secondary. But if funding is provided. the programme
             can be extended to include this group of teachers.
                                                                      ·      ,
       2.    Provide professional training for the graduates and
             pre-trained teachers in the high schools.
             approximately 450. The scholarships made available
             under the proposed Human Resources Development
             programme, will make 200 of these university graduates   :: 1
             professionally trained. The remaining 250 can be
             trained in a programme carried out by a few of the
             teachers' colleges in conjunction with the Faculty of
             Education. Placing the programme in the teachers'
             colleges is more desirable than at the Faculty.
             because more teachers would be trained in a shorter
             period of time and the unit cost would be less.
             Additional funding would be required for this
             programme.                                               ..'"   .
             In the longer term. the aim should be to have a
             secondary teaching force that is mainly graduate         '. .~
             trained. This is justified on two counts - it would




6-10
·. 


            be one way of equalizing resources and it would
            improve the content Knowledge of teachers.   One
            strategy to achieve this end is to supplement the
            existing BA/BSc and Dip Ed. route of teacher education
            with a 3-year B.Ed. which would require more ~ontent
            Knowledge than the existing 2-year B.Ed. now offered
            by the Faculty of Education.   Because of the large
            numbers involved. one might consider placing this
            programme in those teachers' colleges. which have the
            facilities and the staff with the requisite
            qualifications. The Faculty of Education would be
            associated with the programme.   This represents a more
            long term goal. Teachers with a Diploma in Teaching
            have matriculation for the second year of this 3-year
            B.Ed. degree. These teachers should be encouraged to
            complete this degree.

   Teacher Supply

   In order to ensure an adequate supply of teachers in the short
   and long term. it is essential that the number of high school
   places b. expanded. The existing structure with limited places
   in the high school and limited access creates a bottle neCK
   that is very restrictive.   The result is that there are not
   enough high school graduates to satisfy the demands of the
   commercial sector and provide candidates for tertiary and
   higher education.  If these high school places are expanded,
   there would be more candidates for teacher education and less
   need for industry to recruit from the teaching profession. The
   programme of upgrading of the senior cycle of some new
   secondary schools should- continue.

   Teachers' Salaries and Teacher Retention

   Despite its function as-a means of upward social mobility.
   teaching attracts persons who have some core beliefs~ values
   and preferences. When aSKed why they chose teaching. Jamaican
   teachers cite the opportunity for interacting with and
   influencing children, and their admiration for teachers. But
   the opportunity for further training is also an attraction.
   Teaching, therefore. has some special features which attract
   and can potentially sustain and satisfy teachers. Job
   satisfaction. however,' results not just from the profession'S
   inherent characteristics, but also from the quality of the worK
   environment, and the remuneration and rewards which teachers
   get for their worK.




                                                             6-11 

                                                                    .~,       .



The first necessity to ensure retention is a salary that is
competitive with that of other 'occupations requiring comparable
qualifications. The salary scale should be extended enough to
offer motivation to those who remain in the profession for a
long time. Secondly, the teaching profession could be made
more differentiated in function so that more teachers can
aspire to prorr?tion.
Good working conditions include a reasonable pupil-teacher
ratio, decent surroundings in which to work. and support for
teaching in the form of materials, textbooks, and proper
subventions. Good working conditions also include a workplace
where one can become intellectually involved in teaching and
can learn from teaching. Teachers have a need for support           " .I


within their school and for opportunities to talk about what
they do and how they can do it better. They have a need to
continue learning within the schoof environment. supervision
by the Ministry is necessary, based on enlightened policies
regarding teacher development on the job to make teaching more
exciting and interesting, and ultimately, to encourage good
teachers to remain in the classroom.

Strategies for implementing programmes for teacher education
and staff development assume the following:
       o   that ther·e will be at least a lO-year implementation
           period for this project
       o   that for this phase of the project, in the provision
           of additional staff to all-age schools, emphasis will
           be placed on the larger all-age schools, i.e. those
           with an enrollment of over 500. However, school-based
           staff development and teacher improvement will be
           targeted to all schools
                                                                    -
       o   increases in some staff (e.g. guidance counsellors)      ~.'

           will take place on a phased ba~is because of a
           shortage of existing human resources and a need for
           training
       o   all initial expenses and investment will be considered
           as capital expenses. Any expenses incurred aft'er the
           initial year, will be considered recurrent. However,
           all workshop expenditures are considered capital
           expenses                                                 .     ,

            account.                    I

   $5.000.00. and then $6.500.00 per annum for materials
   allowance. Textbooks and materials for remedial classes and
   for needy students .in all-age schools are included in the total
   costing.

Chapter 7
IMPLEMENTATION STRATEGIES


If there is to be genuine equality of oppo~tunlty for most of
the people of Jamaica. then the present system must be
restructured around more equitable patterns and practices in
the schools. The major problem facing those in Jamaica who
wish to effect reform. is how to gradually restructure the
ideas that people have about the processes and outcomes of
education.

The previous chapters in this study. and those in Volume II.
describe the schools and the system. analyze the problems. and
propose possible solutions.

This final chapter is intended to assist those responsible for
providing leadership in secondary education to manage the
change process - the Ministers and senior Ministry of Education
officials in particular. The suggestions are designed to be
consistent with the Study of School Administration of the World
Bank IV project and the UNESCO Report of 1983.

Those reports and this study are based on the same assumptions
about organization. supervision and curriculum. The goal is a
coherent, efficient Ministry with a small coordinating central
office and functional. integrated regional offices. A common
element in both the administration study and in this secondary
reform study is the emphasis given to the restructuring,
training and management of a corps of territorial education
officers.



A practical plan for the reform of secondary education in
Jamaica will almost certainly bring together elements from the
policies and plans described in previous chapters of this
study. No matter what kind of reform policy is finally
developed. however. the implementation of secondary school
reform will require careful planning and the coordination of
simultaneous activities throughout the school system.

A key element of secondary reform, therefore. is that the
planning and coordinating capacities o~ the Ministry of
Education must be strengthened if reform is to succeed.

A concerted effort to bring this about is now clearly underway.
through improved staffing. domestic and foreign training for
staff members. and the integration of computers into the work
of the Planning and StatisticsUnits. ' At present. planning in
the ministry and in the principal aid agencies remains hampered
by the deficiency of accurate and timely information. The
uncertainty that is produced by this deficiency of data is
sometimes compounded by ~ lack of consistent coordination and
communication among projects and agencies.

Comprehensive reform will be more feasible when the present
strengthening of the data-gathering and analysis capabilities
of the Ministry is further advanced and as the distinct but
related reform efforts sponsored by various agencies are
brought into closer coordination. The presence of a stronq.
efficient corps of territorial education officers is essential
to any such improvement.

Progress toward several of the goals of secondary school reform
has already been made under the auspices of a number of
different projects. The curriculum for Grades' 7-9 is being
studied by two separate teams sponsored by the IBRD (this study
and the All Age Schools St. Thomas pilot). while the existing
curriculum for teacher education is being reviewed and
standardized by the Joint Board for Teacher Education.
Planning for the extension of the Textbook Rental Scheme to
all-age schools is meanwhile being undertaken by the ODA. the
original sponsors of the scheme: and research in the schools
for students in need of remediation is being undertaken.  The
development of instruments for the National Assessment
Programme for students in the primary grades is well underway.

While the need for improvement in each of these areas is
pressing. the problem of coordination among the various
projects is a critical -issue. and failure to manage the
situation effectively would threaten to make the situation in
Jamaica's secondary' schools even more difficult than it is at
present.  In the absence of careful monitoring and supervision
by the Ministry. for example. (see Appendix A). it is possible
that the textbooks produced for the rental scheme. or the
teacher education curriculum being developed by the JBTE. will
be incompatible with the guidelines for the new common
curriculum that will be developed by the two IBRD teams.

The commitment shown by these groups in responding to critical
needs within the education system is strong. and the study team
is assured that coordination among their efforts is a high
priority within both the Planning Unit and the Projects
Division of the Ministry.




                                                          7-3 

                      *                *                *
 The nine elements of a reform strategy ~re here presented in
 order of the priority we assign to each in the improvement of
 the schools:

 1. The appointment and training of Territorial Education
 Officers are prerequisite to the e~entual success of all
 curricular. planning and other innovations.  2. The completion        .,
 of current efforts to strengthen the central Ministry's
 planning and coordination functions.  3. The adoption of new
 policies in the area of finance and 4. in political control.
 5. Development and adoption of a new common curriculum and 6.
 appropriate textbooks for all students.  7. Major restructuring
 and cutting back of examinations.  B. Provision of in-service
 training for all teachers and 9. instructional delivery at a
 distance for remote rural areas.                                      '"   ,"




 In essence. this summary list suggests that the implementation        .. 

                                                                        -   ,
 of a comprehensive reform of Jamaica's secondary schools will
 have to be managed over an extended period of time: incremental
 reform.
                                                                        -
                                                                            ,,
 Curricular and administrative changes must be phased in before
 changes in the organization and structure of the schools and
 the system can be implemented.  For example. the abolition of
 the Grade 6 selection examination (the Common Entrance) might
 occur only in the fifth or sixth year of reform. followed by
 the free flow of students to their neighborhood secondary
 schools the next year.

  The following sample    charts illustrate the nature of the
  planning that should    be undertaken by planners in the Ministry.
 with the Territorial     Education Officers. if effective
_ implementation is to    take place.  Cross-coordination among the
  charts is absolutely    imperative and is a major responsibility
  of the C.E.O. of the    school program division of the Ministry.

All such charts should be developed. of course, in a
cooperative planning style by central office Units and Regions.
with strong coordination by the CEO.

Each chart should become the framework for the long-range
planning of each Unit and Regional Office - and as the
programme budget framework.  Each chart should be used by the
Planning Unit as a costing tool for the preparation of project
plans for foreign aid.
