     The following comments may be of help in the preparation of
     planning charts for each of the 9 reform activities. "
         1.  The Appointment and Training bf Territorial Education
             Officers
              This is addressed in some depth in Appendix A. Steps
              have already been undertaken by the government to
              enhance the position within the civil service. A major
              effort to mount a training scheme for new appointees
              and retraining for incumbents should be undertaken.
              Continuous in-service related to policy. curriculum and
              administrative matters (see the proposed charts) should
              be included in the programme budget of the school
              programme division of the Ministry.
         2.  Strengthening of the Planning and Statistics Function
             of the Central Ministry
              In a decentralized Ministry of Education. the
              coordination of planning is more vital than ever. The
              central office must have adequate staff. training and
              communications and computer capability. "There must be
              a close working relationship between the Supervision
              Unit and the Planning Unit - see Appendix E.
         3.  The Adoption of New Policies in Finance
              The discrimination in the present subventions to
              schools must be replaced by a more equitable system.
              The basic policy should be that an equal amount of
              public money should be spent on each child while he or
              she is in the first nine years of school. Exceptions
              should be a matter of compassion for handicapped
              children, or for special and exceptional circumstances.
              such as remoteness or isolation. (see Appendix F)
         4.   The Adoption of New Policies in Political Control
              Appendix F includes not only suggestions for financial
              equalizat~n, but proposals for the inclusion of more
              people in the control of the schools. School boards
              must be given a greater voice in decisions about their
              own  school to accompany "the increased responsibility
              for financial support that they must assume in the
              years ahead. The assistance of the Territorial
              Education Officer will be vital in this regard. One
              safeguard to the integrity of a decentralized system is
              openness - public education must be conducted in
              public, especially with regard to financial matters.



                                                                  7-7 

                                                                      .
                                                                      ,
                                                                          '..




           The roles of officials. both elected and appointed. 

           must be a matter of public record. Minutes of meetings 

           and records of decisions must ~e available for 

           accountability to the public at annual meetings of the 

           community. 

      S.   Development and Adoption of a Common Curriculum
           Chapter 3 and Appendices Band C and Volume II deal
           with specifics of this fundamental issue. The
           production of the necessary curriculum guides must
           precede many of the changes proposed in this study.
           The planning and organizing within the Ministry and the
           schools must centre on the implementation of the common
           curriculum. This should be the main criterion that the
           CEO applies to her or his examination of the planning            .       ,
                                                                                    "



           charts produced by each Unit and Region of the                 ; :"1
                                                                                "




           Ministry; and of each annual plan produced by each 

           school and examined by the Territorial Education 

           Officer. 

      6.   Development and Adoption of Appropriate Textbooks
           The first need is for books for the all-age school
           students - see Appendix D. Until such time as all
           students at all levels of the primary and secondary            ,---,
           schools have suitable books. the present schemes should
           be maintained - the Primary Textbook Plan that involves
           the provision of cheap and locally-developed books for
           every student. and the Secondary Rental Scheme. which
           needs to be expanded and "localized" so that the
           relevance of books to the interests of the students and
           the requirements of the curriculum guides are ensured.
           The temptation at the primary level to approve 'better'
           books that are more expensive and thus out the reach of
           many students should be avoided.
      7.   Restructuring of Examinations
           The alternatives to t~e damage caused to students and 

           the school system by the present examinations are 

           discussed in Chapters 1. 2 and 3 and in some depth. in 

           Appendix G. . The costs alone of the present 

           overemphasis on examinations warrant serious attention 

           to this issue. 


                                                                          .         '




7-8
    8.     In-service Education.
           Chapter 6 outlines recommendations for the continuing
           education and upgrading of teachers and other
           categories of educators. as well as for the recruitment
           of new categories of staff. Please consult Figure 6.1
           (Chapter 6) for the details and the time frame for
           implementation of the plans for teacher education and
           staff development.

    9.     Distance Education

           In spite of administrative reorganization wiih Regional
           Offices and regular visits by T.E.O. 's, there will be
           schools that are too small and remote for the teachers
           and students to participate in opportunities available
           to others. The primary concern is the delivery of
           curricular opportunities to the students.   The Ministry
           will need to revive distance education projects by
           correspondence and radio.  For students and teachers of
           determination. these can be useful if not completely
           satisfactory substitutes for regular schooling.

                     *                 *
A STRATEGIC PLAN FOR IMPLEMENTING REFORM

All the nine elements of a reform strategy that are listed
above should be developed by Ministry officials into charts
like the samples included in the previous section. The charts
need to be organized into an overall plan of action for the
Ministry.  Such a basic critical path or strategic plan is
needed to guide Ministry officials and the school boards.   The
section that follows is intended only as a guide to the
thinking in this study. A feasible strategic plan should be
developed by the people concerned, using 'outside' advice as it
applies:  a simplified implementation strategy document, such
as the sample checklist form at the end of this section should
be prepared. approved by.the Minister of Education. and
distributed to Ministry officials, especially the Territorial
Education Officers. as their basic policy directive for the
next few years.

Progress   towards reform can be measured by using such a
document   as a continuing discussion item at annual general
meetings   of the Territorial Education Officers with all senior
Ministry   officials and the Minister(st of Education.




                                                             7-9 

With such a 'master control document'. the Ministry can take
better charge of policy setting. and planning. not only in
relation to the school boards. and othet educational
institutions and organizations in Jamaica. but to international
agencies and governments. Outside assistance can be
considerably more coherent and relevant in the presence of such        .       ,
a basic strategic plan that is known to all.

The following section departs from the usual form of
recommendations in a study such as this. What follows is a
compendium of policy and administrative suggestions for
implementing those proposals in this study that are maior
departures from traditional ways of conducting education in
Jamaica. These suggestions may be of help in interpreting. and
implementing. the fundamental philosophical changes that are
needed to reform the system. The comments are in the general
chronological sequence that must be followed in order to
provide for consistency in reform activities across the entire
school system.

initiatives that should be undertaken to help both educators
and the public make ready for change.

             reports were discussed. Several sessions in major 

             centres throughout Jamaica would be desirable.

            business. manufacturers arid agricultural

       In Conclusion

   This study was focussed on lower secondary education - Grades
   7. 8 and 9 - but the solutions to many of the problems in the
   schools of Jamaica can only be found in reform of the entire
   system.

   A series of reforms such as those described in the body of this
   study need to be made:  in governing policies. in management
   practices. in the organization of the system. and then. most
   importantly. in teaching practices. Only then can there be
   improvement in the quality of education.

   Reform is needed in the Ministry of Education itself:  in its
   internal organization and lts relationship with the schools. as
   well as its relationships with the Ministry of Finance and the
   Ministry of the Public Service.

   Reform is needed in the discriminating distribution of
   resources to the schools.

   Reform is needed in the examination system which as become
   disproportionately expensive and educationally unsound.

 provide for a nine-year curriculum for all students, so that

 balanced foundation of basic knowledge, skills and attitudes                             ..J
 for Jamaicans in the twenty-first century.

 ensure equitable teaching to thl students.

 Reform is needed in the teaching methods in many of the
 classrooms, with each teacher supported by a renewal
 administrative system at the school, regional and national

 The nine years of common studies recommended in this study are 
                                                                                          t". )
                                                                                                t
 roughly equivalent to three of the developmental stages of 

 children that have been identified in educational research. 

 using Piagetian terminology, these are:                                                  , '\
                                                                                                    :

                                                                                                "

                                                                                          "    ~)


 ages 4-8:     pre-operational                   language development and the
                                                 symbolism that contributes to            ,. )
                                                                                               .;
                                                 that development.                              '.

                                                 no real sense of past or
                                                 future, limited experiences.
 ages 8-12:    concrete operations               beginning of ability to 

                                                 organize ways to do things 

                                                 beyond the immediate goal. 

                                                 some conception of 

                                                 conservation and reversibility. 

                                                 some ability to think of the 

                                                 future and of consequences. 

'ages 12-16:            formal operations        the ability starts to do
                                                 hypothetical reasoning.                  .     ,

These three stages in child development correspond to the lower 

primary, the upper primary and the lower secondary stages of 

the school system. The post-formal-operations stage of young 

adulthood can be considered senior secondary. 

The teachers' colleges of Jamaica might consider the use of 

these stages as areas of concentration in their programmes. 

Teachers' certificates might reflect the special skills that 

they have been trained for in teaching these age groups of 

children. For example, teachers could be trained in two of 

these four areas, in effect creating primary teachers, 

secondary teachers. and a group trained for the upper primary 

and lower secondary. The new group would be particularly 

useful in helping children with the transition from the 


7-20
..



  one-teacher model of the primary school to the more impersonal.
  multi-teacher model of the secondary school. Principals and
  supervisory officers. through in-service courses. could
  eventually be required to have train~ng in all four stages of
  child development.

  But changes such as those just mentioned must await the
  restructuring and revitalization of the system.

    Likewise. curriculum ~evision of the senior secondary grades
    will be needed soon after a common curriculum is adopted in
    Grades 7, 8 and 9:  the Basic Skills Programme underway since
    1984 in technical schools at the senior secondary level will
    reach its full potential only if the earlier years of schooling
    can be revitalized; relationships between the tech/voc in the
    school system and a much needed national training board will
  . have to be clarified; a new attempt is needed to cluster Sixth
    Form classes to improve- quality and efficiency; cooperation
    with the Caribbean Examination Council will be needed.

               *                *                 *
  The common belief among all the consultants who have
  contributed to this study is that a democracy ~equires an
  education system that provides opportunity for all people 
  genuine opportunity which is free of discrimination and free of
  arbitrary selection by those in authority.

  The system must become more fair.  This statement comes out of
  the analysis that the team has done of conditions in the
  schools, and underlies all the recommended changes.  Since the
  end of colonial rule in 1962, the valiant efforts that have
  been made to provide education for all have been piecemeal and
  have been subverted by a misguided search for excellence. The
  emphasis on quality edu~ation for the select few has led to
  elitism, and self-seeking by the elite few has led to the
  neglect of responsibility to others.  The understandable haste
  in a developing nation has created layers of privilege in the
  schools that are reflections of, and that are reflected in, the
  Jamaican society. The reforms suggested here are designed to
  break that cycle.

  Each student, at each· stage of the public school system, must
  be treated as a deserving individual. Each ~ust be supported
  with educational opportunities equivalent to those available to
  theories.   The theories and practices of education, for the
  nine years of the common school, must be consistently related
  to the classrooms where teachers need both support and
  direction as they help every student.

  When the system has been reformed. and when a new curriculum is
  in place. then the on-going pursuit of the schools can become a
  search for quality instruction for every child.

Appendix A
                                    I

THE ROLE OF THE EDUCATION OFFICER

The Education Officer of the Ministry of Education may be
thought of as the human embodiment of the curriculum guides of
the ministry. as well as the ombudsman for the student in the
school system. By this is meant that the chief priority of the
education officer is the improvement of instruction: helping
teachers to imple~ent the ideas in the curriculum guides while
improving the learning opportunities for the students.
It should be the duty of each education officer to carry out
all of her or his many tasks in the light of what is best for
the students in the schools. according to the polici~s of the
Ministry of Education.

                         *     *        *
The Minister of Education is the representative.of the elected
government who is chosen by the Prime Minister to see that the
laws and the government policies in the field of education are
implemented. The minister carries the ultimate responsibility
for education in the country: It is the Minister and the
Minister of State who bear the responsibility for recommending
changes in the law and regulations. who arbitrate among choices
that may be made under the law. and who are responsible for the
implementation of policies. A distinction must be made between
the Minister of Education. on the one hand. and the Ministry of
Education. of which he or she is the head. on the other.
The Ministry is the organization that is needed to carry out
the policies of the state. The civil servants who carryon the
business of providing services for students. parents and the
general public, must work within the Education Act. the Code of
Regulations and government policies within the law. Their
basic loyalty is to the law .
                ·
The Minister and the Minister of State for Education are
servants of the law also. although with special duties and
powers. It is their special au~hority under the law that they
share with education officers. It is this special authority,
and the responsibility for other people that accompanies that
authority, that requires a special place in the civil service
for education officers.

                                                                   '.   ,-    I

                                                                        "-':


                                                                        ,..~




                                                                        ~--
Each officer should be individually appointed by the Public
Service Commission appointed by the Governor General on the             ;1
advice of the Government.  Recommendat~ons to the Commission as              I
to whom should be appointed should be made by the Minister on           ~_l
the advice of the Permanent Secretary for Education, with
regard to the highest levels of competency but without regard           -1
                                                                            -j
to sex, creed, race or politics.                                        -- ;
A special category should be set up in the civil service                -,,
classification system to compensate these individuals                         I
                                                                              ,
adequately, comparable to the status and remuneration accorded          -    I


other professionals in the civil service such as lawyers- and           ... ,..
doctors.                                                                    ,!
                                                                        ,';,
If the Ministry of education curriculum guides are seen as the
vertebrae in the backbone of Ministry policies, then the
education officers are the nerve network of the education
system.

Models for such a system may be found in the corps of Her
Majesty's Inspectorate in Britain, particularly during the
period between World War I and World War II and to about 1965.
A similar group, the area school inspectors, were largely
instrumental in developing the quality of Canadian education,                 ,
                                                                        -   -'
particularly in Ontario 'and the western provinces, from about
1880 to 1950.  These role models have-been chosen because both          :   ')
groups, in their best manifestations, were not only inspectors
of schools, but were leaders in the in-service training of
teachers, and evaluators of the work of students and teachers.

                           '"     '"
The following criteria should be considered in the selection,
training and assignment'of duties of education officers who are
to be both supervisors and curriculum leaders:

Selection of Education Officers

     o   Demonstrated respect for young people and dedicated to
         their education; broad range of teaching experience
         both primary and sec~ndary preferred; out-of-country
         experience an asset; non-educational experience an
         asset.

     o   Excellent quali:ications:  master's deg~ee or higher
         including teacher training; substitution of specialized
         training in some cases.

     o   Personal characteristics:  integrity, "presence,"
         commitment to the development of each individual,
         demonstrated ability, knowledge of philosophies of
         education, loyalty to the nation.

         o      A special training pr·ogramme to be designed for
                incumbents and candidates (s~e Chapter 6) including:
                supervisory techniques: curriculum awareness, time
                management: information system usage.
         o      Internship programme: accompany a good role-model
                officer while he or she performs his or her duties for
                two to four weeks.
         o      In-service: short courses: exchanges with Teachers'
                College and university Faculty of Education staff:
                overseas exchanges and courses.
     Duties of Education officers
         lao Advocate for students:      individuals and groups.
          b.  Improvement of Instruction: in-service planning and
              leadership based on Ministry of Education guides, with
              teachers and principals.
          c. Classroom observation and reporting to School Boards
             and Regional Office: twice yearly per teacher.
          d.  School administration monitoring and reporting to
              School Boards and Regional Office.
         2.  Teacher certification and upgrading: recommending for
             certificates: recommending for salary raises to School
             Boards and Regional Office.
         3.  School Administration: assessing, recommending and
             "reporting to School Boards and Regional Office.
         4.  School Maintenance: assessing, recommending and
             reporting to School Boards and Regional Office.
         5.  School Board functioning: ex-officio member of school
             boards in assigned territory: reporting to Boards on
             school functioning: reporting to School Board and
             Regional Office of school board functioning.
        6.  Assisting with planning for school. school board and
            board needs: teachers, courses. books, supplies,
            equipment, accommodation.
        7.  Providing information: to teachers, principals, school
            boards, area boards, regional office, central office,
            public (public speaking) and cooperating with community
            agencies.

The  customary operating mode of an Education Officer should be
one of enabling other people to function better: discussion, 
             ,       !


questioning, and listening; the gathering of information, 

observation of practice, patient persuasion, and good humour 
with students. teachers, principals, board members, and the 
             '.       ~ 



public. Only as a last resort should the use of authority be
resorted to by an officer. 

The Education Officer at all times remembers that he GZ she is            :       ...
representing the Minister - acting on behalf of the parents               ~ :;j

whose children are in the care of the school.
The  guiding principle of the corps of Education Officers is:
                 "The Improvement of Instruction"

      A suggested "consistency control" document for developing a
      Curriculum Guide for AESTHETICS
      This document is intended for use by the chairperson and
      members of a curriculum committee appointed by the Ministry of
      Education to develop a curriculum guide in the area of
      "Aesthetics" in the junior high programme.
          1.  The Aesthetics committee should meet at the same time as
              the committees developing the other six guides in the areas
              of study recommended for students in lower secondary
              schools: Language Arts, Maths, Science, Technology
              Studies. Social Studies. and Personal & Family Studies.
             The committee should not miss the opportunity to develop
             modules of work that are integrated with the other subject
             fields and which can be taught cooperatively by teachers or
             worked on cooperatively by students in more than one
             subject field.
      2.  "Aesthetics" should continue the same emphases as the
          curriculum guides for Grades 1 to 6. Students should have
          regular opportunities to:
             o   Engage in activities such as art, music, dance and
                 drama.
             o   Express their own inventiveness and problem solving
                 abilities.
             o   Respond emotionally and intellectually to particular
                 objects.
             o   Be engaged in rituals and traditions associated with
                 the cultural forms and symbols of Jamaica.
      3.  Activities such as art. music. dance and drama should be
          part of the regular timetabled curriculum as well as
          after-school exhibitions and performances for parents and
          the community. In addition. the committee should plan
          integrated activities in modules of work with the other
          curriculum committees. particularly Social Studies.
          Language Arts. and Personal and Family Studies.

                                                                       "   .

         the teacher of Aesthetics, in common with all other 

         teachers of a student, sh~res responsibility for 

         teaching the proper u;e ot language. and responsibility 

         towards the environment.

         activities that allow for student-directed and

         resource people should be encouraged. 

     4)  Suggested year by year work for Grades 7, 8. and 9. 

         organized around themes that lend themselves to 

         integration among the sub-areas of Aesthetics as well

         curriculum.

        A suggested "consistency control" document for developing a
        Curriculum Guide in TECHNOLOGY STUDIES
        This document is intended for use by the chairperson and
        members of a curriculum committee appointed by the Ministry of
        Education to develop a curriculum guide in Technology Studies.
        1.  The Technology Studies commit~ee bhould meet at the same
            time as the committees developing the other six guides in
            the areas of Language Arts. Maths. Science. Social Studies,
            Aesthetics and Personal & Family Studies.
            The opportunities must not be missed to develop modules of
            work that are integrated with other subject fields and
            which can be taught cooperatively by teachers or worked on
            cooperatively by students in more than one subject field.
        2.  Technology Studies should continue the same emphasis as
            those in the curriculum guides in Grades 1 to 6 with regard
            to activities. cooperative work. exploration and analysis.
            The course in this subject would:
            o   Use a thematic approach.
            o   Use modules of learning selected from the present
                topics of Agriculture. the Crafts topics of Art and
                Crafts. Business Education. and Industrial Arts.
            o   Require only simple applications of technology using
                readily available materials from the environments of
                the students.
            o   introduce the concepts and skills of" planning,
                measuring. operating. assembling. recording. using
                instructions. evaluating results - systematically
            o   concentrate on the major technological inventions that
                support our civilization. the inventors and their
                methods of work .
        3.  The great technological inventions that support our
            civilization should form the basic core of the course over
            the three years of Grade 7. 8 and 9: the lever, the wheel.
            bridges, boats. engines, etc .· from basic technologies ~o
            the microprocessor. Inqluded also should be the concept of
            technology as systematic planning. including responsibility
            toward the environment.
           With the foregoing general principle in mind. topics should
           be selected from the sources listed above: Agriculture.
           Crafts. Business Education. and Industrial Arts, as well as
           appropriate ideas from any useful sources.

A suggested "consistency control" document for developing a
Curriculum Guideline for LANGUAGE ARTS

This document is intended for use by the chairperson and
members of a curriculum committee appointed by the Ministry of
Education to develop a curriculum guide in the area of Language
Arts.

1.  The Language Arts committee should meet at the same time as
    the committees developing the other six guides in the areas
    of study recommended for students in lower secondary
    schools: Math, Science, Technology Studies, Aesthetics,
    Personal & Family Studies, and Social Studies.

    The opportunities must not be missed to develop modules of
    study that are integrated with other subject fields and
    which be taught cooperatively by teachers or worked on
    cooperatively by students in more than one subject field.

2.  Language Arts should continue the same emphases as the
    curriculum guides for Grades 1 to 6.   Students should have
    regular opportunities to listen, to speak, to read and to
    write.   See the appendix to this document on Standard
    English and Creole.

   In Grade 8 or Grade 9, depending on the ability of the
   school to offer it, one foreign language, preferably
   Spanish, may be offered.

3.  No more than five (5) periods per week should be on the
    timetable for Language Arts, including a foreign language.
    However, the use of language in all other subject areas
    should be consciously planned and evaluated by teachers.
    The use of language in all subjects, both oral and written,
    by students and teachers should be a topic of assessment by
    principals and Territorial Education Officers when they
    observe in classrooms.




                                                            BB-9 

                                                                  .. 



4.  The curriculum guide that is developed for Language Arts
    should contain sections such as the following:
                                                                         i:i
    1)  Introduction: a brief rationale for Language Arts for
        students aged 12 to 15. including the role of Creole.
    2)  Timetable provisions: the five period limit and the 

        expectation that l~nguage will be taught across the 

        curriculum by teachers of all subjects. 

    3)  Listening. speaking. reading and writing topics and 

        suggested activities and exercises. 

    4)  Suggested year by year work for Grade 7. Grade 8 and 

        Grade 9. 

    S)  A few statements of advice to teachers about suitable            '''.,

        materials. sources and suitable ways to use the
        textbooks approved for these grades.
    6)  A section on appropriate evaluation practices with 
             '   .
        suggestions and examples. 

    7)  A section on the requirements and the pedagogy of 

        teaching a foreign language. 

S.  It will be necessary to include suggestions for teaching to
    three ability groups of students in the curriculum guides.
    The students who enter Grade 7 will be widely different in
    their ability to do the Grade 7 work. There should be
    practical suggestions about classroom management and about
    various kinds of suitable content and teaching strategies
    that suit students who can read. those who have trouble
    reading. and those who can read little if at all. Teachers
    of Language Arts should be prepared to work cooperatively
    with teachers of other subjects who also have
    responsibility for their students' aChievement in language.

BB-1e
Appendix:   "Talking Creole/writing English"
The fundamental problem created in the schools by the Standard
English versus Creole situation is th~t Creole is used. to
varying degrees. in much of the daily communication in"
classrooms, while the written work and the examinations which
are the dominant feature of the curriculum, are conducted
exclusively in Standard English.
The practice of "talk in Creole - write in English" produces
abysmally poor results when the ability to write Standard
English is used as the criterion for success. English must be
treated as a second language in the lower primary grades."
Testing in the new National Assessment Programme should take
into account the enlightened policy of the primary reading
teachers espoused by the National Association of Teachers of
Enilish in Jamaica: the reception years of Grades 1, 2 and 3
should be treated as a time of transition for students, where
their teachers consciously and carefully accept oral
communication in Creole, while at the same time, conducting
more and more classes in Standard English.
The diagnostic testing in September of Grade 4 that is
recommended elsewhere in this report as part of the National
Assessment Programme should be designed to ascertain a
student's aChievement in Standard English. but care should be
taken that the Creole speaking child is not made to feel
inferior.
The child should be told that he or she is just" moving towards
the superior ability to speak two languages. Creole and
English, while learning to write in the major common language
of the world which is English.
"Over the past 25 year English has emerged as the werld's
premier international language. It is the main language of
international business, diplomacy, the travel industry, banking
and finance, the arts, advertising and a variety of specialist
professions. Over 60% of the world's scientists are able to
read English; 70% of the world's mail is written in English and
80% of all the information in the world's electronic retrieval
systems is stored in ~nglish." from English: A World
Commodity" by McCallen, Brian; The Economist-, Sept. 1, 1989.

   A suggested "consistency control" document for developing a
   Curriculum Guide in SOCIAL STUDIES
   This document is intended for use by the chairperson and
   members of a curriculum committee appointed by the Ministry of
   Education to develop a curriculum guide in the area of Social
   Studies.
   1.  The Social Studies committee should meet at the same time
       as the committees developing the other six guides in the
       areas of study recommended for students in lower secondary
       schools: Language Arts. Maths. Science. Technology
       Studies. Aesthetics and Personal & Family Studies.
       The opportunities must not be missed to develope modules of
       work that are integrated with other subject fields and
       which can be taught cooperatively by teachers or worked on
       cooperatively by students in more than one subject field.
   2.  Social Studies should continue the same emphasis as the
       curriculum guides for Grades 1 to 6. Students should have
       regular opportunities to
       o   Acquire knowledge of their physical. social. political.
           economic and cultural environments.
       o   Organize and use information for problem solving and
           decision making based on inquiry and reasoning and
           taking relevant values into account.
       o   Work in groups in order to develop interpersonal and
           participation skills needed as members of a democratic
           society
       o   Examine critically prevailing societal attitudes and
           values. including their own.
  3.  The wholistic nature of knowledge and the pervasive
      applications of skil.ls should be emphasized in the design
      of the programme. communication skills from Language Arts
      are integral to a Social Studies programme; much
      mathematical data is need in Social Studies. especially in
      tables and graphs; inquiry skills are-shared with Science;
      technological developments and the nature of art forms are
      pervasive in Social Studies; a concentration on
      environmental studies is most appropriate.
       Integrated modules can be designed cooperatively with most
       of the other subject committees.




                                                             BB-15 

                                                                     .. 



     Religious Education should be a component of Social Studies 

     with an emphasis on personal values and responsibility 

     towards others. 
                  I



     There should be an emphasis on Jamaica in Grade 7, Jamaica
     in the Caribbean in Grade 8 and Jamaica in the World in 
              ~.


     Grade 9. with geography and history correlated. 

     particularly map work. statistics and e onomic studies. 

4.  The curriculum guide that is developed for Social Studies
    should contain sections such as the following:
     1)  Introduction: a brief rationale for Social Studies for
         students aged 12 to 15.                                            ·.. j


     2)  A statement that the Social Studies teacher is also a 

         teacher of Standard English and must meet the needs of 

         each student in her or his classes in this regard as a 

         priority, as well as responsibility toward the 

         environment. 

     3)  Timetable provisions:   five (5) periods per week
     4)  Suggested teaching strategies and learning activities 

         that develop information processing. decision making 

         and group participation. 

     5)  Suggested year by year work for Grade 7. Grade 8 and               . i

         Grade 9. arranged in modules with a theme for each
         module. and some examples of how geography, history and
         civics can be integrated. Religious education themes.
         especially values. can be integrated as the committee
         wishes.                                                            '.. j



     6)  A few statements of advice to teachers about suitable 

         materials. sources of information and suitable ways to 

         use textbooks approved for these grades.

         suggestions and examples based on some of the modules 

         in the guide.

  Appendix C
  THE ROLE OF THE INTEGRATOR IN DEVELOPING CURRICULUM GUIDES FOR
  GRADES 7-9

  The development of seven new curriculum guides for Grades 7. 8
  and 9 in Jamaica requires a limited but important degree of
  coordination among the committees named to write the guides.
  Each subject field has its own priorities. its own structure.
  its unique set of basic principles. These must be honoured and
  their presence evident in the content and structure of each
  curriculum guide.
  However. there are commonalities that also must be respected in
  the design of a curriculum guide. Some of these are listed
  below as responsibilities for a person designated by the ACEO
  of Curriculum as the integrator. A description of the duties
  of the integrator should be made available to each curriculum
  guide committee member in order that the general principles
  will be known to all and a cooperative mood can be established
  from the outset of the curriculum development activity.
  Common Factors in all Junior High Curriculum Guides
  1.  The nature of the interest and abilities of age group 12 to
      IS must guide developers. Many students still require
      concrete examples of ideas before they can grasp abstract
      ideas. They learn best by doing something. usually better
      than by reading. writing or talking.
       As much project activity as possible should be built into
       the guides - particularly in Maths. Science. Technology
       Studies and Aesthetics. Subsidiary committees may need to
       be set up to develop sets of Assignment Cards, or
       arrangements made for subject associations of teachers to
       act as clearing houses for trading Assignment Cards.
       (Perhaps the JTA would undertake such a professional
       activity?)
       A common theme that should be included tn all subjects and
       reflected in the curriculum guides should be mankind's
       responsibility towards the environment. The child's
       personal habits. the care of the school and its
       surroundings, from the community through to national,
       international and the concept of "Spaceship Earth" - the
       individual and collective responsibility for the
       environment should be stressed.


                                                             8C-l
                                                                   "





     The integrator should encourage each committee to include
     practical assistance and suggestions for teachers that are
     consistent with the need for young ~dolescents to                  (           .
     experiment and tryout ideas.                                       "_.J




2.  A limit of five (5) periods per week per subject should be          ·       I
    carefully observed in order to emphasize integration among
    the subjects - a particularly important need for young
    adolescents in Grades 7, 8 and 9.
                                                                        · i
     The  practicing teachers on each committee should be
     listened to carefully about the rate and amount of work
     that classes can cover in a week, a term and a year.
                                                                        ",t.J


     Each subject committee, especially in the traditional
     basics' of language and maths, will argue strenuously for
     addi tional. time on the timetab-Ie compared to those they
     deem to be "lesser" subjects. The counter arguments are:
     that additional classroom time has not produced better
     results; that language and maths should permeate all other
                                                                        ..      '\



     subjects if they are truly basic and should not be isolated
     in the curriculum; that ·the quality of awareness lacking
     among lower achieving students is probably a result of an
     overly narrow focus on maths and English to the exclusion
     of much of the remainder of the curriculum in the primary
     schools.
3.  A modular structure should be common to all curriculum
    guides. The content should be organized in units of work.
    preferably around themes of interest to young adolescent
    students.                                                           ·   .
     The possibilities for integration between Maths and Science
     in this approach are many. The structure of mathematics
     should not be the organizing principle of that guide in
     particular, since it is an abstract construct that most 12"
     to 15 year olds are not ready to comprehend. However, it
     may be advisable for the committee to build a structure of
     mathematics before it re-organizes the work into themes of
     interest to students, with activities.
                                 -
     As soon as activities are designed for maths, the
     integration possibilities with Science and Technology
     Studies become apparent.  (And the timetable limit bf 5
     pe=iods per week becomes less restrictive!)
4.  The modular units of work in each curriculum guide should
    have suggested times attached to them - the total number of
    periods probably needed to accomplish the objectives of the
    module - within the 5 periods per week limit.



8C-2 

.. 


  S.  There should be alternative activities for slower,average
      and faster students included in each module of work.  It is
      imperative that all students work at the same modules of
      work at the same time. although i~ is expected that there
      will be differentiation in the quantity and quality of work
      covered on each theme.

  6.  It should be understood by each curriculum guide committ.!e
      that each draft document produced by the committee will be
      edited by the Ministry of Education before it is published
      and becomes mandatory for use in the Junior High programme
      of all schoolS.

  7.  Curriculum guide committee members should be free to
       publish or make arrangements with publishers of educational
       learning materials at any time after appointment to the
     . committee. with the condition that any intent to publish or
       connection with a publisher(s) be made known to .the ACEO.
       Curriculum. within a week of any formal commitment being
       made by the committee member .

        Appendix D
        COORDINATION OF TEXTBOOK AND CURRICULUM POLICIES

        The need for students in Jamaican Jchools to have textbooKS is
        fundamental to their learning. There is a wide variety of
        other learning materials that are useful and important in
        teaching and learning. but the textbooK remains. for the
        foreseeable future. a major tool for learning for each student
        in Jamaica.
        A basic text is a 'book that deals with the content of a
        curriculum guide and is consistent with the paedagogy
        recommended in the guide.
        There have been two major initiatives of value and importance
        in recent years.
        In primary schools across the nation. all students each year
        receive readers written by Jamaican authors and printed in
        Jamaica.
        At the secondary school level. the textbook rental scheme has
        made it possible for students' in high schools. technical
        schools. comprehensive schools and secondary schools. to rent
        bOOKS purchased from British publishers through British
        government loan. But students in Grades 7. Band 9 of all-age
        schools were not included.
        There should be a small operational section'in the Publications
        Section of the Planning and Development Division of the
        Ministry of Education that is closely connected to the core and
        tech/voc curriculum units,and is devoted to the approval. and
        as necessary. the development of textbOoks for use in the
        primary and secondary schools.
        The mandate of the section should be to ensure the provision
        and availability of books consistent with the Ministry of
        Education curriculum guides. for all students in primary and
        secondary schools.
        A Textbook Approvals section would be responsible for
        producing. once each year. about January 1. the list of
        textbooKS approved by the Ministry for use in the schools the
        following school year.
        The "Approved Textbooks" list should include all primary and
        secondary school bOOKS that the Ministry has deemed suitable
        for use as basic texts.

                                                                   80-1 

                                                                   "




The Textbook Approvals Section would be responsible for the
Primary Textbook Project and f~r the Secondary Textbook Rental
Scheme.                                /

Both primary and secondary textbook distribution would be a
matter of concern to the corps of area education officers.   One                ;
of their on-going activities should be to see that every                    j



student has the books that he or she is sJpposed to have.   A
few minutes of random spot-checking during regular visits to
classrooms would be enough to ascertain. that the distribution
                                                                        ,.J
systems of both textbooks schemes have been effective.
                                                                        ..r.,
The Textbook Approval Section would be the contact between the                  )


Ministry and book publishers.   There is a need for discussion,
negotiation, contracting, etc., in order to ensure a supply of
suitable books for the schools.   From time to time direct
arrangements for the production of books may have to be
undertaken by the section, depending on the initiative of
publishers.

The section should have the authority to negotiate with
publishers about such matters as reasonable prices for books,
given that a Ministry list of approved texts constitutes free           ".\

                                                                        "    ,
advertising and a captive market for the publishers: about the          ~..- )
quality of books: about distribution schemes: about the needs
of the curriculum for specialized books; etc.

An approvals procedure should be developed and published in
order that all publishers and would-be publishers of textbooks
are aware of the procedures for submitting books to the
Ministry for possible use in the schools as basic texts. and
the evaluation criteria that will be used.

Such a procedure would include the delivery of perhaps six
                                                                        .-'
copies of a book to the Textbook Approvals Section.   Five
copies could be sent for evaluation by anonymous evaluators,
selected by curriculum officers.  The evaluators could include
practicing teachers, teachers, college staff members, and
university faculty members.  Guides· for evaluation could be
prepared for the evaluators with such headings as:   relevance
to the curriculum guide, accuracy, pedagogy, prejudice.

The curriculum officer would prepare a summary of the anonymous
evaluators' recommendations, then make a recommendation for or
against listing of the book in the "Approved Textbooks" list.
The recommendation should be sent to the head of.the curriculum
unit who should make a final approval or rejection of each book.

The "Approved TextbooKS" list could be used to implement
policies about Jamaican authors, local publishing, loc~l
printing, etc., as economic and political realities allowed.
                                                                        Ia. ···




8D-2 

,   .
        The highest priority of the Textbook Approvals Section in the
        immediate future, under the direction of the Curriculum Units.
        should be to encourage the production of suitable textbooks for
        backward reading students in all secondary_level schools. This
        will be largely a matter of making arrangements for publishing
        materials that are already available.
        There are so.e thirty manuscripts ready or under development in
        the HIRLAP (High Interest Readers Language Arts Programme)
        project of the Core Curriculum Unit for reading in Grades 7. B.
        and 9.
        There are dozens of suitable publications that have been
        developed over the years in the JAMAL organization. These can
        not only provide easy-reading materials but serve to increase
        students' awareness of their country and its culture.
        These existing materials should be reprinted. if necessary with
        minor editing. and made available as soon as possible to the
        backward reading students in all secondary schools -- high.
        comprehensive. technical. new secondary. vocational and
        all-age. First distribution should be to the all-age and new
        secondary schools.
        It is recommended that the preparation and pUblishing of other
        publications by the Ministry be transferred to a section or
        unit in the Educational Servic~s Division of the re-organized
        Ministry.

        Appendix E
        THE MISSION AND OPERATING MOD~ OF THE EDUCATIONAL OPERATIONS
        DIVISION OF THE MINISTRY OF EDUCATION;

        The Mission
        The Education Operations Division. by whatever name. is the
        center .of the headquarters organization of the Ministry of
        Education because its mission is central to the chief purpose
        of the schools - the provision of educational opportunity for
        every citizen of the country.
        In its operation. it concentrates on two major tasks: 1)
        coordinating. delivering. and supervising the nationwide
        programmes of the schools, including the supply of teachers,
        and 2) developing suitable programmes for all students:
        curriculum guides. in-service programmes, and other programmes
        and policies that are concerned with students' work, such as
        the provision of textbooks, evaluation, guidance, etc.
        The headquarters staff of the Ministry must be maintained at a
        minimum number of people in proportion to the staff in the
        Regional Offices who interface directly with the schools - the
        absolute priority of the Ministry is service to the schools.
        The Operating Mode
        The Division should be headed by a qualified. experienced
        educator with the title of Deputy Permanent Secretary. This
        should be the only division with a head of this rank.
        Ideally, the appointee should have been head of, or at least a
        member of: the Core Curriculum, the Tech/Voc Curriculum, and
        at lease one other unit in the Ministry before appointment to
        the post. Experience in teacher education, in the JTA, in
        academic work at the University, in technical work at CAST or
        at the Jamaica School of Agriculture would also be desirable
        evidence of ability and rounded experience.
        The primary characteristic of the individual should be an
        egalitarian philosophy of education and a broad outlook,
        perhaps reinforced by international experience.
        The Deputy Permanent Secretary should be the leader of a group
        of Chief Education Officers.

        Each Chief Education Officer should be responsible for an
        aspect of the educational programme of the nation:
        supervision, core curriculum, technical/vocational curriculum,
        textbooks, evaluation, special services (library. guidance).


                                                                    8E-l
                                                                  ,   I




The CEO's and the Deputy PS together form a Programme Council
whose function is to coordinate. the programme which is to be             /""''''.
delivered to the schools, through the supervision unit and the
Regional Offices.

        Appendix F
                                                /


        A FOUNDATION GRANT POLICY TO EQUALIZE OPPORTUNITY

        The present system of subvention~to the various types of
        schools in Jamaica has developed over the years in response to
        specific issues as they have arisen. Thus primary schools
        receive government support as a historical response to the need
        for literacy and numeracy among workers in the nineteenth
        century. The new secondary schools are funded by government as
        a result of decisions made mainly after independence in 1962.
        to provide more ac~ess for citizens to secondary education and
        better skills for the work force. The high schools are funded
        by government as a result of requests for assistance when the
        various churches and trusts found the financial burden
        onerous. Technical schools. as well as the
        technical-vocational part of the new secondary schools. are
        supported because of the role allocated to them in providing
        skills for the work force of the nation.
        These different circumstances have led to the creation of a
        diverse system of schools, supported by inequitable grants from
        the government.
        This study of secondary school reform has not been limited to
        the secondary level of the public system because of the
        interlinked nature of so many educational policies. As is the
        case with curriculum. it is not possible to deal with funding
        equity at the secondary level without considering primary
        education.
        Teachers at both primary and secondary levels are paid
        according to qualifications and experience rather than the ages
        of their students. This is as it should be. But when one
        looks at the grants for the different levels. and types of
        schools within levels. sepa~ately f~om teachers' sala~ies, then
        gross inequities are appa~ent. Even with the equalizing effect
        of teachers' salaries included. the differences in support are
        increasingly obvious f~om primary to secondary to tertiary, as
        well as among the various kinds of secondary schools.
        The contr ibution of government to the education of' an 8 yea.:::
        old student in a primary school for the year 1988 was about
        Ja$SS8., salaries included.




                                                                       8F-l
                                                                    .   ,




If a student was 13 years old in 1988; he or she had government
support:
     of Ja$ 558. if in an all-age school
     of Ja$ 1202. if in a new secondary school
     of Ja$ 1669. if in a high school
If a student was 18 years old in 1988. he or she had government
support:
                                                                             ~-   "
     of Ja$ 1628. at a community college                                    U"._


     of Ja$ 3357. at the College of Arts. Science and Technology
     of Ja$ 19,000. - 48.000 at the University of the West Indies
It is recognized that expenditure per pupil must usually be
greater at the secondary and tertiary levels than at the
primary. The inequity in Jamaica is in the amount of that
difference: should the value of an Arts degree at university
be 35 times the value of learning to read in the lower primary
school?
What follows is a suggested "formula" for government grants to
the publicly-supported schools. The proposal incorporates the
government's policy of encouraging local support of schools.
It is also a methodology for change to correct the wide
inequa~ities in support for individual students in the various
kinds of schools - both the amounts of money allocated to the
formula's categories and the categories can be changed each
year as money is available and conditions change. It is likely
that increases to high schools would be limited until
equalization was aChieved. requiring them to depend even more
than at present on support from their communities. That in
itself would be an equalization factor. given the preponderance
of middle class attendance at high schools.




                                                                            .'", 



8F-2 

." 


            A FOUNDATION GRANT FORMUtA WITH LOCAL SUPPORT OF SCHOOLS
                                               /

       1.   Basic per-pupil grant for a Grade 1-11 students:
            Number of students (Grades 1-11) x $ .... per student
   2.       Additional Programme Factors
                   class size: special allowances for appr~ved small
                   classes
                   teacher qualifications: degrees. certificates,
                   experience
                   special education students: certified handicapsi
                   equipment. etc.
                   technical students (Grades 10 & 11): equipment. work
                   experience. etc.
                   textbooks: if provided
                   supplies and equipment: by approved subject
   3.       Administration
   4.       Maintenance
   S.       Capital Costs
   6.       Interim Equalization Factors:
            The basic per-pupil grant. item 1 above. should be modified
            with interim Equalization Factors until parity among all
            schools has been achieved.   (The assumption is that an
            immediate move to an equal basic per-pupil grant is not
            possible.)
                e.g.: all-age factor = basic grant + $x 

                       new secondary factor = basic grant +$y 

            NOTE 1:  This formula can be employed within the present
                     method of vote and item budgeting in the
                     legislature. It supports the move to programme
                     budgeting .
               ·
            NOTE 2:  The basic grant per pupil can be the same for
                     primary and secondary pupils because the
                     additional programme factors take into account the
                     special needs of students in the various school
                     programmes, regardless of age: class size.
                     teacher qualifications. special equipment. etc.
                     Factors can be added as needed.




                                                                     SF-3 

                                                                  ,
                                                                  .
                                                                       .
                                                                       \
                                                                  '- __.i

The Basic Grant, taken together,with the Additional Programme
Factors, constitute what can be called ,THE FOUNDATION
PROGRAMME. II
The Foundation Programme is what the government can provide . .
Community Support of Schools·
Anything needed or wanted in a school beyond the Foundation
Programme, should be provided, as possible. by .the local
community. The local school board becomes the manager of the
resources that the Home & School or Parent-Teachers Association
is able to provide. the gifts that local service clubs or other
                                                                  :.
community organizations offer, the support that churches,         , «

The Education Tax
In future years, it will probably become desirable that the
recently instituted Jamaican Education Tax be returned to
school boards in the form of an equalization grant to provide
for schools that are in very poor communities or that do not
have sponsors.
Public Accountability
Every school board, annually, should be required to publish a
simple summary of its receipts and expenditures - government
grants and income from all sources as well as total
expenditures on salaries. equipment, maintenance, etc.
Publishing should be by newspaper, by notice posted in public
places. and by student delivery to homes. The annual account
should be certified by the chairman of the board and the
Territorial Education Officer.
Area School Boards.
I t would be desirable. at the time. that the Education Tax is
used for equalization purposes, to create area school boards as
the agency for coordination of equalization. There are too
many schools, nea=ly 900 in Jamaica. for administration to be
carried out directly from Kingston. Area school boards would
be responsible for several school boards and their schools in a
geographical area. The area board, consisting at least in part
of representatives from the school boards. and in part of
Regional Education Officers. with the relevant MP's as a formal
Advisory Committee. would make decisions about spending the
equalization grants provided by the Education Tax. A basic

BF-4
· ·




      equity factor would probably'have to be established also at
      that time. in order to deter~ine the relative wealth of the
      potential support for each school - land assessment or income
      tax revenues or some other objective criterion for establishing
      the potential of a community ability to support its school.
      Compensatory Policies and the Miristry of Education
      The principle of compensation should be introduced into the
      educational policies. both financial and educational. of the
      Ministry.
      Such policy should be viewed as a government response to. the
      need to equalize opportunity for those least able to help
      themselves.
      For example:
          1.  In relation to government subventions to the schools.
              the basic per pupil grant that is proposed elsewhere in
              this report should be higher for students in all-age
              schools in remote and mountainous schools and for those
              in low-income areas. than for students in high,
              technical and new secondary schools.
          2.  Teacher compensation packiges should be devised for
              housing and transportation in remote areas: to provide
              incentives for well-qualified people to spend at least
              a year or two. perhaps early in their careers. in
              remote and rural areas.
          3.  For supervision. younger education officers. with
              rugged four-wheel drive vehicles, should be assigned to
              remote and mountainous areas for their first
              assignments, in order to improve the quality of
              education in those areas and to make a name for'
              themselves to earn promoti~n or desirable transfers.

Appendix G
A NATIONAL ASSESSMENT                  I




Programme and Revision of the Common Entrance Exam
Part A -- An Educational View
The entire set of examinations currently in use and under
development in Jamaican schools should be examined from an
overall systems point of view.
There is a need for a cost analysis of the present multiplicity
of national assessment activities that have accrued over the
years in the schools. (see Part B of this appendix.)
Such an analysis should take into account not only the costs
allocated to the budget items dealing with the exams, but the
costs of lost 'time on task' in the other units of the Ministry
when officers are assigned to such duties as assessing exam
papers: evaluating teacher college. students, marks and exams:
marking exam papers, such as the CXC and other Jamaican exam's;
allocating students to schools using CEE,results; etc., etc.
There is an even greater need for a rationalization of all
testing into a patte~n suited to the purposes and design of the
curriculum that students meet as they progress through the
schools. The National Assessment Program is such a pattern.
But the question of the Common Entrance Examination must be
addressed first. THe CEE overshadows any discussion of the
examination system in Jamaican schools and must be dealt with
in order that any overall scheme for rationalizing the pattern
of examination can be presented.
Why Revise the CEE?
The exam of most ·significance in the schools. and in the
nation, is the Common Entrance Examination.
The CEE is a striking example of a once good idea that has been
overtaken by events to the point where it has b'ecome an
impediment to progress, affecting both primary and secondary
schools.
When it was devised, it was a useful   and necessary instrument
for the selection of students at the   end of primary school, in
order to choose those most likely to   benefit from the very
limited number of places in the high   schools.
The CEE is a selection examination only. It tests some English
and mathematics. and mental ability. It has a comm~ndable
correlation with success in high schools.
                                                             8G-l
                                                                    ,.......

                                                                    i-r
But this exam has also led to the identification of children as
early as Grade One for probable- success in writing it. and
those children tend to be given priority attention. It has
also been well demonstrated that teaching 'backward' from the
CEE. that is. drilling students on what is thought will be on
the test paper. does not improve the standards of the primary
schools.
The present test is not geared to the approved curriculum
guides for the primary grades - it does not test the knowledge
that the children are expected to have learned in the social
sciences. in science. or in aesthetics.
Too many of the children not considered of CEE potential are        ......,;i

functionally illiterate when they leave primary school.
Too many of the children do not have the social awareness that
comes from a balanced and all-round curiosity about their
world: the kind of awareness that is developed in part through
social studies. science and in particular. the arts.
                                                                    .     .'

There are other tests that. are somewhat related t6 the CEE in
use in Jamaican schools. The Grade Nine Achievement Test and
the Grade Eight Technical School Entrance Test have-been
devised to ameliorate somewhat the awful finality of the CEE on
the lives of 80\ of Jamaican children. These 'second chance'
exams provide very limited opportunities. on the order of 6\
and 8\. to find a place in a high school.
There are. however. other kinds of examinations   than those for
selection only. There are diagnostic exams and    achievement
exams. Either of these is better suited to the    early stages of
common. general education for all people than a   pure selection    , 1
exam like the CEE.
The CEE. therefore. should be revised. The revision should
take place in the year in which the new Grade 6 aChievement
test in the National Assessment Programme (see below) is ready
for use.
The Grade 6 N.A.P. test can be used immediately as the Common
Entrance Exam.
It can select students. as does the present CEE test. for the
different levels of secondary schools. But it will have added
value also in diagnosis and standards appraisal. of direct
benefit to the students and teachers. because the questions
asked in it are derived from the curriculum guides issued by
the Ministry for use in the primary schools.




8G-2 

·   ·



        The   Na~ionQl   Assessment Proqxamme
        The Ministry has been developing a systematized approach to
        student assessment nationally throug~ the National Assessment
        Programme.

        The National Assessment Programme should ultimately deal with
        the period of common studies. a period that should include
        Grades One to Nine after a common curriculum is finally
        implemented in Jamaican schools.  Such a goal may be reached.
        if there is sustained effort by all concerned. in about 10
        years.

        It should be a basic policy that diagnostic testing is the most
        valuable for students and teachers. and that achievement
        standards can be abstracted from diagnostic tests with much
        less intrusion into the teaching/learning,cycle in the
        classroom than other kinds of test and exams.

        Care should be taken to ensure that any new system does not
        have so many tests that 'teaching to the test' becomes the
        common classroom strategy.

        Care should also be taken to ensure that the costs of the new
        system do no~ become disproportionate to the benefits.

        For example. the National Assessment Programme might consist of
        the following:

        Grade One

        The readiness test which has been developed for Grade One and
        is nearly ready for adoption.

        Grade Three/Four

        The proposed Grade Three Achievement Test now under development
        by the Ministry should be renamed the Grade Four Diagnostic
        Test and. if necessary. be redesigned as a diagnostic test.

        A diagnostic test given at the beginning of a school year.
        administered and marked by the teacher who will be teaching the
        child. can help student. teacher and parents to understand the
        child's academic needs better.

        The test should be used in September in Grade Four.
        Achievement tests given at the end of a school year to young
        children have little effect as a motivator of the children.
        End of year tests are of little use to the teaching process.

                                                                  .. 


either. since they are after the fact of the year's work.
Their value is assumed to be tQ the parents. who then adopt
unwarranted attitudes to their child oIl'the basis of superior
or inferior results on one test.
The Grade Four Diagnostic Test should be designed to give
                                                                              ,~
teachers some idea of how well a child has progressed in                      j


mastery of the topics in the Ministry of Education curriculum
guides for Grades One to Three. There should be at least one
question requiring a short prose answer since written language
is such a high priority in the curriculum guides.

sUbmitted by teachers. reviewed by area and curriculum

people. Items collected each year should be saved until an

curriculum - a useful function for computers.
The results of the test should be reviewed by the principal and
the area education officer. Regional and national results can            ,,,:

possible in-service work with teachers "and at the national

The two important factors in the selection of test items are"
1) suitability for the age group, and 2) relevance to the MOE
curriculum guides for Grades One to Three.
Grade Six CEE
The Grade six Achievement Test currently under development
should be a completion test at the end of six years of school,
because it is also needed as a selection test to fulfill the
function of a common entrance exam to secondary school.
It should be administered about the middle of April each year
to all Grade Six students in the nation. There must be at
least one question requiring a short essay type answer to
assess the student's ability in written expression as compared
with the requirements of the Ministry of Education curriculum
guides.
The test results should supplement the teacher's assessment of
a student'S aChievement and would become an important part of
~he transfer information that is sent to the school where the
student will begin Grade Seven.

involves teachers, principals. education officers and concerned
parents in a testing plan that helps students. does not intrude
too much into the teaching/learning processes in the classroom,
is reasonable in cost, and yet gives assurance to the public
that standards are being measured.

             in the Jamaican school system. Other educational institutions.
             alonq with business and industry, should be assisted to chanqe

             simplified standards of any new system adopted by the schools.
