                     A NATIONAL ASSESSMENT PROGRAMME AND REVISION OF THE COMMON
                        ·               ENTRANCE EXAMINATION
                                                      /

                Part B - An Economics View

                The institution of a common curriculum for Grades 7-9 in
                Jamaican secondary schools implies that selection should be
                kept to a minimum prior to Grade 9. No curriculum is truly
                common if 15 percent or more of the students in the relevant
                cohort can be expected to drop out of school before completing
                it, and investments in curriculum development and
                implementation cannot yield their full benefits if the new
                materials are not provided to all children.  A common
                curriculum for Grades 7-9 will thus require changes in the
                Common Entrance Examination (CEE), both in terms of the content
                of the exam and in terms of its timing.

                1.   The Present System

                The CEE is the single main determinant of success and failure
                in the Jamaican educational system, and the competition to
                "pass" the exam is consequently intense.  The pervasive ·effects
                of this competition are felt throughout the educational system.

                Students take the CEE in the sixth grade (sometimes in the
                fifth), and those who "pass"are rewarded with places in
                secondary high schools. Pass rates are low (+/-20%), and are
                determined by the availability of high school places, rather
                than by absolute standards of performance.  High schools are
                commonly the best and the most prestigious secondary schools in
                Jamaica. and enrollment in them provides privileged access to
                further educational and occupational opportunities.  Those who
                "fail" the exam are relegated to new secondary schools and
                all-age schools. which are perceived to be inferior to high
                schools on virtually all measures of educational quality. Many
                of the students who fail the CEE drop out of school entirely in
                or soon after Grade 7. when it becomes apparent that further
                educational oppo~tunities are closed to them.

                The effects of the CEE are felt not only in secondary schools
                and after. but in primary schools as well.  In primary schools
                the formal curriculum is largely neglected in the later grades
                in favor of courses aimed at preparation for the exam.   Elite
                "prep" schools devote their efforts to ensuring their students'
                success on the CEE, and other primary schools are obliged to
                emulate them.  Schools offer specialized "common entrance"
                classes to prepare their students for the exam. and students
                also attend extracurricular CEE courses after school and during
                school holidays. for which tuition fees are charged.   The
                quantity of private and public resources devoted to the CEE



                                                                            BG-9 

                                                                  .. 



exam (and so diverted from other educational uses) is
impossible to measure with any precision. but some rouqh
estimates are presented in the following section of this paper.
The bad effects of the CEE on basic education in Jamaica are
amplified by the fact that it is an aptitude rather than an
achievemp.nt ~xam, desiqned to predict success in hiqh school
rather t~an to assess mastery of the primary school
curriculum. Time qiven to preparation for the CEE is ~herefore
mostly if not entirely time taken from reqular instruction. As
a result, children who "fail" the exam are doubly deprived. On
the one hand. they are debarred from the pursuit of valued
educational and occupational opportunities. On the other hand,
they are deprived of much of the education they miqht otherwise          <_I

receive in primary school. Reportedly hiqh rates of functional
illiteracy amonq primary school leavers and the resources qiven
to remedial instructional proqrams in Grades 7-9 in nearly all
secondary and all-aqe schools indicate the maqnitude of these
effects, and their costs.
2.   The Costs of the Common Entrance Examination
The costs of the CEE cannot be specified precisely, but it is
possible to identify the main components of those costs. and to
provide some rouqh estimates of their maqnitude. Estimates of
the cost of the CEE are presented in Table 1.
The administrative costs of the CEE include the costs of
producinq the exam, distributinq it to schools and collectinq
it aqain. inviqilatinq the exam in the schools. and markinq the
examination papers. Estimates of these costs are provided in
the Ministry of Education budqet, and are shown in Row 1 of
Table 1.
To the cost of administerinq the exam must be added the cost of
closinq schools on the day that the examination is qiven. which
is equivalent to sliqhtly more than one-half of one percent of
annual recurrent costs in those schools. On the conservative
assumption that expendi tures· in the 400 schools where the CEE
                                                                             .,
is written are no larqer than in those where it is not. these            :


costs can be estimated at J$473,600, as shown in Row 2 of Table
1.

The provision of specialized Common Entrance classes or the
diversion of reqular classroom time to CEE preparation
represent further costs of the exam. It is hard to estimate
how much teacher time is used in this way. In many schools,
for example. CEE preparation classes are offered after school
or durinq holidays, and students pay tuition. The cost of
these classes is primarily borne by students, as is discussed

below. In regular English and Maths classes. however, it is
possible if not certain that mu.ch of the available time in
Grades 5 and 6 (and even Grade 4) is devoted to exam
preparation. and these subjects are the core elements of the
curriculum at this level. Insofar as this is true, a large
share of total annual recurrent expenditure in these grades
should in fact be assigned as a cost of the CEE. Alternative
estimates of this cost based on contrasting sets of assumptions
are shown in Row 3 of Table 1.
As was noted above, at least some part of the need for remedial
programs in Grades 7-9 in high, secondary. and all-age schools
can be attributed to the diversion of instructional time to
exam preparation in earlier grades, and some part of the cost
of these programs must therefore also be assigned to the CEE.
The direct costs of remedial programs include the salaries of
teachers in remedial classes and the cost of materials for.
their students. An additional cost is the further loss of
instructional time for students in Grades 7-9, where class time
for remediation is diverted from the standard curriculum.
(This cost will become even more salient with the present
effort to adopt and implement a common curriculum for these
grades.) Alternative estimates of these costs are presented in
Row 4 of Table 1.
Extracurricular courses devoted to preparation for the CEE are
offered after school and during holidays in many schools. The
cost of these courses is borne mainly by students. who pay
tuition directly to the schools. or to the teachers. and who
also purchase their own books. In addition to the financial
burdens imposed by tuition and the purchase of books, there is
also an opportunity cost for these courses. as students who
attend are prevented from earning income during the period of
the courses. The value of their time is no doubt small. but
for children of 10 or 11 it is nevertheless certainly a f&etor.
There is some public cost for CEE preparation courses as well,
in that the courses are commonly held on school'premises. and     .
                                                                  ,~   .1
                                                                       -'

make use of school facilities (e.g. desks and blackboards).
Insofar as these courses also make use of school supplies. e.g.
paper. chalk, their public cost is significantly increased.
Estimates of the public and private costs of CEE preparation
courses are provided in Row 5 of Table 1.
High and low estimates of the total cost of the CEE are
p:esented in Row 6 of Table 1. The frailty of the estimates
and the arbitrary nature of many of the assumptions on which
they are based must be acknowledged, but it should also be
noted that both estimates are likely to err on the low rather
than the high side.



8G-12 

While large, these figures still fail to take into account the
full cost to the nation of the CEE. As was noted above, the
diversion of instructional resources from the regular
curriculum to exam preparation at least contributes to the high
rate of functional illiteracy among primary school leavers,
while the administration of the exam in Grade 6 and the
consequent closing of opportunities for those who "fail"
contributes to high rates of drop-out in Graies ~, 8, and 9.
The prodigal waste of human resources to which the CEE
contributes is a cost that Jamaica can ill afford to bear. The
chance to reduce or eliminate this cost is among the strongest
arguments in favoL of a comprehensive reform of Jamaican
secondary schools. which would include replacement of the
present CEE by an achievement test (i.e. the NAP) and the
postponement of selection for specialized secondary education
to the end of Grade 9.
3.   Policy Alternatives
It is a widely and long acknowledged fact in Jamaica that the
present stranglehold of the CEE on the country's educational
system must be broken if the quality and coherence of primary
and secondary schooling are to be restored. One salutary
change is already underway. The Grade 6 achievement test
developed as a component of the National Assessment Program is
being studied as a potential replacement for the present CEE
test. The shift from an aptitude to an achievement basis for
the CEE exam should in itself go some way toward improving the
quality of Jamaica's primary schools, and toward enlarging the
competencies of primary school students as well.
A second reform in the CEE has been proposed (most recently by
the Minister of Education), which would shift the CEE from
Grade 6 to Grade 9. Postponing selection for differentiated
secondary education for three years would restore the integrity
of the primary school curriculum in Jamaica, and would
encourage the provision of basic educational opportunities for
all Jamaican students. It might also be expected to reduce the~
rate of drop-out in Grades 7-9, as many students now remain in
school only as long as they retain the possibility of being
selectee for high school admission. By postponing the CEE,
students could be kept in school for longer periods, and they
would very likely learn more while they were there.
There are serious obstacles to the implementation of such a
reform. however. As long ai the Jamaican system of secondary
schools remains highly stratified in terms of educational
quality, the problem of allocating Grade 7 students among
schools will persist. If the problem is not to be solved by
competitive examinations it will have to be solved in some
other way, and alternative policies are likely to encounter


                                                          8G-13
significant opposition. Moreover. postponing the CEE ,implies

that students will be sorted into differentiated secondary 

schools after Grade 9 rather than Grade/7. Insofar as this is     .   ,    ;


so there can be no presumption that students will remain in the
same schools for Grades 7-9 and Grades lO-ll. but students who
are required to transfer from elite to less prestigious schools   ,
                                                                           ,
                                                                           ,
after Grade 9 can nevertheless be expected to pro~est.
The present selection system has the advantage of relying on a
mechanism. the CEE. that is viewed as "meritocratic" and hence    · J
legitimate by most Jamaicans. Any alternative 'policy must be
widely viewed as equally legitimate in order to be successful.    ,        ,
                                                                           ,
The inequities inherent in the present stratified secondary       ,
                                                                      ,
                                                                           ,
                                                                  ,_ J
school system and the problem posed by the need for a
legitimate basis for sorting students into highly unequal
opportunities should be borne in mind in evaluating the policy
alternatives presented elsewhere in this report.
4.   The Costs of Reform
The financial savings in the Ministry of Education's budget
that will be realized by reforming the,CEE are likely to be
small at best. The administrative cost of the exam will not be    .. 1
                                                                           ,
reduced by a change in its content or timing. and students may    ., J
                                                                           )




well continue to purchase private instruction in preparation
for the exam. To the extent that the NAP is curriculum rather
than aptitude based. however. the demand for such instruction
may be reduced. as students will be able to prepare for the
exam in. rather than after. class. Replacing the CEE with the
NAP and postponing selection to the end of Grade 9 will in fact
probably increase the financial cost of primary and junior high
education in Jamaica. because it may cause students who would
now drop out to remain in school through the completion of
Grade 9. Some estimates of the costs and savings that might
follow reform of the CEE are presented in Table 2.
In economic terms. the savings that will follow from the reform
of the CEE are sUbstantial. The main components of the present    ., l


cost of the CEE are the diversion of time from the assigned
curriculum to exam~reparation and the use of instructional
time for remediation in Grades 7-9. as Table 1 shows. The
shift from an aptitude to an achievement basis for the exam
will reduce both of these costs to zero. With respect to the
first. the use of the NAP as a selection device will reward a
focus on the assigned curriculum rather than its neglect. thus
eliminating any incentive for the diversion of curricular
time. With respect to the second. increased instruction within
the ass~gned curriculum in Grades Sand 6 will reduce the need
for remediation in Grades 7-9. While some students will
continue to require remedial instruction. the cost can be fully           -,
attributed to differences among students in aptitude and
motivation. and not to the CEE.

The principal cost of examination reform is the cost of
                                                                  .   .

expanding enrollments in Grade& 7-9. Postponing selection to
the end of Grade 9 will remove one of the main causes for
persistently high drop out rates in these grades. and so should
cause students to remain in school longer. In Table 2 it is
assumed that the percentage of students completing nine grades
will rise from 75 to 90 percent. The co~t of this expansion .is
not small. but additional expenditur~ on instruction at this
level should significantly increase the internal and external
efficiency of the educational system. raise the average
achievement level of Jamaican students. and provide better
qualified candidates for the local labor market. These
benefits can only be attained at the cost of providing more
school places for students and employing more teachers in
junior high schools. but evidence from around the world
suggests that the magnitude of the benefits to be gained more
than justifies the cost.




8G-16 

Appendix H
CONSTRAINTS ON THE MINISTRY   Or   EDUCATION's MISSION

The Ministry of Education is the organized expression of a
nation's will to provide opportunities for learning for its
people.
This Secondary Reform Project study in Jamaica has focused
particularly on the lower secondary years of the publicly
supported education system. The study team, however, has
examined briefly the primary schools, the upper secondary and
the tertiary stages as well. Various sections of the report
make comment on all stages.
The position taken in this study is that curriculum and school
administration must be integrated if they are to be effective
in providing educational opportunity for all. Therefore, this
study was carried on during its first three months in
conjunction with the Study of School Administration.
There is a major and basic area of organization that affects
the nature of educational opportunity and the ability of the
Ministry of Education to provide it. The issue was not
addressed directly by the School Administration Study, nor is
it the direct focus of this study. Nevertheless, mention
should be made of th~ problem - the relationship of the
Ministry of Education to other departments of the government.

             *                     *
The Ministry of Education is one among many ministries of the
government. The Minister and the Minister of State represent
the interests of education in the competition for resources
that occurs around the Cabinet table. This is as it should be
in a parliamentary democracy.
Below the Minister, the Permanent Secretary and the other civil
servants in the ministry carry out their duties within the
policies of the government, according to approved procedures.
This study is focused particularly on curriculum, some of the
factors that enable or inhibit the provision of good
educational opportunity for all citizens, and some alternative
designs for the studies that students should undertake in the
schools. But it has become apparent also that there are
factors outside the Ministry that are hindering, and indeed,
preventing the provision of quality service to students and
teachers in the schools.




                                                          8H-l
There has been no in-depth study. The following comments are
based on limited observation of the practices in the Ministry
of Education in Jamaica. compar~d with ~xperience in another
civil service in canada. which also has a colonial heritage.
but a longer history of independence.
                                                                   .   "   .~


It appears that the financial controls exercised by the
Minis'ry of Finance are too complex and too minutely pervasive
to allow Ministry of Education officers to carry out their
responsibilities effectively.
Instead of a process of budget approval by the Legislature for
education. and then the respon~ibility for control of
expenditure of the line items in the budget being within the
Ministry of Education. the line item control of expenditure is
in the Ministry of Finance. The effect is that long range
plans. such as those for in-service summer courses which are
part of the programme descriptions approved in the Education
budget. are not possible because of the uncertainty about money
allocations. Ministry officers are put in the position of
calling meetings. scheduling summer courses. establishing
committees. etc .· not on the basis of long-range plans approved
in the annual budget setting process. but on the short-term
availability of funds allocated item by item from t~e Ministry
of Finance.
Ministry officials are often seriously embarrassed by the short
notices given to teachers and others for courses and meetings.
Delays in responding to requisitions for supplies and equipment
are often blamed. fairly or not. on "no money available from
Finance for this matter." There is considerable inconvenience
and embarrassment for teachers who must change personal plans
on short notice.
The overall result is a general air of confusion and
inefficiency about administrative matters that affects
officers. principals and teachers, and is translated into
arbitrary dealings with students.
There may well be similar effects in the administrative
operations of the Ministry in addition to the supervisory
functions that have been noted by this team.
A general solution to the problem should be sought in the
following management principle.
